Equating Direction-Affirmers with the Dahriyya
Al-Nasafi argues that those who affirm direction for God are like the Dahriyya in denying the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how affirming direction for God contradicts His transcendence and logically aligns with atheistic denial of the Creator.

Core Idea
Affirming direction for God actually denies His eternity.

Al-Nasafi presents a striking argument: those who claim God is in a direction or place are not honoring Him but are unknowingly joining the ranks of the Dahriyya, who deny the Creator entirely. This passage sharpens our understanding of divine transcendence by exposing the logical flaw in anthropomorphic conceptions of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the strong language: 'فضاهوا الدهرية' (they imitated the Dahriyya).
2
Identify key terms
Locate the terms: 'أمارات الحدوث' (marks of contingency), 'الجهة' (direction), 'التمكن' (spatial location). Understand how they function as signs of createdness.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow Al-Nasafi's reasoning: (1) Direction and location are marks of contingency. (2) Those who affirm them for God attribute contingency to the eternal. (3) This effectively denies the true Eternal Creator, just like the Dahriyya.
The conclusion is that the direction-affirmers are the real deniers.
4
Reflect on the counterintuitive claim
Consider why this argument is so powerful: what seems like a respectful belief (God is 'above') is actually a negation of His transcendence.
This connects to the broader Islamic teaching of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم قائلين بعدم القديم. فضاهوا الدهرية في نفي الصانع، الذي ليس فيه شيء من أمارات الحدوث وساعدوهم بإثبات قدم من هو متمكن في المكان أو متحيز إلى جهة في إثبات قدم من تحققت أمارات حدوثه، وبإثبات القدم للعالم نفي الصانع، فإذا عند الوقوف على هذه الحقائق علم أنهم هم النافون للصانع في الحقيقة دون من أثبته ونفي عنه الجهة والتمكن اللذين هما من أمارات الحدوث. والله الموفق.
Those who affirm non-existence by denying the eternal. They imitate the Dahriyya in denying the Creator, in whom there is no mark of contingency, and they aid them by affirming the eternity of one who is located in a place or confined to a direction, thus affirming the eternity of one whose marks of contingency are realized, and by affirming the eternity of the world they deny the Creator. When these facts are considered, it becomes known that they are the actual deniers of the Creator, not those who affirm Him and negate from Him direction and spatial location, which are marks of contingency. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that God is in a direction or place. The author equates them with the Dahriyya (materialist atheists) because by attributing to the eternal a mark of contingency (direction/place), they effectively deny His eternity. The argument proceeds as follows: The true Creator has no marks of contingency (amarat al-huduth). Direction and spatial location are among those marks. Therefore, anyone who affirms them for God is actually negating His deity, because they affirm eternity for something that exhibits contingency. The conclusion is that those who negate direction from God are the true affirmers of the Creator, while those who affirm direction are true deniers.

Key Terms
العدم
الدهرية
أمارات الحدوث
الجهة
التمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming direction for God (e.g., 'He is above') is a respectful belief that honors His majesty.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that direction is a mark of contingency, so attributing it to God actually denies His transcendence and aligns with atheistic denial of the Creator.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, how do those who affirm direction for God resemble the Dahriyya?
A) They both believe in the eternity of the world.
B) They both deny the Creator by attributing marks of contingency to the eternal.
C) They both reject the use of rational proofs.
D) They both affirm the existence of a single Creator.

Key Takeaway
Affirming direction or location for God is not divine honor but actually undermines His transcendence and aligns with denial of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refuting the Argument from Innate Disposition
Al-Nasafi refutes the argument that innate disposition proves God is in a direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's refutation of the argument that innate human disposition proves God is in a direction, and how he distinguishes between the common person and the erudite scholar.

Core Idea
Innate disposition is not a proof; only the ignorant rely on it, scholars follow evidence.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi addresses a common argument that people naturally believe God is above. He does not dismiss it outright but carefully distinguishes between those who rely on imagination and those who rely on evidence. This lesson unpacks his response and shows why the argument fails for the knowledgeable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author structures his response by distinguishing two groups of people.
2
Identify the two groups
Find where Al-Nasafi introduces the two categories: the common person who does not discipline his mind, and the erudite scholars who know the difference between possible and impossible.
These are the key to the refutation.
3
Understand why the common person's belief is conceded
Al-Nasafi concedes that the common person may believe God is in a direction because of ignorance: he does not know that being confined to a direction is a mark of contingency. He sees celestial bodies and imagines God must be there.
This belief is based on imagination, not evidence.
4
Understand why the scholars' belief is not conceded
For the erudite, the argument is not conceded because they rely on evidence, not imagination. Evidence has established that God cannot be in a direction.
The scholars know the difference between possible and impossible.
5
Reflect on the role of evidence
Ask yourself: What makes the difference between the two groups? The answer is the use of reason and evidence (dalīl). Al-Nasafi shows that innate disposition alone is not a reliable source of theological knowledge.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو الجواب عن قولهم : إن الناس مجبولون على العلم أنه تعالى في جهة العلو، حتى إنهم لو تركوا وما هم عليه مجبولون لاعتقدوا أن صانعهم في جهة العلو. فإنا نقول لهم: إن عنيتم بهذا من لم يرض عقله بالتدبير والتفكير ولم يتمهر في معرفة الحقائق بإدمان النظر والتأمل فمسلم أنه بهواه يعتقد أن صانعه بجهة منه، لما أنه لا يعرف أن التجيز بجهة من أمارات الحدوث وهي منفية عن القديم ولما يرى أن ما ليس بقائم به يكون بجهة، ثم يرى صفاء الأجرام العلوية وشرف الأجسام المنيرة في الحسن، فظن جهلا منه أنه تعالى لابد من كونه بتلك الجهة منه، لخروج ما ليس بقائم به ولا بجهة منه عن الوهم وفضيلة تلك الجهة على سائر الجهات عنده، وإن عنيتم به الحذاق من العلماء العارفين بالفرق بين الجائز والممتنع والممكن والمحال فغير مسلم، إذ هؤلاء يثبتون الأمر على الدليل دون الوهم وقد قام الدليل عندهم على استحالة كونه تعالى في جهة. والله الموفق.
This is the answer to their saying: 'People are naturally disposed to know that God is in the direction of above, so much so that if they were left to their natural disposition they would believe that their Creator is in the direction of above.' We say to them: If you mean by this one who does not discipline his mind by reflection and thought and has not become proficient in knowing realities by persistent investigation and meditation, then it is conceded that he believes out of desire that his Creator is in some direction, because he does not know that being confined to a direction is among the marks of contingency which are negated from the Eternal, and he sees that whatever is not self-subsistent is in a direction, and he sees the purity of the celestial bodies and the nobility of luminous shining objects, so he ignorantly imagines that God must be in that direction because that which is not self-subsistent and not in a direction is beyond his imagination, and because that direction is superior to others in his view. But if you mean by this the erudite scholars who know the difference between the possible and the impossible and the contingent and the absurd, then it is not conceded, for these people affirm things on the basis of evidence, not imagination, and evidence has established for them that it is impossible for God to be in a direction. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that human innate disposition (jiblah) proves God is in the direction of above. The author structures his response by distinguishing two categories of people. First, he addresses the common person who has not disciplined his mind through reflection and study. Such a person may indeed believe God is in a direction out of ignorance: he does not know that being confined to a direction is a mark of contingency (amarat al-huduth) which must be negated from the eternal (al-qadim). He sees celestial bodies and thinks the 'above' is superior, and since whatever is not self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsihī) appears to him to be in a direction, he imagines God must also be there. The author concedes that this type of person may hold such a belief. Second, he addresses the erudite scholars (al-hudhdhaq min al-ulama') who know the difference between the possible (jā'iz), impossible (mumtani'), contingent (mumkin), and absurd (muḥāl). For them, the argument from disposition is not conceded, because they rely on evidence (dalīl) not imagination, and evidence has established the impossibility (istiḥāla) of God being in a direction. Thus the disposition argument only applies to the ignorant, not to the knowledgeable.

Key Terms
مجبولون
الجهة
التجيز
أمارات الحدوث
الحذاق من العلماء
الجائز والممتنع والممكن والمحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Innate disposition is a reliable source of theological knowledge and proves God is in a direction.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that innate disposition only leads to belief in a direction for those who do not use reason. Erudite scholars rely on evidence which proves God is beyond direction.

Checkpoint
How does Al-Nasafi respond to the claim that people are naturally disposed to believe God is in the direction of above?
A) He accepts it as a valid proof for all people.
B) He rejects it entirely by saying it is based on imagination.
C) He concedes it for the uncritical but rejects it for the erudite who rely on evidence.
D) He argues that natural disposition is the foundation of all knowledge of God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that the innate disposition argument only applies to the ignorant; the scholars rely on evidence which proves God is beyond direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author says scholars affirm things based on evidence, not imagination. In your own life, is there any belief about Allah that you hold simply because it feels natural or 'common sense'? Will you examine that belief in light of what revelation and reason actually teach?

Refuting the Argument from Raising Hands
Al-Nasafi refutes the argument from raising hands and gives analogies from prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ritual gestures like raising hands in supplication do not imply that Allah is located in a physical direction.

Core Idea
Ritual directions are not evidence for God's location.

Al-Nasafi addresses a common argument: the practice of raising hands towards the sky during supplication is taken as proof that Allah is in that direction. He refutes this by examining other ritual acts in Islam—facing the Kaaba, looking at the place of prostration, and varying prayer directions—none of which imply God's physical presence in those places. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows how Islamic practice actually reinforces divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument being refuted
The opponents claim that the consensus (ijmā') of raising hands towards the sky during supplication proves that God is in that direction. Al-Nasafi dismisses this as invalid because the action itself does not provide evidence for God's location.
This is a classic argument from practice, which Al-Nasafi rejects as inconclusive.
2
Examine the analogies from prayer
Al-Nasafi gives three analogies: facing the Kaaba, looking at the place of prostration, and praying towards various directions (east, Yemen, Syria). In each case, the ritual direction does not imply God is there.
These analogies show that ritual directions are for human benefit, not to indicate God's location.
3
Grasp the deeper purpose of directions
The author suggests that different directions in different states (prayer, supplication) are prescribed to avert the illusion (wahm) that God is confined to a specific direction. This reinforces divine transcendence.
The Throne is also mentioned as the qibla of hearts, analogous to the Kaaba for bodies.
4
Clarify the terms 'inzal/tanzil'
The words 'sending down' refer to Gabriel (Jibril) who brought the Quran, not to any movement of Allah or the Quran itself. Gabriel descended from above because his station was there, but Allah is beyond direction.
This addresses a potential confusion from scriptural language about descent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلقهم بالإجماع برفع الأيدي إلى السماء عند المناجاة والدعاء باطل، لما ليس في ذلك دليل كونه تعالى في تلك الجهة. هذا كما أنهم أمروا بالتوجه في الصلاة إلى الكعبة وليس هو في الكعبة، وأمروا برمي أبصارهم إلى مواضع سجودهم حالة القيام في الصلاة بعد نزول قوله تعالى: ﴿ قَدْ أَفْلَحَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ فِي صَلَاتِهِمْ خَشِعُونَ ﴾ وبعدما كانوا يصلون شاخصة أبصارهم نحو السماء وليس هو في الأرض، وكذا حالة السجود أمروا بوضع الوجوه على الأرض، وليس هو تعالى تحت الأرض فكذا هذا المنجري يصلي إلى المشرق واليمن والشام وليس هو تعالى في هذه الجهات، ثم هو يعبد كما في هذه المواضع، ويحتمل أنه تعالى أمر بالتوجه إلى هذه المواضع المختلفة عند اختلاف الأحوال ليندفع وهم تحيزه في ذلك دليلا لمن عرفه أنه ليس بجهة منا وقيل : إن العرش جعل ويصير قبلة للقلوب عند الدعاء كما جعلت الكعبة قبلة للأبدان في حالة الصلاة. واستعمل لفظة الإنزال والتنزيل منصرف إلى الآتي بالقرآن، فأما القرآن فلا يوصف بالانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، والآتي به وهو جبريل عليه السلام كان ينزل من جهة العلو، كما أن مقامه كان بتلك الجهة. والله الموفق.
Their appeal to the consensus of raising hands towards the sky during supplication and prayer is invalid, for there is no evidence in that that God is in that direction. This is like the fact that they were commanded to face the Kaaba in prayer, yet He is not in the Kaaba, and they were commanded to cast their eyes towards the places of prostration while standing in prayer after the revelation of the verse {Successful are the believers, who are humbly submissive in their prayer} [23:1-2], after they used to pray with their eyes fixed towards the sky, yet He is not in the earth. Likewise during prostration they were commanded to place their faces on the ground, yet God is not under the earth. Similarly, this man prays facing the east, Yemen, and Syria, yet God is not in these directions. Moreover, He is worshipped in these places. It is possible that God commanded turning towards these different places in different circumstances to avert the illusion of His confinement in a direction, as evidence for one who knows Him that He is not in a direction from us. It was also said: The Throne was made and becomes the qibla of hearts during supplication, just as the Kaaba was made the qibla of bodies during prayer. The word 'inzāl' and 'tanzīl' (sending down) refers to the one who brought the Quran; as for the Quran, it is not described as moving from place to place, and the one who brought it, Gabriel (peace be upon him), used to descend from the direction of above, as his station was in that direction. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that the consensus (ijmā') of raising hands towards the sky during supplication proves that God is in that direction. The author states that this action provides no evidence for God's location. He then provides several analogies from Islamic practice to illustrate the point. First, Muslims are commanded to face the Kaaba during prayer, yet God is not in the Kaaba. Second, they were commanded to look at the place of prostration, though God is not in the earth. Similarly, a person may pray facing different directions (east, Yemen, Syria) without implying God is there. The author suggests that the purpose of these different directions is to avert the illusion (wahm) of God's confinement in a particular direction. He also mentions the explanation that the Throne (al-'arsh) is the qibla of hearts in supplication, just as the Kaaba is the qibla of bodies in prayer. Finally, he addresses the terms 'inzāl' and 'tanzīl' (sending down), clarifying that they refer to the one who brought the Quran (Jibril), not to a movement of the Quran itself. Jibril descended from a direction because his station was there, but God transcends direction.

Key Terms
الإجماع
المناجاة
القبلة
العرش
الإنزال والتنزيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Raising hands towards the sky in supplication indicates that Allah is located in that direction.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that such ritual actions do not provide evidence for God's location, as similar actions (facing the Kaaba, looking down) do not imply God is there. The purpose of these acts is to unify worship and avert the illusion of divine confinement.

Checkpoint

B) Because such actions do not constitute evidence for God's location, as shown by analogies from prayer.
C) Because raising hands is only done by a minority of Muslims.
D) Because the Quran explicitly denies that God is in the sky.

Key Takeaway
Ritual directions in Islam serve to unify worship, not to indicate God's location; Allah transcends all directions.

Interpreting Ambiguous Verses in Light of Definitive Proofs
Al-Nasafi argues that ambiguous verses about God's location must be interpreted according to definitive proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that ambiguous Quranic verses about God's attributes must be interpreted in light of definitive textual and rational proofs to avoid contradiction.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses must be interpreted to agree with definitive proofs, not used to overturn them.

How should we handle verses that seem to indicate God is in a place? Al-Nasafi provides a clear method: we start with what is definitively established — by clear verses and rational proofs — and then interpret any ambiguous verses in harmony with that foundation. This lesson presents that core hermeneutical principle, showing how to maintain consistency in understanding God's revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a refutation followed by a positive ruling.
The passage begins with 'الرد على...' indicating it is a direct response to an opposing view.
2
Identify the two kinds of proof
Find where Al-Nasafi distinguishes between definitive proofs (al-āya al-muḥkama and rational proofs) and ambiguous verses (al-āyāt al-muḥtamala). Understand their roles.
Definitive proofs admit no interpretation; ambiguous verses admit multiple interpretations.
3
Understand the ruling on ambiguous verses
What does Al-Nasafi say is obligatory when encountering ambiguous verses? He states they must be interpreted (ḥaml) to agree with the definitive proofs to avoid contradiction.
This is the key takeaway: ambiguous texts are not independent proofs; they are subordinate to clear ones.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this principle prevents contradictions in God's speech and preserves the integrity of rational proofs. Why is it necessary to interpret rather than reject?
The ultimate aim is to avoid tanāquḍ (contradiction) in the words of the All-Wise and All-Aware.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على ما تمسكوا به من الآيات الموهمة كونه تعالى في المكان. وأما تعلقهم بتلك الآيات فنقول في ذلك: إنا أثبتنا بالآية المحكمة التي لا تحتمل التأويل وبالدلائل العقلية التي لا احتمال فيها أن تمكنه في مكان مخصوص والأمكنة كلها محال، فلا يجوز إبطال هذه الدلائل بما تلوا من الآيات المحتملة ضروبا من التأويلات، بل يجب حملها على ما يوافق الدلائل المحكمة دفعا للتناقض عن دلائل الحكيم الخبير جلت أسماؤه.
Refutation of their clinging to verses that give the illusion that God is in a place. As for their clinging to those verses, we say regarding that: We have established by the definitive verse (al-āya al-muḥkama) that admits no interpretation, and by rational proofs (al-dalā’il al-‘aqliyya) that admit no possibility, that His being in a specific place and all places is impossible. Therefore, it is not permissible to invalidate these proofs by the verses they recite, which are ambiguous (muḥtamala) and admit various interpretations. Rather, it is obligatory to interpret them in a way that agrees with the definitive proofs, to avoid contradiction from the proofs of the All-Wise, the All-Aware, exalted are His names.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of opponents who cite verses from the Quran that might give the illusion (īhām) that God is in a place. The author states that we have already established, through definitive verses (al-āya al-muḥkama) that admit no interpretation, and through rational proofs (al-dalā’il al-‘aqliyya) that admit no possibility, that it is impossible (muḥāl) for God to be in any specific place or in places in general. Therefore, it is not permissible (lā yajūz) to invalidate (ibṭāl) these definitive proofs by citing ambiguous verses (al-āyāt al-muḥtamala) that admit various interpretations. Instead, it is obligatory to interpret (ḥaml) those ambiguous verses in a way that agrees with the definitive proofs, in order to avoid contradiction (tanāquḍ) in the words of the All-Wise, the All-Aware (al-ḥakīm al-khabīr). This establishes a key methodological principle: ambiguous texts must be interpreted in light of clear and definitive texts and rational proofs.

Key Terms
الآية المحكمة
الآيات المحتملة
الدلائل العقلية
التأويل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ambiguous verses about God's location should be taken literally even if they conflict with rational proofs.
Correction: Al-Nasafi states that such verses must be interpreted in a way consistent with definitive proofs to avoid contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafi, what should be done when ambiguous verses seem to imply God is in a place?
A) They should be taken literally because they are Quranic.
B) They should be rejected as unreliable.
C) They must be interpreted in a way consistent with definitive proofs and rational evidence.
D) They should be left without interpretation (tafwīḍ).

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous Quranic verses must always be interpreted in light of definitive textual and rational proofs to maintain consistency in divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'The Most Merciful is established over the Throne', does your heart immediately submit to the definitive proofs of Allah's transcendence, or do you find yourself tempted to imagine a physical direction? What does your automatic reaction say about the depth of your tawheed?
?
Is there any belief or practice you hold that is based solely on an ambiguous text, while you have ignored the clear proofs that contradict it? Will you commit today to re-examine that belief and interpret the text in light of the definitive proofs?
?
The method here is to avoid any contradiction in Allah's speech. In your own understanding of the Qur'an and Sunnah, do you tolerate contradictions? Are you willing to reinterpret your own cherished understandings when they conflict with clear proofs, even if it humbles your intellect?

Refuting the Anthropomorphic Argument from Raising Hands
Nasafi refutes the argument from raising hands by using analogies from worship and clarifying scriptural terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of raising hands in supplication does not prove that God is located in a specific direction, and how analogies from prayer practices support divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The direction of worship never confines the object of worship.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi responds to those who claim that raising hands towards the sky in prayer proves God is in the sky. He argues that this reasoning is flawed, using several analogies from the very acts of worship commanded in Islam. Before we read the text, consider how the Ka'ba is faced in prayer, yet God is not inside the Ka'ba. This sets the stage for a broader refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
First, read the Arabic and translation to get the gist. Then read again to identify the main argument and its refutation.
The passage uses several analogies; try to notice them.
2
Identify the flawed argument
The opponent argues that since Muslims raise hands to heaven in supplication, God must be in the sky. Nasafi rejects this as baseless.
3
Analyze the analogies
Nasafi gives four analogies: facing the Ka'ba, looking at prostration spot, placing face on ground, praying in different directions. In each, the direction of worship does not mean God is there.
Consider how each analogy undermines the opponent's reasoning.
4
Understand the theological rationale
Nasafi explains that God commands different directions to repel the illusion of His confinement, proving His transcendence beyond direction.
This reinforces the core idea of tanzih.
5
Clarify the term 'sending down'
Lastly, Nasafi addresses the word 'inzal' (sending down) used for the Qur'an. He says it refers to Jibril descending, not the Qur'an moving, and Jibril's station was above.
This shows that scriptural language does not imply divine location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلقهم بالإجماع برفع الأيدي إلى السماء عند المناجاة والدعاء باطل، لما ليس في ذلك دليل كونه تعالى في تلك الجهة. هذا كما أنهم أمروا بالتوجه في الصلاة إلى الكعبة وليس هو في الكعبة، وأمروا برمي أبصارهم إلى مواضع سجودهم حالة القيام في الصلاة بعد نزول قوله تعالى: ﴿ قَدْ أَفْلَحَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ ففِي صَلَاتِهِمْ خَشِعُونَ ﴾ وبعدما كانوا يصلون شاخصة أبصارهم نحو السماء وليس هو في الأرض، وكذا حالة السجود أمروا بوضع الوجوه على الأرض، وليس هو تعالى تحت الأرض فكذا هذا المنجري يصلي إلى المشرق واليمن والشام وليس هو تعالى في هذه الجهات، ثم هو يعبد كما في هذه المواضع، ويحتمل أنه تعالى أمر بالتوجه إلى هذه المواضع المختلفة عند اختلاف الأحوال ليندفع وهم تحيزه في ذلك دليلا لمن عرفه أنه ليس بجهة منا وقيل : إن العرش جعل ويصير قبلة للقلوب عند الدعاء كما جعلت الكعبة قبلة للأبدان في حالة الصلاة. واستعمل لفظة الإنزال والتنزيل منصرف إلى الآتي بالقرآن، فأما القرآن فلا يوصف بالانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، والآتي به وهو جبريل عليه السلام كان ينزل من جهة العلو، كما أن مقامه كان بتلك الجهة. والله الموفق.
And their holding fast to the consensus of raising the hands to the sky at the time of supplication and prayer is false, because there is no proof in that of His being—exalted is He—in that direction. This is just as they were commanded to face the Ka'ba in prayer while He is not in the Ka'ba, and they were commanded to cast their eyes towards the places of their prostration while standing in prayer after the revelation of His saying: 'Successful indeed are the believers who are in their prayers humbly,' while previously they used to pray with their eyes fixed towards the sky while He is not in the earth. Likewise, in the state of prostration they were commanded to place their faces on the ground, while He—exalted is He—is not under the ground. So too, this one who prays faces the east, Yemen, and Syria while He—exalted is He—is not in these directions; then He is worshipped as He is in these places. And it is probable that He—exalted is He—commanded facing these different places at different times to repel the illusion of His being confined in that, as a proof for whoever knows Him that He is not in a direction from us. And it was said: the Throne was made and becomes a qibla for hearts at the time of supplication, just as the Ka'ba was made a qibla for bodies in the state of prayer. And the word 'sending down' (inzal and tanzil) is applied to the one who brings the Qur'an. As for the Qur'an, it is not described as moving from place to place, and the one who brought it—Jibril, peace be upon him—used to descend from the direction of above, just as his station was in that direction. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument used by anthropomorphists who claim that raising the hands towards the sky in supplication indicates that God is located in the sky. The author (Imam al-Nasafi) states that this argument is invalid because there is no proof in that action for God being in a specific direction. He then provides several analogies to demonstrate that the direction of worship or prayer does not imply God's location. First, Muslims are commanded to face the Ka'ba in prayer, yet God is not confined within the Ka'ba. Second, they are commanded to look towards the place of prostration while standing, but God is not in the earth. Third, during prostration they place their faces on the ground, but God is not under the ground. Fourth, a worshipper may pray facing different directions like east, Yemen, Syria, but God is not in any of those directions; yet He is worshipped in all of them. The author then suggests a theological rationale: God commanded facing different directions at different times to dispel the illusion that He is confined in a particular direction, serving as a proof for those who know Him that He is not in any direction from us. He also mentions an interpretation that the Throne is made a qibla for hearts in supplication, just as the Ka'ba is a qibla for bodies in prayer. Finally, he addresses the term 'sending down' (inzal/tanzil) used for the Qur'an, clarifying that it refers to the one who brings the Qur'an (Jibril), not the Qur'an itself, which does not move. Jibril descended from above because his station was there, but that does not imply God is in a direction.

جهة
قبلة
العرش

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Raising hands towards the sky in supplication proves that God is located in the sky.
Correction: Nasafi refutes this by showing that direction in worship does not imply God's location; analogies from prayer practices demonstrate that God is beyond all directions.

Checkpoint

C) Because the sky is not a place for God.
D) Because the Prophet forbade raising hands.

Key Takeaway
Raising hands in supplication does not imply God's location; rather, it is a symbolic act of seeking, just as facing the Ka'ba does not mean God is inside it.

Interpreting Ambiguous Verses About God's Location
Nasafi establishes the method of interpreting mutashabihat by muhkamat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's method of interpreting ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat) by clear verses (muhkamat) and rational proofs to avoid contradiction.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses must be interpreted in light of clear verses and reason to avoid contradiction.

In this passage, Nasafi lays out a key principle of Islamic theology: when faced with ambiguous verses about God's attributes, we must interpret them in accordance with the clear verses (muhkamat) and rational proofs. He then powerfully demonstrates why taking all verses literally would lead to contradictions within the Qur'an itself, thereby establishing the necessity of a consistent hermeneutical method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument. Pay attention to key terms like al-āya al-muḥkama and al-dalā'il al-'aqliyya.
The passage uses a dense, scholarly style typical of kalam works.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage first states the principle (clear proofs take precedence), then provides a demonstration by listing verses that would conflict if taken literally.
Notice how Nasafi uses Quran 4:82 to argue that contradiction is absent from the Qur'an.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why this method is essential for understanding divine attributes and how it guards against anthropomorphism.
This principle is foundational for the Maturidi approach to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بتلك الآيات فنقول في ذلك: إنا أثبتنا بالآية المحكمة التي لا تحتمل التأويل وبالدلائل العقلية التي لا احتمال فيها أن تمكنه في مكان مخصوص والأمكنة كلها محال، فلا يجوز إبطال هذه الدلائل بما تلوا من الآيات المحتملة ضروبا من التأويلات، بل يجب حملها على ما يوافق الدلائل المحكمة دفعا للتناقض عن دلائل الحكيم الخبير جلت أسماؤه. ويحقق هذا أن حمل الآيات على ظواهرها والامتناع عن صرفها إلى ما تحتمله من التأويل يوجب تناقضا فاحشا فى كتاب الله تعالى وبنفيه استدل الله تعالى على أن القرآن من عنده بقوله تعالى: وَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ عِندِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلَافًا كَثِيرًا وببيانه أنه تعالى قال في آية: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى وقال في آية أخرى أينتُم مَّن فِي السَّمَله وقال في آية أخرى: مَا يَكُونُ مِن نَجْوَى ثَلَثَةِ إِلَّا هُوَ رَابِعُهُمْ لا رابعهم وقال في آية أخرى: ( إِنَّ رَبَّكَ لَبِالْمِرْصَادِ وقال في آية أخرى أَلَا إِنَّهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْ وتُحيط ثم لا وجه للقول إنه على العرش وإنه في السماء وإنه بالمشرق عند المناجين به والمغرب والروم والزنج والهند والعراق، بل في كل بلدة وقرية في حالة واحدة عند المتناجين في هذه الأمكنة في ساعة، ولا في ساعات بالتحول والتنقل، لاستحالة الحركة عليه، وأنه بالمرصاد، وأنه محيط بكل شيء من جوانبه الأربع فيصير كالحفة لكل شيء لما في كون شيء واحد في الأمكنة الكثيرة من الامتناع ، وليس من يجري بعض هذه الآيات على الظاهر، ويصرف ما وراء ذلك إلى ما عنده من التأويل بأولى من صاحبه الذي يرى في تعين المكان خلاف رأيه
And as for their holding fast to those verses, we say concerning that: We have established, by the clear verse (al-āya al-muḥkama) that does not admit interpretation and by rational proofs (al-dalā'il al-'aqliyya) that have no possibility [of doubt], that His being in a specific place, while all places are impossible for Him, is impossible. So it is not permissible to invalidate these proofs by what they have recited of the verses that admit various kinds of interpretation. Rather, they must be interpreted in a way that agrees with the clear proofs, to repel contradiction from the proofs of the All-Wise, the All-Aware—exalted are His names. And this is confirmed by the fact that taking the verses according to their literal meanings and refraining from turning them to what they admit of interpretation entails an enormous contradiction in the Book of God—and by denying it, God proved that the Qur'an is from Him by His saying: 'Had it been from other than God, they would have found therein much contradiction.' And its explanation: He—exalted is He—said in one verse: 'The Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne,' and said in another verse: 'Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?' and said in another verse: 'There is no secret conversation of three but He is the fourth of them, and not of five but He is the sixth,' and said in another verse: 'Indeed your Lord is indeed in ambush,' and said in another verse: 'Indeed, He is of all things encompassing.' Then there is no way to say that He is upon the Throne and that He is in the heaven and that He is in the east at the time of supplicators, and in the west, and in Rome, and in Zanj, and in India, and in Iraq—rather in every city and village in one state at the time of those supplicating in these places in one hour, and not in hours by movement and transition, due to the impossibility of motion upon Him—and that He is in ambush, and that He encompasses everything from its four sides, so He becomes like a container for everything, because of the impossibility of one thing being in many places. And he who applies some of these verses literally and diverts what is beyond that to his own interpretation is not more entitled than his counterpart who holds a different opinion concerning a specific place.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a methodological refutation of anthropomorphists who appeal to ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) to claim that God is in a specific place. The author first states the foundational principle: we have already proven, through clear verses (āyāt muḥkamāt) that do not admit interpretation and through rational proofs that have no possibility of doubt, that God cannot be confined to a place, and that being in a place is impossible for Him. Therefore, it is not permissible to invalidate these certain proofs by citing ambiguous verses that admit various interpretations. Rather, those verses must be interpreted in a way consistent with the clear proofs, in order to avoid contradiction in the divine revelations. The author then confirms this by pointing out that taking all verses literally leads to a glaring contradiction in the Qur'an itself, and God Himself has argued for the divine origin of the Qur'an by its lack of contradiction (Quran 4:82). He provides a demonstration: God says in one verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne' (20:5), in another 'Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?' (67:16), in another 'He is with you wherever you are' (58:7) and specifically 'There is no secret conversation of three but He is the fourth' (58:7), in another 'Indeed your Lord is indeed in ambush' (89:14), and in another 'He encompasses everything' (41:54). If taken literally, these would contradict each other: He cannot be on the Throne, in heaven, with everyone as a fourth, in ambush, and encompassing everything from all sides simultaneously without involving impossible notions like motion, change, or being in many places at once. The author then argues that one who takes some of these verses literally and interprets others arbitrarily is not superior to another who holds a different view on the specific place. This demonstrates the necessity of a consistent hermeneutical method that prioritizes clear rational and scriptural proofs.

الدلائل العقلية
التأويل
الظاهر
التناقض
الاستواء
العرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that all Quranic verses must be taken literally without considering rational proofs or the principle of non-contradiction.
Correction: Nasafi argues that such literalism leads to contradictions within the Qur'an itself, which God denies. Therefore, a consistent hermeneutical method is required that interprets ambiguous verses in light of clear proofs.

Checkpoint

B) Because taking them literally leads to contradictions among the verses, as shown by examples.
C) Because the Prophet forbade reading them literally.
D) Because they are only meant for scholars.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous Quranic verses about God's attributes must be interpreted in accordance with clear verses and rational proofs to preserve the Qur'an's freedom from contradiction.

The Impossibility of Literal Interpretation: Avoiding Contradiction in the Quran
Al-Nasafi argues that literal interpretation of anthropomorphic verses leads to contradiction, which contradicts the Quran's own proof of divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taking anthropomorphic verses literally leads to contradiction and why this proves the necessity of taʼwīl (interpretation).

Core Idea
Literal interpretation of these verses contradicts the Quran's own claim of consistency.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi demonstrates a key methodological principle: God Himself uses the absence of contradiction as proof of the Quran's divine origin. Yet if we insist on the literal meaning of verses describing God's attributes, we end up with conflicting statements. Al-Nasafi shows that this conflict forces us to abandon literal interpretation and adopt taʼwīl.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the rhetorical structure: thesis, proof, illustration, reductio, conclusion.
2
Identify the verses cited
List the Quranic verses al-Nasafi mentions. Think about how a literal reading of each would conflict with the others.
Verses: Q 20:5, 67:16, 58:7, 89:14, 41:54.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that literal interpretation is impossible and taʼwīl is necessary. Reflect on why picking some verses literally and interpreting others arbitrarily is not a valid option.
This establishes the principle of tanzīh (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحقق هذا أن حمل الآيات على ظواهرها والامتناع عن صرفها إلى ما تحتمله من التأويل يوجب تناقضا فاحشا فى كتاب الله تعالى وبنفيه استدل الله تعالى على أن القرآن من عنده بقوله تعالى: وَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ عِندِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلَافًا كَثِيرًا وبيانه أنه تعالى قال في آية: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى وقال في آية أخرى أَأَمِنتُم مَّن فِي السَّمَاء وقال في آية أخرى: مَا يَكُونُ مِن نَّجْوَىٰ ثَلَاثَةٍ إِلَّا هُوَ رَابِعُهُمْ وقال في آية أخرى: إِنَّ رَبَّكَ لَبِالْمِرْصَادِ وقال في آية أخرى أَلَا إِنَّهُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ مُحِيطٌ ثم لا وجه للقول إنه على العرش وإنه في السماء وإنه بالمشرق عند المناجين به والمغرب والروم والزنج والهند والعراق، بل في كل بلدة وقرية في حالة واحدة عند المتناجين في هذه الأمكنة في ساعة، ولا في ساعات بالتحول والتنقل، لاستحالة الحركة عليه، وأنه بالمرصاد، وأنه محيط بكل شيء من جوانبه الأربع فيصير كالحفة لكل شيء لما في كون شيء واحد في الأمكنة الكثيرة من الامتناع، وليس من يجري بعض هذه الآيات على الظاهر، ويصرف ما وراء ذلك إلى ما عنده من التأويل بأولى من صاحبه الذي يرى في تعين المكان خلاف رأيه، فإذا ظهرت صحة ما ادعينا من تعذر حمل الآيات على الظواهر، ووجوب الصرف إلى ما يصح من التأويلات.
And this is verified by the fact that applying the verses according to their literal meanings (ẓawāhir) and refraining from interpreting them according to what they may bear of interpretation (taʼwīl) necessitates a gross contradiction in the Book of God Most High; and by negating contradiction, God Most High proved that the Quran is from Him by His saying: 'And if it had been from other than God, they would have found therein much discrepancy' (Q 4:82). Its explanation: He said in one verse: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (Q 20:5), and said in another verse: 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven' (Q 67:16), and said in another verse: 'There is no secret conversation of three but that He is the fourth of them' (Q 58:7), and said in another verse: 'Indeed your Lord is ever watchful' (Q 89:14), and said in another verse: 'Indeed He encompasses all things' (Q 41:54). Then there is no basis for saying: He is on the Throne, and He is in heaven, and He is in the east for those who whisper to Him, and in the west, and in Rome, and in Zanj, and in India, and in Iraq, rather in every town and village, in a single state for those whispering in these places at one hour, nor in hours by moving and transferring, due to the impossibility of motion upon Him, and that He is watchful, and that He encompasses everything from its four sides, thus becoming like a container for everything, because of the impossibility of a single thing being in many places. And one who applies some of these verses literally and diverts the rest to his own interpretation is no more entitled than his companion who holds a contrary opinion regarding the determination of place. So when the correctness of what we claimed—the impossibility of applying the verses to their literal meanings and the necessity of diverting them to sound interpretations—has become evident.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a proof that taking anthropomorphic verses literally leads to contradiction in the Quran. The author argues that if one insists on the literal meaning (ẓāhir) of verses like "Istiwā'" (establishing on the Throne), "being in heaven," "being the fourth of three," "being watchful," and "encompassing everything," then these descriptions conflict with each other when considered together. For instance, if God is literally on the Throne, He cannot simultaneously be in heaven, nor can He be physically present in every location where people whisper to Him. The author points out that God Himself uses the lack of contradiction as a proof for the Quran's divine origin (Q 4:82). The argument then proceeds: taking some verses literally and others metaphorically is arbitrary—no one has more right to pick and choose than another. Therefore, the only consistent position is to acknowledge that literal interpretation is impossible and that taʼwīl (interpretation) is necessary to avoid contradiction. This establishes the principle of tanzīh (transcendence) and the rejection of anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
ظاهر (ẓāhir)
تأويل (taʼwīl)
تناقض (tanāquḍ)
استوى (istawā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Literal interpretation is the only way to respect the Quran's outward meaning.
Correction: The author shows that literal interpretation leads to internal contradiction, so it cannot be the only valid approach; taʼwīl is necessary to maintain consistency.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to prove that literal interpretation of anthropomorphic verses is inconsistent?
A) The consensus of the early Muslims
B) The Quran's own claim of no contradiction, and the mutual conflict of literal meanings
C) The impossibility of God having attributes
D) The need for a special interpreter

Key Takeaway
The Quran's own claim of consistency forces us to interpret anthropomorphic verses figuratively to avoid contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'the Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne', do you silently imagine a physical throne and a body sitting on it? This lesson reveals that such a mental image leads to contradiction. Are you willing to strip away that image and accept that God's 'istiwa’' is beyond your imagination? How would your worship change if you no longer pictured God as confined to any place? Commit today to consciously refer such verses to the scholars' ta'wil rather than your own mental pictures.
?
You believe the Quran is from God because it has no contradiction. But if you insist on taking these verses literally, you are introducing contradiction yourself. Do you have the humility to admit that your initial understanding might be wrong? Will you actively seek sound tafsir to correct your assumptions? Make a plan: find a reliable scholar's interpretation of Q 20:5 and study it this week.
?
Al-Nasafi says that picking some verses literally and interpreting others arbitrarily is inconsistent. Have you ever done that—accepting one verse as literal and another as figurative based on what feels comfortable? That is no more valid than your neighbour's opposite choice. This lesson demands consistency: either all anthropomorphic verses are literal (impossible) or all require ta'wil. Which camp will you choose? Let this be a turning point where you surrender your own preferences to the Quran's own logic.

Two Maturidi Approaches to Ambiguous Verses
Nasafi describes two approaches of Maturidi scholars toward ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major Maturidi approaches to Qur'anic ambiguous verses (mutashabihat): tafwid and ta'wil, and how both uphold divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Maturidis agree on transcendence but differ on interpreting ambiguous verses.

How should we handle Qur'anic verses that seem to describe God in physical terms? Maturidi scholars developed two valid approaches, both grounded in preserving tawhid. This passage from Nasafi outlines the difference between those who refrain from interpretation and those who engage in it cautiously.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that Nasafi first mentions the two groups and then explains each.
Pay attention to the heading that frames the discussion.
2
Identify the two approaches
The first group does not interpret (tafwid); they simply affirm the verses. The second group interprets (ta'wil) by considering possible meanings compatible with tawhid.
Both groups agree that God is not in a place or direction.
3
Understand the common ground
Despite their difference in method, both approaches affirm God's transcendence. The difference is in wording, not in core belief.
Nasafi explicitly states that the Salaf differed in expression but agreed in meaning.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows the flexibility within the Maturidi school. Neither approach is considered wrong; both are valid ways to protect tawhid.
The common misunderstanding is that Maturidis reject ta'wil entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظواهر، ووجوب الصرف إلى ما يصح من التأويلات.
[اختلاف المشايخ في الآيات المتشابهة]:
ثم بعد ذلك اختلف مشايخنا رحمهم الله:
منهم من قال في هذه الآيات: إنها متشابهة نعتقد فيها أن لا وجه لإجرائها على ظواهرها، ونؤمن بتنزيلها ولا نشتغل بتأويلها، ونعتقد أن ما أراد الله تعالى بها حق. وهؤلاء يطلقون ما ورد به الشرع فيقولون: الرحمن على العرش استوى ويقولون: إنه تعالى القاهر فوق عباده، وكذا كل آية في هذا. وما يروي عن السلف من ألفاظ يوهم ظاهرها إثبات الجهة والمكان فهو محمول على هذا. وبين السلف اختلاف في الألفاظ التي يطلقون فيها كل ذلك اختلاف منهم في العبارة مع اتفاقهم في المعنى، أنه تعالى ليس بمتمكن في مكان ولا متحيز في جهة.
ومنهم من يشتغل ببيان احتمال الآيات معاني مختلفة سوى ظواهرها ويقولون: نعلم أن المراد بعض ما تحتمله الألفاظ من المعاني التي لا تكون منافية للتوحيد والقدم، ولا يقطعون على مراد الله تعالى لانعدام دليل يوجب القطع على المراد وتعين بعض المعاني.
The apparent meanings, and the necessity of turning to valid interpretations. [The difference of the teachers regarding the ambiguous verses]: Then after that, our teachers (may God have mercy on them) differed: Among them are those who said concerning these verses: They are ambiguous; we believe that there is no way to apply them according to their apparent meanings, and we believe in their revelation and do not engage in their interpretation, and we believe that what God Almighty intended by them is true. These people utter what the Law has brought, saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne,' and they say: He is the Subduer above His servants, and likewise every verse in this regard. And what is narrated from the Salaf of expressions whose apparent meaning suggests the affirmation of direction and place is to be understood in this way. There is among the Salaf a difference in the expressions they use in all that—a difference in wording with agreement in meaning: that He is not located in a place nor confined to a direction. And among them are those who engage in explaining that the verses may have different meanings other than their apparent ones, and they say: We know that the intended meaning is some of the possible meanings that the words can bear, which are not contrary to tawhid and pre-eternity, and they do not make a definitive assertion about what God intended due to the lack of evidence that would necessitate certainty about the intended meaning and specify some of the meanings.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents the two major approaches within the Maturidi school regarding the ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) of the Qur'an. The first approach is that of tafwid (consigning the meaning to God): the verses are affirmed as they came, without engaging in ta'wil (interpretation). The proponents of this view recite the verses such as 'al-Rahmanu 'ala al-'arshi istawa' and similar, but they do not interpret them literally in a way that would attribute spatial location or direction to God. They also apply this to the sayings of the Salaf that might imply direction, understanding them in a sense consistent with God's transcendence. The second approach allows for ta'wil: these scholars examine the possible meanings of the verses that are compatible with tawhid and God's pre-eternity, and they do not definitively assert which meaning God intended, because there is no decisive evidence specifying one meaning. Thus, both groups agree on the core point that God is not limited by place or direction; their difference lies in whether to explicate possible meanings or to refrain entirely.

Key Terms
متشابهات (mutashabihat)
ظاهر (zahir)
تأويل (ta'wil)
تفويض (tafwid)
توحيد (tawhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Maturidi school rejects ta'wil entirely.
Correction: The Maturidi school actually accommodates both tafwid and ta'wil as valid approaches, as shown by the two groups mentioned by Nasafi.

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between the two Maturidi approaches to ambiguous verses?
A) One group affirms literal meanings; the other interprets metaphorically.
B) One group refrains from interpretation; the other engages in probable interpretation.
C) One group accepts only the apparent meaning; the other rejects all meanings.
D) One group denies the verses are from God; the other affirms them.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school includes both tafwid and ta'wil as valid methods for handling ambiguous verses, both preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne,' do you feel a tension between the literal wording and your belief that God is not in a place? Which of the two Maturidi approaches do you instinctively lean toward, and what does that reveal about your trust in God's transcendence?
?
The Salaf differed in wording but agreed on the meaning that God is not limited by space. Are you willing to hold a conviction without needing to explain it fully? Where in your life do you feel the urge to 'solve' ambiguity rather than submit to it?
?
If you were to encounter an ambiguous verse tomorrow, would your heart be more inclined to say 'I believe what God intended is true' (tafwid) or to seek a compatible interpretation (ta'wil)? What does your answer tell you about your relationship with God's words?

The Multiple Meanings of 'Arsh in the Qur'an and Poetry
Nasafi lists two meanings of 'arsh with poetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the word 'arsh can refer to either a physical throne (the greatest creation) or metaphorically to kingship/sovereignty, as supported by Arabic poetry.

Core Idea
'Arsh has a literal and a figurative meaning; context determines which is intended.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by analyzing the term 'arsh (throne), which appears in the Qur'an and in pre-Islamic poetry. He shows that besides the apparent meaning of a physical throne surrounded by angels, it is also used to mean kingdom or sovereignty. This linguistic analysis is crucial for interpreting verses about Allah's throne without falling into anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage about 'arsh
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice that Nasafi mentions two meanings: the literal throne and the metaphorical kingship.
The passage includes three poetic examples.
2
Examine the poetic evidence
Look at the three lines of poetry. Each uses 'arsh to mean kingdom or authority. Understand how this supports the metaphorical meaning.
3
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how having a metaphorical meaning for 'arsh allows us to interpret divine verses without attributing a physical body to Allah.
This connects to the broader topic of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العرش يذكر، ويراد به السرير المحفوف بالملائكة الذي هو أعظم المخلوقات وهو ظاهر في الشريعة، ويذكر ويراد به الملك.
قال الشاعر:
إذا ما بنو مروان زالت عروشهم وأودت كما أودت إياد وحمير
وقال زهير:
تداركتما عبسا وقد ثل عرشها وذبيان إذ زلت بأقدامها النعل
وقال النابغة:
عروش تفانوا بعد عز وإن هم هووا بعدما نالوا السلامة والغنـ
والاستواء يذكر ويراد به الاستقرار كقوله تعالى: ﴿ واستوت على الجودي ﴾، ويذكر ويراد به الاستقامة التي هي ضد الاعوجاج، ويذكر ويراد به التمام على ما قال تعالى: ﴿ ولما بلغ أشده واستوى ﴾، ويذكر ويراد به الاستيلاء كما يقال: استوى فلان على بلده كذا قال القائل في بشر بن مروان:
قد استوى بشر على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق
وقال الآخر:
اذكر بلانا بصفين ونصرتنا حتى استوى لأبيك الملك في عدن
أي ثبت له الملك فيه. ويذكر ويراد به الارتفاع والعلو كما قال تعالى: قـ
Then the 'arsh (throne) is mentioned, and by it is meant the bed surrounded by angels, which is the greatest of creation, and that is the apparent meaning in the Law; and it is mentioned, and by it is meant the kingdom. The poet said: 'When the Banu Marwan's thrones fell and they perished as perished Iyad and Humayr.' And Zuhayr said: 'You rescued 'Abs when its throne had toppled, and Dhubyan when the sandal slipped from their feet.' And al-Nabigha said: 'Thrones they perished after might, and they fell after attaining safety and spoils...' And istiwa is mentioned, and by it is meant settling, as His statement: 'And it settled upon the Judi'; and it is mentioned, and by it is meant uprightness which is the opposite of crookedness; and it is mentioned, and by it is meant completion, as His statement: 'When he reached his maturity and became complete'; and it is mentioned, and by it is meant domination, as it is said: 'So-and-so dominated his city' as the poet said about Bishr ibn Marwan: 'Bishr has dominated Iraq without sword or shed blood.' And another said: 'Remember our trial at Siffin and our victory until the kingship became established for your father in 'Adan,' meaning it became established for him. And it is mentioned, and by it is meant elevation and highness, as He said: (Qur'anic verse incomplete).
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed linguistic analysis of two key terms that appear in the mutashabihat: 'arsh (throne) and istiwa (establishing). The author shows that these words have multiple legitimate meanings in the Arabic language, apart from their literal or physical senses. For 'arsh, it can refer to the actual throne of God (the greatest creature) or metaphorically to kingdom or sovereignty, as evidenced by pre-Islamic poetry. For istiwa, he lists five possible meanings: (1) settling or coming to rest, as with Noah's ark; (2) uprightness, as opposed to crookedness; (3) completion, as in reaching maturity; (4) domination or conquest, as in taking control of a country; and (5) elevation and highness. Each meaning is supported by Qur'anic verses or Arabic poetry. The point is that the verses about God's 'arsh and istiwa do not necessarily imply anthropomorphism; they can be interpreted in ways consistent with God's transcendence. The author does not definitively choose one meaning but shows the possible interpretations, which is in line with the second approach mentioned earlier.

Key Terms
عرش ('arsh)
سرير (sarir)
مَلِك (mulk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the word 'arsh always and exclusively means a physical throne
Correction: In Arabic, 'arsh can also mean kingship or sovereignty, as shown by pre-Islamic poetry. The Qur'anic usage should be understood in light of this linguistic range.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a meaning of 'arsh besides the physical throne?
A) Knowledge
B) Kingdom
C) Power
D) Light

Key Takeaway
The word 'arsh in Arabic can refer both to a physical throne and to kingship, and this flexibility is essential for interpreting verses about God's Throne.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Now that you've seen literal and metaphorical meanings of 'arsh in Arabic poetry, does your heart resist the idea of a non-physical throne? What does that resistance reveal about your attachment to your own mental images of God?

Nasafi's lesson shows that context determines meaning. The Five Meanings of Istiwa' in the Qur'an and Arabic
Nasafi lists five meanings of istiwa' with scriptural and poetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct meanings of the word istiwa as enumerated by Imam al-Nasafi, supported by Qur'anic verses and poetry.

Core Idea
Istiwa' has at least five meanings; context alone determines which applies.

After discussing 'arsh, Imam al-Nasafi turns to the word istiwa' (establishing, settling), which appears in verses like 'the Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (Taha 5). He lists five possible meanings from the Arabic language: settling, uprightness, completion, domination, and elevation. Each is supported by a Qur'anic verse or a line of poetry. This linguistic survey shows that the word does not necessarily imply physicality or change.

This is part of the Maturidi approach to divine attributes.

The Two Types of Elevation and the Istila Interpretation
Nasafi distinguishes between two types of elevation and applies it to 'istiwa ala al-arsh'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi classifies elevation (uluww) into locative and rank elevation, and how he argues that 'istiwa' in the Quran can mean 'istila' (dominion) over the Throne.

Core Idea
Elevation can be of place or rank; 'Istiwa' over the Throne means Allah's dominion, not physical location.

Imam Nasafi begins by clarifying the meaning of elevation in the Quran. He distinguishes between physical height and metaphorical highness of rank. This distinction allows him to interpret the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa' as referring to Allah's absolute sovereignty over the throne, not a physical settling. In this passage, he lays out the foundation for this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of elevation
Nasafi states that the word 'uluww' can mean either locative elevation or rank elevation. Note how he uses the example of 'You and those with you have settled' to introduce this classification.
This is a key hermeneutical move.
2
See the application to 'istiwa'
He then says that 'istiwa' on the Throne can be interpreted as 'istila' (mastery). The Throne is mentioned specifically for honor, similar to how Allah says 'the she-camel of Allah' or 'the mosques of Allah'.
3
Consider the analogy of a governor
Nasafi draws an analogy: a governor is called 'governor of the town' even though he governs the whole region. Similarly, Allah is 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' even though He is Lord of everything.
This counters the idea that the Throne is a special locus of divine presence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسْتَوَيْتَ أَنتَ وَمَن مَّعَكَ وَهَذَا النَّوْعُ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى قَسِيمٍ يُذْكَرُ وَيُرَادُ بِهِ الْعُلُوُّ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْمَكَانِ وَيُذْكَرُ وَيُرَادُ بِهِ الْعُلُوُّ مِنْ حَيْثُ الرُّتْبَةِ. فَعَلَى هَذَا فَيَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُرَادُ مِنْهُ اسْتَوْلَى عَلَى الْعَرْشِ الَّذِي هُوَ أَعْظَمُ الْمَخْلُوقَاتِ وَتَخْصِيصُهُ بِالذِّكْرِ كَانَ تَشْرِيفًا لَهُ إِذْ إِضَافَةُ جُزْئِيَّةِ الْأَشْيَاءِ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لِيَتَعَظَّمَ ذَلِكَ كَمَا قَالَ: {نَاقَةَ اللَّهِ} وَ{أَنَّ الْمَسَاجِدَ لِلَّهِ} وَ{أَنَّهُ لَمَا قَامَ عَبْدُ اللَّهِ} وَغَيْرِهِ. أَوْ كَانَ لِدَلَالَةِ أَنَّ مَا دُونَهُ كَانَ مُسْتَوْلَىً عَلَيْهِ بِالِاسْتِيلَاءِ عَلَيْهِ كَمَا يُقَال: فُلَانٌ أَمِيرُ هَذِهِ الْبَلْدَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَ أَمِيرًا لِأَهْلِهَا وَلِرَسَاتِيقِهَا وَقُرَاهَا قَالَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى: {وَهُوَ رَبُّ الْعَرْشِ الْعَظِيمِ} وَإِنْ كَانَ رَبًّا لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ ظَاهِرٌ. وَتَزْيِيفُ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةِ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ لِمَكَانِ أَنَّ الِاسْتِيلَاءَ يَكُونُ بَعْدَ الضَّعْفِ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَنِسْبَتُهُمْ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ إِلَى الْمُعْتَزِلَةِ لَيْسَ بِشَيْءٍ لِأَنَّ أَصْحَابَنَا أَوَّلُوا هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ وَلَمْ تَخْتَصَّ بِهِ الْمُعْتَزِلَةُ وَكَوْنُ الِاسْتِيلَاءِ إِنْ كَانَ فِي الشَّاهِدِ عَقِيبَ الضَّعْفِ وَلَكِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ هَذَا عِبَارَةً عَنِ اسْتِيلَاءٍ عَنْ ضَعْفٍ فِي اللُّغَةِ بَلْ ذَلِكَ ثَبَتَ عَلَىٰ وِفَاقِ الْعَادَةِ. كَمَا يُقَال: عَلِمَ فُلَانٌ وَكَانَ ذَلِكَ فِي الْمَخْلُوقِينَ بَعْدَ الْجَهْلِ. وَيُقَال: قَدَرَ وَكَانَ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ الْعَجْزِ وَهَذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ جَائِزٌ فِي اللَّهِ تَعَالَى عَلَىٰ إِرَادَةِ تَحَقُّقِ الْعِلْمِ وَالْقُدْرَةِ بِدُونِ سَابِقَةِ الْجَهْلِ وَالْعَجْزِ فَكَذَا هَذَا عَلَىٰ أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ الْمَوْضُوعَ لِمَعْنَيَيْنِ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَلَا يَسْتَحِيلُ الْآخَرُ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ إِذَا أُضِيفَ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى مَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ دُونَ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ كَلَفْظَةِ الْعَجَبِ فَإِنَّ الْعَجَبَ فِي اللُّغَةِ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ اسْتِحْسَانِ الشَّيْءِ مَعَ الْجَهْلِ بِسَبَبِهِ فَإِذَا أُضِيفَ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فِي مِثْلِ قَوْلِهِ: {بَلْ عَجِبْتَ وَيَسْخَرُونَ} فِي قِرَاءَةِ مَنْ قَرَأَ بِضَمِّ التَّاءِ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ الِاسْتِحْسَانُ فَحَسْبُ فَفِي اللَّفْظِ الَّذِي مَا وُضِعَ لِلضَّعْفِ بَلْ وُضِعَ لِنَفَاذِ السَّلْطَنَةِ وَالتَّصَرُّفِ وَتَثْبُتُ فِيهِ سَابِقَةُ الضَّعْفِ لَا بِدَلَالَةِ اللَّفْظِ بَلْ بِوِفَاقِ الْعَادَةِ لِأَنْ لَا يُفْهَمَ مِنْهُ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَى اللَّهِ أَوْلَى وَاللَّهُ الْمُوَفِّقُ. وَلَوْ أُرِيدَ بِالْعَرْشِ الْمُلْكُ لَكَانَ الِاسْتِوَاءُ عِبَارَةً عَنِ التَّمَامِ أَيْ تَمَامِ الْمَمْلُوكِ كَمَا رُوِيَ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُمَا أَنَّهُ تَعَالَى خَلَقَ الْعَالَمَ فِي سِتَّةِ أَيَّامٍ ثُمَّ خَلَقَ الْبَشَرَ فِي الْيَوْمِ السَّابِعِ فَإِنَّهُمُ التَّمَامُ إِذْ خَلَقَ لَهُمْ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ وَقَدْ بَالَغَ أَئِمَّةُ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ فِي بَيَانِ مَا تَحْتَمِلُ هَذِهِ الْآيَاتُ مِنَ التَّأْوِيلَاتِ الَّتِي لَا تُنَافِي التَّوْحِيدَ وَالْقِدَمَ. أَعْرَضْنَا عَنْ ذِكْرِهَا مَخَافَةَ التَّطْوِيلِ إِذْ فِي هَذَا الْقَدْرِ كِفَايَةٌ لِمَنْ نَصَحَ نَفْسَهُ. وَبِاللَّهِ الرَّشَادُ. وَقَالُوا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: {وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ اللَّهُ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهٌ} أَرَادَ بِهِ ثُبُوتَ الْإِلَهِيَّةِ فِي السَّمَاءِ لَا ثُبُوتَ ذَاتِهِ كَمَا تَقُول: فُلَانٌ أَمِيرٌ بِبُخَارَىٰ وَسَمَرْقَنْدَ وَيُرَادُ
You and those with you have settled (استويت) — and this type divides into a division mentioned meaning elevation in terms of place, and mentioned meaning elevation in terms of rank. Thus, it is possible that what is meant by it is 'He gained mastery (استولى) over the Throne which is the greatest of creations, and its specific mention was an honor for it, since the attribution of particular things to Allah is to magnify that, as He said: 'the she-camel of Allah' and 'that the mosques are for Allah' and 'that when the servant of Allah stood' and other than that. Or it was to indicate that what is below it was mastered by mastery over it, as it is said: 'So-and-so is the governor of this town' even though he is governor over its people and its districts and its villages. Allah said: 'And He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' even though He is Lord of everything. And all of this is apparent. And the Ash'aris' falsification of this interpretation because of the fact that mastery (استيلاء) occurs after weakness, which is not conceivable in Allah, and their attribution of this interpretation to the Mu'tazila is nothing, because our associates (أصحابنا) interpreted this interpretation, and the Mu'tazila were not unique to it. And that mastery, even if in the witnessed world it is subsequent to weakness, but this was not an expression for mastery from weakness in the language; rather that is established in accordance with custom. Just as it is said: 'So-and-so knew' and that was among created beings after ignorance. And it is said: 'He had power' and that was after incapacity. And this usage is permissible with respect to Allah intending the realization of knowledge and power without preceding ignorance and incapacity. So likewise this. Moreover, the word set for two meanings, one of which is impossible for Allah and the other not impossible, is understood from it, when attributed to Allah, what is not impossible for Him, not what is impossible for Him, like the word 'wonder' (العجب); for wonder in the language is an expression for approval of something along with ignorance of its cause. So when it is attributed to Allah in such as His saying: 'But you wondered and they mock' in the reading of whoever read with a damma on the ta, it is understood as approval only. So in the word that was not set for weakness but set for the penetration of sovereignty and disposal, and in which precedence of weakness is established not by the indication of the word but by accordance with custom, it is more fitting that not be understood from it what is impossible for Allah. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And if by 'Throne' sovereignty (الملك) is meant, then 'istiwa' (استواء) is an expression for completion, i.e., completion of the possessed, as narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both: 'He created the world in six days, then created humans on the seventh day, for they are the completion, since He created everything for them.' And the imams of Ahl al-Sunna have elaborated in explaining what these verses admit of interpretations that do not contradict tawhid and preeternity. We have refrained from mentioning them out of fear of prolixity, since this amount is sufficient for one who advises himself. And with Allah is guidance. And they said concerning His saying: 'And He is the one who in heaven is Allah and on earth is the God' — He meant by it the establishment of divinity (الألوهية) in heaven, not the establishment of His essence, as you say: 'So-and-so is an emir in Bukhara and Samarkand' and is meant
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is an extended defense of the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'الرحمن على العرش استوى' as meaning Allah's dominion and mastery (استيلاء) over the Throne, rather than a literal physical settling. The author begins by classifying the concept of 'uluww (elevation) into two types: locative elevation and rank elevation. He then applies this to the verse, suggesting that 'استوى' here means 'استولى' (gained mastery), and that the specific mention of the Throne is for honor, just as Allah specifically mentions 'the she-camel of Allah' or 'the servant of Allah' to magnify them. He also draws an analogy: just as saying 'so-and-so is the governor of this town' does not negate that he governs the surrounding areas, so Allah's being 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' does not contradict His being Lord of everything. The author then refutes the Ash'ari objection that 'استيلاء' implies prior weakness, which is impossible for Allah. He argues that this interpretation is not exclusive to the Mu'tazila, as the author's own school (Maturidis) also holds it. He explains that although in human experience mastery often follows weakness, the word itself in the Arabic language does not necessarily imply that; rather, the temporal sequence is a customary association, not a linguistic necessity. He gives parallel examples: words like 'عَلِمَ' (knew) and 'قَدَرَ' (was able) are used for Allah even though they imply prior ignorance or incapacity when applied to creatures; but when applied to Allah, they mean the perfection of knowledge and power without any prior deficiency. Similarly, the word 'استيلاء' should be understood as perfect sovereignty without any prior weakness. He then uses the example of the word 'عجب' (wonder), which in human language implies approval mixed with ignorance, but when attributed to Allah in the Quran (in the reading 'بل عجبت'), it means only approval, without ignorance. Thus, for a word that can bear two meanings—one impossible for Allah and one possible—the possible meaning is adopted when the word is attributed to Allah. The author then presents an alternative interpretation: if 'العرش' means 'الملك' (sovereignty), then 'الاستواء' means 'التمام' (completion), as reported from Ibn Abbas: after creating the world in six days, Allah created humans on the seventh day, completing the creation for their benefit. The author concludes by noting that Ahl al-Sunna imams have extensively discussed permissible interpretations that do not compromise tawhid and divine preeternity, but he refrains from further detail. Finally, he turns to the verse 'وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله', explaining that it means the establishment of divinity and lordship in heaven and earth, not the physical location of Allah's essence. He gives the analogy of saying 'so-and-so is an emir in Bukhara and Samarkand', meaning he exercises authority there, not that his body is present in both places simultaneously.

استولى
العرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that the specific mention of the Throne in the verse means that Allah is physically located on it.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the specific mention is for honor, not to indicate physical location. He provides examples and analogies to show that the verse points to Allah's dominion, not His spatial presence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi in this passage, why is the Throne specifically mentioned in the verse 'al-Rahman ala al-arsh istawa'?
A) Because Allah is physically present on the Throne
B) To honor the Throne as the greatest creation
C) Because the Throne is the only creation that contains Allah
D) To indicate that the Throne is independent of Allah's power

Key Takeaway
The verse about the Throne indicates Allah's sovereignty over the greatest creation, not His physical location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear 'Allah is over the Throne,' do you instinctively picture a direction or location? After this lesson, what will you do to correct that ingrained thought?
?
If Allah's mention of the Throne is for honor and to show His comprehensive dominion, what does that mean for how you view other 'great' things in your life? Are you tempted to assign more importance to them than they deserve?
?
The governor analogy shows that authority over the capital implies authority over the whole country. Does your daily life reflect a belief that Allah's authority over the greatest creation means He is in control of every small detail? If not, what is the first step to change that?
