Refuting the Transference of Vision Conditions to God
Imam Nasafi refutes the transfer of physical vision conditions to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the physical conditions required for vision in the created world (such as physical contact, image impression, and light) cannot be transferred to God, and how this establishes divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The conditions of physical vision cannot be applied to the Unseen God.

This passage explains the Maturidi position that vision of God does not require the conditions that apply to physical sight. The author recounts and refutes the claims of those like al-Nazzam who made bodiliness a necessary cause of vision, showing that each condition is impossible for God. The lesson clarifies the distinction between the witnessed world and the Unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the term 'المماسة' (physical contact) appears early.
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage discusses three conditions for physical vision: physical contact, impression of image in the eye, and light. Find each one in the text.
3
Understand the evidence for each condition
For each condition, the author provides evidence from ordinary experience (e.g., light is needed because vision fails in darkness). Reflect on how these proofs rely on physicality.
The condition of image impression is tied to the human eye and blindness.
4
Grasp the principle of non-transference
The author concludes that since the conditions cannot apply to God (who is not a body), the ruling of vision cannot be transferred. Understand the logical step: no conditions → no ruling.
This is the core argument: distinction between witnessed and Unseen.
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this argument preserves God's transcendence by preventing anthropomorphism. Think about why the Mu'tazilites might have insisted on these conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمماسة لا تجوز إلا على الأجسام. ويصير بيان هذا الشرط تحقيقا لجعل النظام علة الرؤية كونه جسما، إذ المماسة لا تتصور إلا بين جسمين. آلة وعند بعضهم يشترط أيضا انطباع صورة المرئي في العين التي هي الرؤية، كما ينطبع في المرآة صورة الأجسام المقابلة لها لولا ذلك الانطباع لما حصل العلم بالمرئي، دليله انعدام الرؤية عند زوال الانجلاء عن العين وبطلان الانطباع وتبدل البصر بالعمى، ولا يتصور انطباع الصورة إلا في حق الجسم إذ لا صورة لغيره، قالوا: وكذلك الضوء الحاصل في هواء المسافة الممتدة بين الرائى والمرئي شرط الحصول الرؤية، دليله انعدامها عند ارتفاع الضوء بطريان ظلام الليل، وذا محال في حق الله تعالى فصح بهذا ما ادعينا من انعدام تعدي علة الرؤية وشروطها إلى الغائب ولا يتعدى الحكم أيضا. يحققه أن أحدهما إما العلة وإما الشرط، لو تعدى وحده بدون صاحبه لا يتعدى الحكم، فإذا لم يتعديا جميعا أولى أن لا يتعدى هذا كله لبيان المفارقة بين الشاهد والغائب في العلة والشروط، ومنع التعدية لانعدام المساواة.
Physical contact is only possible for bodies. And clarifying this condition serves to establish that al-Nazzam made being a body the cause of vision, since physical contact can only be conceived between two bodies. Tool. And according to some, it is also required that the image of the seen object be impressed upon the eye, which is the vision, just as the image of facing bodies is impressed in a mirror; were it not for that impression, knowledge of the seen object would not be obtained. Its evidence is the non-existence of vision when the clarity of the eye disappears, the invalidation of the impression, and the change of sight into blindness. And the impression of an image can only be conceived for a body, since there is no image for a non-body. They said: likewise, the light present in the air extending between the seer and the seen is a condition for the occurrence of vision; its evidence is its non-existence when light rises due to the onset of night darkness. And that is impossible in the case of God Almighty. Thus by this is established what we claimed: non-transference of the cause of vision and its conditions to the Unseen, nor does the ruling transfer. He verifies that either one of them is the cause or the condition; if it transferred alone without its companion, the ruling would not transfer. So if they do not both transfer, it is even more fitting that this entire [argument] not transfer, in order to demonstrate the distinction between the witnessed and the Unseen in cause and conditions, and to prevent transference due to lack of equality.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the possibility of vision of God by showing that the conditions required for vision in the physical world (such as physical contact, impression of image, light) cannot apply to God, who is not a body. The author first recounts the views of those who require these conditions, particularly al-Nazzam who made bodiliness a cause of vision. Then he provides evidence for each condition from ordinary experience. Finally he argues that since these conditions cannot be found in God, the ruling (vision) cannot be transferred to Him. The key point is the distinction between the witnessed (al-shahid) and the Unseen (al-gha'ib); conditions that hold for the former cannot be assumed for the latter without proof of equality.

العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It might be thought that if we prove God is not a body, then vision is impossible; but the author is actually showing that the conditions of physical vision are absent, not that vision is essentially impossible for God.
Correction: The passage does not assert that vision of God is impossible absolutely; rather, it argues that the physical conditions required for created beings' vision do not apply to God, leaving open the possibility of a non-physical vision.

Key Takeaway
Physical conditions for vision in the created world cannot be transferred to God, preserving divine transcendence and the distinction between the witnessed and the Unseen.

Impossibility of Seeing God in Whole or in Part
Imam Nasafi argues by division that God cannot be seen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God cannot be seen because He is neither finite nor divisible.

Core Idea
God cannot be seen because He is not finite and not divisible.

Imam Nasafi presents a crisp logical division: any seen object is either seen entirely or partially. Since God is neither finite (to be seen entirely) nor composed of parts (to be seen partially), it follows that physical vision of God is impossible. This argument highlights God's transcendence beyond the conditions of created vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how the argument is structured as a division (taqsim).
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author divides the seen object into two categories: seen wholly or seen in part. Write down each possibility and the reason it is ruled out for God.
God is not finte (muntah) so He cannot be seen wholly; God is not divisible (mutab'id/mutajazzi') so He cannot be seen partially.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Since both possibilities are impossible, the author concludes that visual perception of God is impossible. Ask yourself: is there any third possibility? The argument says no.
This argument is valid by exhaustion – if all logical options are impossible, the conclusion stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام من حيث ذات المرئي فنقول : المرئي إما أن يرى كله وإما أن يرى بعضه، ولا جائز أن يرى الله تعالى كله لأنه تعالى ليس بمتناه، ولا جائز أن يرى بعضه، لأنه تعالى ليس بمتبعض ولا متجزئ، وإذا امتنع الوجهان امتنعت الرؤية لانعدام القسم الثالث.
As for the discussion from the perspective of the essence of the seen object: we say: The seen object is either seen in its entirety or seen in part. It is not possible that God Almighty be seen in His entirety because He is not finite, and it is not possible that He be seen in part because He is not composed of parts nor divisible. So if both possibilities are impossible, vision is impossible due to the lack of a third division.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This is a concise argument by division (taqsim). The author assumes that anything seen is either seen wholly or partially. God is not finite (so cannot be seen wholly) and not divisible (so cannot be seen partially). Therefore, vision of God is impossible. This argument is based on the nature of the seen object itself, concluding that physical vision cannot apply to God.

Key Terms
المرئي
المتناهي
المتبعض
المتجزئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because God is too large
B) Because God is not composed of parts
C) Because parts are only for bodies
D) Because God is infinite

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence over finiteness and divisibility logically precludes any possibility of physical vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The argument depends on God's infinity and simplicity. Do you live as if God is infinite and beyond your senses, or do you sometimes act as if He is limited or confined? How can this truth change the way you worship and trust Him today?

Why We Do Not See God Now
Imam Nasafi eliminates possible reasons for not seeing God now.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of vision of God in this world cannot be attributed to temporary impediments like defective eyesight, distance, or a veil, according to Imam Nasafi's argument.

Core Idea
The inability to see God now is not due to a flaw in our eyesight but to God's nature.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a potential objection: If God can be seen in the afterlife (as per Sunni belief), why is He not seen now? He systematically eliminates possible causes for our current inability, showing that the only explanation is that God is inherently not visible in this world. This argument reinforces divine transcendence and sets the stage for understanding beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the logical structure: enumeration of possible causes then elimination.
2
Identify the three causes eliminated
The passage lists three possible reasons for not seeing God: a defect in the sight, fineness/subtlety of God, and distance or a veil. Write them down.
The argument against each is that it is impossible or irrelevant with respect to God.
3
Understand why each cause fails
For defect in sight: sighted people also do not see God. For fineness/subtlety: these are attributes of bodies, not God. For distance: it is impossible to conceive distance between God and the seer.
The text cuts off before concluding, but the implication is clear: God is not visible in this world by nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام من حيث الاستدلال بانعدام الرؤية للحال فإنا لا نراه للحال وانعدام رؤية ما هو المرئي لا يخلو من وجوه أحدها آفة في البصر. والآخر معنى في المسافة بين الرائي والمرئي وهو البعد والحجاب. والمعاني كلها منعدمة. أما الآفة في البصر فمنعدمة لأن البصراء لا يرون. وكذا الرقة واللطافة لاستحالتهما على ذات الله إذ هما من صفات الأجسام. وكذا البعد لاستحالة المسافة
As for the discussion from the perspective of inferring the non-existence of vision in the present: we do not see Him now. And the non-existence of vision of that which is the seen object is not without one of [several] aspects: either a defect in the sight, or a meaning in the distance between the seer and the seen object, namely distance or a veil. All these meanings are non-existent. As for the defect in the sight, it is non-existent because the sighted do not see. Likewise fineness and subtlety are impossible for God's essence, since they are attributes of bodies. Likewise distance, because distance is impossible...
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage attempts to prove that the absence of vision of God now (in this world) cannot be attributed to temporary impediments like defective eyesight, distance, or a veil. The author argues that since sighted people do not see God, it cannot be due to a defect in their eyes. Also, fineness (riqqa) and subtlety (latafa) are attributes of bodies and cannot apply to God. Distance also cannot be conceived between God and the seer. Therefore, the only remaining explanation is that God is not visible by nature. The argument is incomplete in the text but the direction is clear.

Key Terms

البعد
الحجاب
الرقة
اللطافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that if they had better eyesight, they could see God now.
Correction: The passage argues that even the sighted do not see God, so defective eyesight is not the reason.

Checkpoint

A) Because all people are sighted
B) Because sighted people also do not see God
C) Because sight is not needed for seeing God
D) Because God is invisible to all

Key Takeaway
The inability to see God now is not due to any defect in our sight or distance, but because God's nature is not visible in this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you could see God right now, would your life change? Would you still need faith? This lesson says that God's nature is not visible in this world. Are you content to live by faith without sight, or do you secretly wish for a sign? Repent of any impatience and resolve to trust His wisdom.
?
The author eliminates every physical cause for not seeing God, leaving only His transcendent nature. Does that humble you? How often do you try to reduce God to something you can grasp? Commit to worshiping Him as He is, not as you imagine.

Refutation of Vision Now by Eliminating Obstacles
Nasafi eliminates all obstacles to vision to prove God's essence is not visible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi theologians argue that God's invisibility in this world is due to His essence being inherently non-visible, using an argument by elimination of all possible obstacles.

Core Idea
God's invisibility is not due to any defect or veil, but because He is not seeable by nature.

This passage presents a crisp rational argument from Imam Nasafi. He begins by noting that we do not see God now. Then he systematically lists all possible reasons why something might not be seen, and eliminates each one as applying to God. The only remaining explanation is that God’s essence is simply not of the type that can be seen. This is a key Maturidi argument against anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
The author uses a very structured argument.
2
Identify the obstacles eliminated
List all the potential obstacles to vision that the author mentions: affliction in the eye, distance, veil, thinness/subtleness. Notice how each is addressed.
Thinness/subtleness are attributes of bodies, so they cannot apply to God.
3
Understand the conclusion
After eliminating all obstacles, the author concludes that the only remaining reason is that God's essence is not capable of being seen. Recognize the theological importance: God's transcendence is preserved.
This argument is used to refute anthropomorphic interpretations of certain Quranic verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام من حيث الاستدلال بانعدام الرؤية للحال فإنا لا نراه للحال وانعدام رؤية ما هو المرئي لا يخلو من وجوه أحدها آفة في البصر. والآخر معنى في المسافة بين الرائي والمرئي وهو البعد والحجاب. والمعاني كلها منعدمة. أما الآفة في البصر فمنعدمة لأن البصراء لا يرون. وكذا الرقة واللطافة لاستحالتهما على ذات الله إذ هما من صفات الأجسام. وكذا البعد لاستحالة المسافة بين الله وبين خلقه، إذ هي جارية فيما بين المتحيزين وكذا الحجاب والتحيز من أمارات الجواهر. و إذا انعدمت الرؤية مع ارتفاع الموانع لم يبق لانعدامها وجه إلا خروج الذات عن أن يكون جائز الرؤية.
As for the discussion regarding the inference from the absence of vision at present: We do not see Him at present, and the absence of vision of what is capable of being seen cannot be free from [one of] aspects: one of them is an affliction in the eye, another is a condition in the distance between the seer and the seen, namely distance and a veil. And all these meanings are absent. As for the affliction in the eye, it is absent because those with sight do not see Him. Similarly, thinness and subtleness are impossible for the essence of God, as they are attributes of bodies. Similarly, distance is impossible due to the impossibility of spatial interval between God and His creation, for it occurs only between spatial objects. Similarly, the veil and spatiality are signs of substances. And when vision is absent while all obstacles are absent, there remains no reason for its absence except that the essence is not capable of being seen.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by elimination. The author lists possible reasons why something is not seen: defect in the seer's eyesight, or something in between like distance or veil. He then shows all these are impossible in God's case. Eyesight is fine (many see but not God), thinness and subtlety are impossible for God (they are bodily attributes), distance is impossible because God is not in a spatial relation, veil and spatiality are impossible because they pertain to substances. Since all obstacles are absent, the only remaining reason is that God is not capable of being seen.

Key Terms
آفة
بصر
مسافة
حجاب
رقة
لطافة
تحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God is not seen now because of a veil or our spiritual deficiency.
Correction: The author argues that all such obstacles are impossible for God; the real reason is that His essence is inherently not visible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the only remaining reason for God not being seen now?
A) Our eyes are defective
B) There is a veil
C) God's essence is not capable of being seen
D) He is too far

Key Takeaway
When all external obstacles are absent, the non-occurrence of vision proves that the object itself is not seeable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God is not seeable by nature, what does that mean for how you seek intimacy with Him? Are you trying to 'see' Him through imagination or emotional experiences that might reduce Him to something created? How can you instead draw near to Him through what He has revealed?
?
In moments of doubt, do you catch yourself wishing for a visible sign or proof? How does this passage challenge that desire and call you to a deeper trust that is not based on sight? What would it take for you to be content with the unseen God?

The Mountain Verse Argument Against Mu'tazila
Nasafi explains the verse about the mountain to refute Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the conditional structure of the mountain verse to logically refute the Mu'tazila reinterpretation of 'seeing Allah' as 'seeing a sign.'

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila reinterpretation contradicts the verse's own condition.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a sharp logical argument against the Mu'tazila, who deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the hereafter. By analyzing the condition in the verse 'If the mountain remains in its place, then you will see Me,' he shows that their reinterpretation leads to an absurd contradiction. The sign they claim appears only when the condition is false, making their reading incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the source Arabic and then the literal English translation to get a sense of Nasafi's argument.
Pay attention to the conditional sentence.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila reinterpretation
Notice that the Mu'tazila reinterpret 'you will see Me' as 'you will see My sign.' Understand their motivation: to deny the vision of Allah.
3
Analyze the logical flaw
Examine the condition: the sign (mountain crumbling) appears only if the mountain does NOT remain. But the verse says 'if it remains... then you will see Me/sign.' If the mountain remains, the sign does not appear. So the Mu'tazila's reinterpretation makes the condition false whenever the consequent is true, which is absurd.
Think about the timing of the sign.
4
Consider the additional point about Musa
Nasafi adds that Musa already had direct knowledge of Allah. Requesting a proof of existence would be irrational. This reinforces that the request was for vision, not a sign.
This strengthens the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس وهو الذي يبين حيرة المعتزلة وعنادهم وبلادة أفهامهم، أن الله تعالى قال : فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَركني فيصير على تقدير كلام هؤلاء الجهلة بالحقائق فإن استقر مكانه فسوف ترى آيتي، ولا يخفي على من له أدنى لب أن هذه الآية ترى عند اندكاك الجبل لا عند استقراره بل عند استقراره تنعدم رؤية الآية. يحققه أنه عليه السلام كان عرفه وسمع كلامه وجعل يناجيه، وليس من دأب : العاقل أن يقول لمن سبقت له به معرفة وفاوضه في الكلام وناجاه أن يقول له: عرفني نفسك وأقم لي دلالة ثبوتك ووجودك، ولو قال ذلك لنسب إلى الجنون والحمق.
And the fifth—which clarifies the confusion of the Mu'tazila, their stubbornness, and the dullness of their understandings—is that Allah Almighty said: 'So if it remains in its place, then you will see Me (tarakunī).' So according to the interpretation of these ignorant people of realities, it would become: 'If it remains in its place, then you will see My sign (āyatī).' But it is not hidden from anyone possessing the least intellect that this sign is seen at the crumbling of the mountain, not at its stability; rather, at its stability, the vision of the sign ceases. It is confirmed that he (peace be upon him) knew Him and heard His speech and began to converse with Him, and it is not the habit of an intelligent person to say to one with whom he has prior acquaintance and with whom he has conversed and spoken: 'Make Yourself known to me and establish for me a proof of Your establishment and existence.' If he said that, he would be attributed to insanity and foolishness.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, which directly exposes the Mu'tazila error. Allah says: 'If the mountain remains in its place, then you will see Me' (Qur'an 7:143). If we reinterpret 'see Me' as 'see My sign' (as the Mu'tazila claim to avoid vision of Allah), then the condition would mean: if the mountain remains, you will see My sign. But the sign (the mountain's crumbling) occurs when the mountain does not remain. So the reinterpretation collapses. Additionally, the author adds that Musa already knew Allah and spoke with Him; asking for a proof of existence would be absurd. Therefore, the request was for vision, not a sign.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse about the mountain supports the idea that Allah's sign is seen when the mountain remains stable.
Correction: Actually, the sign (the mountain crumbling) appears when the mountain does not remain; thus the vision in the verse is not a sign but direct vision of Allah.

Checkpoint

A) When the mountain remains stable.
B) When the mountain crumbles.
C) When Musa speaks to Allah.
D) When the people demand it.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument shows that the Mu'tazila reinterpretation of the verse is logically self-defeating because the sign appears only when the mountain crumbles, not when it remains.

?

?

The Textual Corruption in al-Jubba'i's Interpretation
Nasafi refutes al-Jubba'i's claim that the request for vision was to increase certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi refutes al-Jubba'i's interpretation of Musa's request for vision based on the principle of textual integrity.

Core Idea
Twisting Allah's words leads to disbelief.

Al-Jubba'i, a Mu'tazili theologian, claimed that Prophet Musa's request to see Allah was not literal but a pedagogical device to strengthen the faith of the Israelites. Nasafi refutes this by exposing the textual deviation required by such an interpretation. This lesson examines his argument and its implications for Quranic hermeneutics.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Notice how Nasafi builds his refutation step by step.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse quoted (أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ).
2
Identify the key point of deviation
Nasafi argues that al-Jubba'i's interpretation changes the addressee of the verse. Identify the shift from 'Show me' to 'Show them' and why that matters.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Nasafi uses an analogy: if such reinterpretation is allowed, it could change other Quranic narratives (e.g., builder of Kaaba). Consider why this would be disbelief.
This shows the principle that distorting divine speech is a serious offense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه ليس بمرئي ليكون ذلك أشد تمكنا في قلوبهم إلى هذا الاعتراض ذهب الجبائي ومن ساعده. [فساد هذا الرأي]: وهذا أيضا فاسد فإن الله تعالى أخبر أنه قال : أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ثم قال له: لَن تَركني ولو كان الأمر على ما زعموا لكان من حق الكلام أن يقول: أرهم ينظروا إليك ثم يقول لهم: لن تروني، فإذا هذا عدول عن إخبار الله تعالى وصرف له إلى غير ما أخبر ، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يقال : إن الله تعالى وإن أخبر عن نوح أنه ركب السفينة كان المراد منه إدريس، وأن الباني للكعبة هو إسحاق مع يعقوب وإن كان الله تعالى قال : يَرْفَعُ إِبْرَاهِمُ الْقَوَاعِدَ مِنَ الْبَيْتِ وَإِسْمَعِيل الآية ولو قيل ذا لم يخف كفر هذا القائل وتكذيبه الله تعالى في إخباره، فكذا هذا.
That he is not visible so that that would be more firmly established in their hearts. To this objection al-Jubba'i and those who agreed with him resorted. [Corruption of this opinion]: This too is corrupt, for Allah the Exalted informed that He said: 'Show me so that I may look at You,' then He said to him: 'You will not see Me.' If the matter were as they claimed, it would be proper for the speech to say: 'Show them so that they may look at you,' then He would say to them: 'You will not see Me.' So this is a deviation from the information of Allah the Exalted and a turning of it to other than what He informed. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible to say that Allah the Exalted, although He informed about Noah that he boarded the ark, the intended meaning was Idris, and that the builder of the Ka'ba was Isaac with Jacob, although Allah the Exalted said: 'Abraham raises the foundations of the House, and Ishmael'—the verse. And if this were said, the disbelief of this speaker and his denying Allah the Exalted in His information would not be hidden. So likewise is this.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection by al-Jubba'i (a Mu'tazili theologian) regarding the request of Prophet Musa (Moses) to see Allah. Al-Jubba'i claimed that the request was not for actual vision, but rather to increase certainty in the hearts of the people, implying that the words 'Show me so that I may look at You' were a pedagogical device. Nasafi refutes this by pointing out the direct Quranic text: Allah informed that Musa said 'Show me' and Allah replied 'You will not see Me.' If al-Jubba'i's interpretation were correct, the text should have said 'Show them' and 'You will not see Me.' Changing the addressee is a deviation (عدول) from Allah's exact words. Nasafi then argues that such arbitrary reinterpretation leads to absurdities, like claiming that the builder of the Ka'ba was Isaac instead of Abraham and Ishmael, which would be clear disbelief. Thus, al-Jubba'i's position is corrupt.

الجبائي
عدول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Musa's request to see Allah was not literal but a pedagogical device to increase the people's certainty.
Correction: The Quranic text clearly shows Musa said 'Show me' and Allah replied 'You will not see Me.' Any interpretation that changes the addressee distorts Allah's words and leads to disbelief.

Checkpoint

A) It claims vision is impossible for Allah.
B) It misattributes the request to the seventy chosen ones.
C) It changes the addressee of the Quranic verses, implying textual distortion.
D) It denies the existence of prophets after Musa.

Key Takeaway
Altering the addressee of a divine speech is a form of textual distortion that can lead to outright disbelief.

If Vision Were Impossible, Musa Would Have Immediately Refuted
Nasafi presents a prophetic argument for the possibility of divine vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that Prophet Musa's delayed response to the request for vision proves that seeing Allah is rationally possible and not inherently disbelief.

Core Idea
If vision were impossible, Musa would have refuted immediately.

Prophet Musa's response to his people's request to see Allah is a key proof in the kalam debate on divine vision. Nasafi argues that if the request itself were inherently disbelief, the Prophet would have rejected it on the spot. Instead, his measured response shows that the request was not kufr, implying the possibility of vision. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Nasafi's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice that Nasafi uses a conditional argument (if...then).
2
Identify the core argument
The core argument is that if requesting vision were disbelief (kufr), Musa would have refuted them immediately. Since he did not, the request was not kufr.
Consider the analogy with the earlier request for an idol.
3
Follow the refutation of the counterargument
Nasafi anticipates an objection: perhaps Musa delayed for greater effect. He refutes it by distinguishing between the disbelievers who asked and the seventy righteous men present.
The seventy would not ask for something kufr, and even if they saw a divine response, it wouldn't convince the disbelievers.

And the second: That if vision were not permissible with respect to Allah the Exalted and were disbelief, then Moses, peace be upon him, would not have delayed refuting them, but would have refuted them at the time their speech struck his ears, and he would not have left them and their belief in what it is not permissible to believe due to what it contains of approval of disbelief. And the prophets, peace be upon them, were sent to change it, not to approve it. So it is more likely that they would not accept the statement of these seventy, especially since they were stripped of divine support and the indication of infallibility from lying was absent in their case. So in requesting what is disbelief in the presence of
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument (والثاني) for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). Nasafi uses a prophetic analogy: if requesting vision were inherently disbelief (kufr), then Prophet Musa (Moses) would have immediately refuted his people upon hearing their request, just as he immediately refuted them earlier when they asked for a physical idol (Quran 7:138). Instead, Musa delayed and asked Allah to show Himself, indicating that the request itself was not kufr. Nasafi then addresses a potential counterargument: perhaps Musa delayed because the people would not accept his words, so he asked Allah to respond directly for greater effect. Nasafi refutes this by noting that the request for vision in the Quran was actually from disbelievers (Quran 2:55), not from the seventy chosen Israelites who were present when Musa asked. The seventy were pious and would not ask for something kufr. Furthermore, if the request had come from disbelievers, the seventy would not have believed them anyway, even after seeing a divine response, because they had already rejected Moses' clear proofs. Thus, the only coherent explanation is that requesting vision is not inherently kufr, and ru'ya is permissible for Allah.

Key Terms
رؤية
تقرير على الكفر
السبعين
موسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act of asking to see Allah is always disbelief (kufr) regardless of context.
Correction: Musa's response shows that the request itself was not inherently kufr; otherwise he would have refuted immediately. The context and the asker's intention matter.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi argue that the request for vision was not inherently kufr?
A) Because the seventy chosen ones asked for it.
B) Because Musa immediately refuted the request.
C) Because if it were kufr, Musa would have refuted them immediately, but he instead asked Allah.
D) Because the request came from disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Musa's delayed response to the request for vision demonstrates that the request itself was not inherently disbelief, thus establishing the rational possibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt about a fundamental belief in your faith, do you immediately reject it like Musa did with the idol request, or do you hesitate, thinking it might be permissible? What would it take for you to respond with the same urgency as a prophet?
?
The seventy chosen ones did not ask for a sign because they already trusted Allah and His messenger. Is there an area of your life where you are demanding a sign from Allah before obeying His command? What would it mean to submit without that sign?
?
Nasafi's argument shows that silence in the face of false belief can be misinterpreted as approval. Are there beliefs or practices in your community that you suspect contradict the Quran, yet you remain silent? Will you speak out, even if it costs you comfort or relationships?

Mu'tazila and Maturidi Arguments on the Cause of Visibility
Mu'tazila argue visibility requires being a body; Maturidi argue it requires being self-subsistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila and Maturidi arguments on the cause of visibility and why the Maturidi view grounds visibility in self-subsistence rather than corporeality.

Core Idea
Visibility is rooted in self-subsistence, not in being a body.

This passage presents a key theological debate on whether God can be seen. Both early Maturidis and Mu'tazila agreed that accidents cannot be seen, but they disagreed on what makes something visible in the witnessed world. The Mu'tazila argued that only bodies are visible, while the Maturidis argued that it is self-subsistence that enables visibility. This distinction protects God's transcendence while affirming the possibility of vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the consensus
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that both groups agreed that accidents (a'rad) cannot be seen.
This consensus is the starting point for the debate.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
Find where the Mu'tazila claim that the cause of visibility is corporeality (jismiyya). They argue that if something is not a body, it cannot be seen, so God (who is not a body) cannot be seen.
They also equate claiming visibility with claiming corporeality.
3
Identify the Maturidi argument
Find where the Maturidis argue that the cause is being self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dhatih). Since God is self-subsistent, it is permissible to see Him.
This shifts the focus from being a body to being an independent entity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the Maturidi view is important: it allows the possibility of seeing God without attributing a body to Him, preserving His transcendence.
The common misunderstanding is that visibility implies corporeality; the Maturidi argument refutes that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: كان أوائلنا وأوائل المعتزلة سبق منهم الإجماع أن الأعراض مستحيلة الرؤية، واختلفوا بعد هذا الإجماع أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية في الشاهد ما هي؟ فزعمت أوائلهم أنها الجسمية فكل ما هو جسم كان مرئيا، وكل ما لم يكن جسما فهو مستحيل الرؤية وقالوا: إن البارئ جل وعلا ليس بجسم فيستحيل رؤيته وزعموا أن كل من قال : إن الله تعالى مرئي فقد زعم إذ لا فرق بين قول جسم القائل هذا جسم وبين قولهم هذا مرئي كما لا فرق بين قولهم: هو عشرة وبين قولهم : هو قدر مائة وأصحابنا - رحمهم الله - قالوا: إن العلة المطلقة للرؤية هي كون القائم بالذات فما كان قائما بالذات يجوز رؤيته، وما لا يكون قائما بالذات يستحيل رؤيته والله تعالى قائم بالذات فكان جائز الرؤية، فمن زعم أن الله تعالى يستحيل رؤيته فقد ألحقه بما لا قيام له بذاته
We say: Our early scholars and the early Mu'tazila had prior consensus that accidents (a'rad) are impossible to see. Then after this consensus they differed: what is the absolute cause of visibility in the witnessed world? Their early scholars claimed it is corporeality (al-jismiyya): so everything that is a body (jism) is visible, and everything that is not a body is impossible to see. They said: The Creator, exalted and majestic, is not a body, so it is impossible to see Him. They claimed that whoever says God is visible has claimed, for there is no difference between someone saying 'this is a body' and their saying 'this is visible', just as there is no difference between their saying 'it is ten' and their saying 'it is one hundred.' And our associates (may God have mercy on them) said: The absolute cause of visibility is being self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dhatih): so whatever is self-subsistent is permissible to see, and whatever is not self-subsistent is impossible to see. God is self-subsistent, so He is permissible to see. Whoever claims that God is impossible to see has likened Him to what has no self-subsistence.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalām argument on the possibility of seeing God. It begins by noting that both early Maturidis and early Mu'tazila agreed that accidents (a'rad, non-substantial properties) cannot be seen. After this consensus, they disagreed on the cause of visibility in the witnessed world. The early Mu'tazila proposed that the cause is being a body (jismiyya): anything that is a body is visible, and anything not a body cannot be seen. Since God is not a body, He cannot be seen. They equated asserting visibility with asserting corporeality. The Maturidi response shifts the cause from corporeality to self-subsistence (qa'im bi-dhatih): whatever exists independently (not inhering in another) can be seen, and whatever does not (i.e., accidents) cannot. Since God is self-subsistent, it is permissible to see Him. Those who deny this wrongly liken God to accidents that lack self-subsistence.

Key Terms
أعراض (a'rad)
جسم (jism)
رؤية (ru'ya)
قائم بالذات (qa'im bi-dhatih)
علة (ʿilla)
الشاهد (al-shāhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Maturidi view makes God similar to bodies by affirming visibility.
Correction: The Maturidi argument explicitly shifts the cause from corporeality to self-subsistence, so visibility does not imply being a body.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi argument in this passage, what is the absolute cause of visibility?
A) Being a body (jism)
B) Being an accident (ʿarad)
C) Being self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dhatih)
D) Being a substance (jawhar)

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi argument shifts the cause of visibility from corporeality to self-subsistence, thereby permitting the vision of God without attributing a body to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly yearn to see your Creator, or have you accepted the Mu'tazila's assumption that He cannot be seen because He is not like physical bodies? What in your life shows whether you long for that vision?
?
The Mu'tazila used an analogy equating saying 'this is a body' with 'this is visible'. What false analogies have you made about God, reducing His attributes to created things? How will you guard against that today?
?
The Maturidi grounded visibility in God's self-subsistence. How does your understanding of God's independence (qā'im bi-dhātih) affect your reliance on Him versus created means? What are you clinging to that you think supports you, rather than God Himself?

Existence as the Cause of Vision: The Consensus Argument
Nasafi states the absolute cause and cites consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the consensus of early theologians to establish existence as the cause of visibility.

Core Idea
Consensus among kalam theologians shows existence is the cause of vision.

Nasafi argues that the absolute cause of visibility in the sensible world is simply existence. To support this, he first points to a consensus among leading theologians, including al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and al-Nazzam, that at least some accidents are visible. This agreement provides a foundation for his claim without needing to prove each case individually.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the claim that existence is the cause.
2
Identify the scholars cited
Notice which scholars agreed or disagreed on specific types of accidents.
al-Jubba'i agreed on all three genera; his son disagreed on modalities; al-Nazzam on colors as bodies.
3
Understand the consensus argument
See how Nasafi uses their partial agreement as sufficient evidence without needing further proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه ادعى الأعراض – ذهبوا إلى أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية في الشاهد المجوزة لها، الوجود. وأن كل موجود رؤيته ممكنة جائزة ودليل ذلك أنا نرى الجوهر والألوان والأكوان وهي الحركة والسكون والقرب والبعد والاجتماع والافتراق، وقد وافقنا الجبائي، على أن هذه الأجناس الثلاثة مرئية وابنه أبو هاشم وافقنا على رؤية الألوان، وخالفنا في رؤية الأكوان والنظام وافقنا على رؤية الألوان غير أنها أجسام ، ولقد أقمنا الدلائل على كونها أعراض، فإجماع هؤلاء أغنانا عن إقامة الدليل على رؤية بعض الأعراض، وكونها جائزة الرؤية ثم الدليل مع هذا على رؤية الأعراض التمييز بالبصر بين الأسود والأبيض وبين المجتمعين والمفترقين من غير قيام السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق بآلة الرؤية وهو البصر، ولم يعرف كون الجواهر مرئية إلا بالتمييز بالبصر بين أجناسها وحصول العلم بوجودها، وهذا المعنى بعينه في حق الأكوان والألوان موجود فكانت مرئية، ولو لم تكن مرئية لعرف بالرؤية وجود الجواهر ولكان لا يوقف على كونه متحركا أو ساكنا أو مجتمعا أو متفرقا كما لا يوقف على الطعوم بمجرد الرؤية لانعدام تعلقها بالطعوم على ما أجرى الله تعالى العادة، وإن كانت مرئية عندنا لكونها موجودة على ما نبين بعد هذا - إن شاء الله تعالى.
Its claim about accidents – they held that the absolute cause for vision in the witnessed world that permits it is existence. And that every existent's vision is possible and permissible. Evidence for that is that we see substance (jawhar), colors (alwān), and modalities (akwān) which are movement, stillness, proximity, distance, union, and separation. And al-Jubba'i agreed with us that these three genera are visible; his son Abu Hashim agreed with us on the visibility of colors but disagreed with us on the visibility of modalities; and al-Nazzam agreed with us on the visibility of colors but [held] that they are bodies. We have indeed established proofs that they are accidents. So the consensus (ijmā') of these [scholars] suffices us from providing a proof for the visibility of some accidents and their being permissible of vision. Then the proof for the visibility of accidents is the discrimination by sight between black and white, and between things that are together and separated, without the blackness, whiteness, union, and separation subsisting in the organ of vision, which is sight. And the visibility of substances is known only through discrimination by sight between their genera and the acquisition of knowledge of their existence. This same meaning in the case of modalities and colors is present, so they are visible. And if they were not visible, the existence of substances would not be known by sight, and one would not ascertain whether something is moving or still, or together or separated, just as one cannot ascertain tastes by mere sight due to the lack of connection of tastes to it according to what God Almighty has established as custom. And they are visible to us because they are existent, as we will clarify after this, God willing.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-proof passage: it defines the absolute cause (illa) of vision as existence (wujud). The author first states the claim, then provides two lines of evidence: (1) consensus (ijma') of al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and al-Nazzam that certain things are visible, even though they differ on specifics; (2) a direct proof from sensory discrimination by sight. The argument proceeds by observing that we visually distinguish between colors and spatial relations (modalities) without those qualities inhering in the eye. The same holds for substances: we distinguish between their genera by sight, thus obtaining knowledge of their existence. If substances were not visible, we could not discern modalities like movement by sight, which contradicts experience. Therefore, the common factor making all these things visible is their existence, not any other attribute. This establishes the foundation for the subsequent search for the precise cause.

Key Terms
الوجود
الرؤية
العلة
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which scholar agreed that substances, colors, and modalities are all visible?
A) Abu Hashim
B) al-Jubba'i
C) al-Nazzam
D) Nasafi

Key Takeaway
Nasafi uses the partial consensus of theologians to argue that existence, not type, is the basis for visibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence itself is the cause of vision, what does that mean for your perception of Allah? Are you truly aware that His mere existence demands that He be seen? Yet you have not seen Him—what does that say about your relationship with your Creator?
?
Nasafi uses consensus to establish a principle. In your own life, do you rely on the consensus of scholars to shape your beliefs about Allah, or do you let your own assumptions override their agreement? Where might you be resisting the collective understanding of Ahl al-Sunnah?
?
The lesson argues that every existent is potentially visible. One day you will see Allah. How does that certainty affect your actions today? Are you living as if you will meet Him face to face?

Existence as the Cause of Vision: The Proof from Sensory Distinction
Nasafi presents a direct sensory proof for existence as the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's direct argument that sensory discrimination by sight proves existence is the cause of visibility.

Core Idea
We visually distinguish colors and spatial relations; this proves existence is the common cause.

After establishing a consensus foundation, Nasafi offers a direct proof from everyday visual experience. He argues that our ability to distinguish between black and white, or between objects that are together or separated, without those qualities inhering in the eye itself, shows that what we see is their existence. This same reasoning applies to substances and modalities, proving that existence is the universal cause of visibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the lines beginning with 'Then the proof for the visibility of accidents'.
2
Follow the sensory argument
Understand that discrimination by sight occurs without the qualities being in the eye.
Blackness and whiteness are not in the eye itself.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Noting that if substances were not visible, we couldn't discern movement, etc.
4
Appreciate the logic
The argument mirrors that of distinguishing substances by sight.

Its existence points to a greater reality: that God sustains it. When was the last time you looked at something mundane and saw it as a sign of Allah's existence? Refuting the Argument from 'Mutakawwin' for Divine Invisibility
Refutes the claim that God is invisible because He is mutakawwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God cannot be seen because He is mutakawwin (originated) is logically unsound.

Core Idea
The term 'mutakawwin' is ambiguous and fails to block God's visibility.

In this passage, al-Nasafi addresses an objection raised by those who argue that God cannot be seen because He is 'mutakawwin' (originated). He carefully unpacks the meaning of the term and shows that, no matter how it is interpreted, it does not support the conclusion of invisibility. This refutation highlights the importance of precise terminology in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two interpretations of 'mutakawwin'.
2
Analyze the two meanings
Identify the two possible meanings of 'mutakawwin' given by al-Nasafi and see why each fails to prove invisibility.
The author uses a logical analysis: definitional ambiguity.
3
Examine the example of the atom
Consider the example of a single indivisible atom. Reflect on why it would be visible even though it is not mutakawwin in the relevant sense.
This shows that even without the accident of kawun, visibility is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه متكونا لأن المتكون إذا أريد به الموجود فهو ما تدعيه وإن أريد به ما قام به عرض يسمى كونا فهو فاسد ، لأن اللون والكون مرئيان، وكذا لو خلق الله تعلى في ابتداء خلقه العالم جوهرا واحدا لكان مرئيا على ما قررنا قبل هذا من وجوب كون الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ مرئيا، ولم يكن متكونا حينئذ.
And it is not permissible that He be seen because He is mutakawwin (originated), because al-mutakawwin, if what is meant by it is al-mawjud (the existent), then it is what you claim; and if what is meant by it is that which has an accident called kawun, then it is invalid, because color and kawun are visible. Likewise, if Allah the Exalted, at the beginning of His creation of the world, created a single atom, it would be visible according to what we established before this regarding the necessity of the indivisible part being visible, and it would not be mutakawwin then.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The opponents argue that God cannot be seen because He is 'mutakawwin' (originated). The author (al-Nasafi) clarifies the term: if 'mutakawwin' means 'existent', then it is conceded that God is existent, and that does not preclude visibility. If it means 'possessing an accident called kawun', this is false because colors and 'kawun' themselves are visible—they are accidents that are seen. The author gives an example: even a single indivisible atom would be visible, and it is not 'mutakawwin' in the sense of having that accident. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
mutakawwin
mawjud
kawun
juz' alladhi la yatajazzā

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that if something is 'mutakawwin' (originated), it cannot be seen.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that 'mutakawwin' has two possible meanings, neither of which precludes visibility. The term doesn't automatically block vision.

Checkpoint
What is the flaw in using 'mutakawwin' as a reason for God's invisibility?
A) It is not a divine attribute.
B) The term is ambiguous; if it means 'existent' it does not negate visibility, and if it means 'having an accident called kawun', that accident is itself visible.
C) 'Mutakawwin' applies only to bodies.
D) The Quran denies that God is mutakawwin.

Key Takeaway
The term 'mutakawwin' is ambiguous and cannot be used to argue for divine invisibility.

Refutation of Knownness and Mentionedness as Reasons for Invisibility
Refutes the claim that God is invisible because He is known and mentioned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why being known or mentioned does not entail invisibility, and how the author uses logical transitivity to refute the objection.

Core Idea
Being known does not make God invisible; even non-existents are known and mentioned.

The author continues to dismantle objections against the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter. Here, he addresses the argument that God cannot be seen because He is 'known' and 'mentioned' — attributes that, in worldly experience, might be associated with non-existence. Let's see how he turns this argument on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Locate the claim that God is not visible because He is ma'lum (known) and madhkur (mentioned). Understand this as an objection to the possibility of seeing God.
The opponent assumes that being known implies invisibility.
3
Understand the first counterargument
The author points out that non-existent things (ma'dum) are also known and mentioned but are not visible. This shows that being known does not cause invisibility.
The non-existent is a clear counterexample.
4
Notice the second counterargument using transitivity
The author uses a hypothetical: if the opponents claim that in the witnessed world things are seen because they are known, then by the principle of ta'diya (transitivity) this would apply to God, forcing them to admit God's visibility.
This exposes inconsistency in their reasoning.
5
Reflect on the logical principle
Consider how ta'diya is used in kalam to apply a ruling from the witnessed to the unseen when the cause is shared. Here, the author shows the opponents' own logic backfires.
This is a common argumentative technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه معلوما أو مذكورا لأن المعدوم معلوم ومذكور وليس بمرئي، على أن الخصوم لو ادعوا أن الشاهد يرى لكونه معلوما أو مذكورا للزمتهم التعدية لكونه تعالى معلوما مذكورا .
And it is not permissible that He be seen because He is ma'lum (known) or madhkur (mentioned), because the non-existent (ma'dum) is known and mentioned but is not visible. Moreover, if the opponents claimed that the witnessed thing (shahid) is seen because it is known or mentioned, then the transitive argument (ta'diya) would be incumbent upon them for the Exalted being known and mentioned.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage refutes another objection. The opponents say God cannot be seen because He is known and mentioned, like the non-existent which is known and mentioned but not visible. The author points out that non-existent things are indeed known and mentioned yet not visible, so being known does not imply invisibility. Moreover, if the opponents were to argue that in the witnessed realm (shahid) things are seen because they are known, then by the principle of transitivity (ta'diya), God would also be seen since He is known. This exposes inconsistency in their reasoning.

Key Terms
ma'lum
madhkur
ma'dum
ta'diya
shahid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being known and mentioned implies non-existence or invisibility, so God (who is known) cannot be seen.
Correction: Non-existent things are also known and mentioned but are not visible, so being known does not entail invisibility. Moreover, the opponents' own logic would require them to admit God's visibility.

Checkpoint
What is the author's counterargument regarding 'ma'lum' and 'madhkur'?
A) God is not really known or mentioned.
B) Non-existent things are known and mentioned but not visible, so these terms do not prevent visibility; also, the opponents' logic would require them to admit God's visibility.
C) The terms apply only to contingent things.
D) The Quran says God is invisible.

Key Takeaway
Being known or mentioned does not prevent visibility; these attributes apply even to non-existent things, and applying the opponents' logic consistently leads to the conclusion that God is visible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You probably recite God's names and speak of Him often. But when was the last time you felt a genuine longing to see Him, not just to know about Him? Does your mental image of God stop at 'known and mentioned', or do you expect more?
?
The non-existent is known and mentioned but not seen. Are there aspects of your faith that you merely 'know' and 'mention' without any real encounter? What would you do this week to move from knowing about God to truly seeking His vision?
?
The opponents used God's being known as an excuse to avoid expecting to see Him. What excuses—busyness, intellectualism, distractions—do you use to avoid a direct, personal experience with the Divine? Confess one excuse you will abandon starting today.

Refuting Temporality as a Barrier to Seeing God
Refutes the claim that God is invisible because He is temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that God cannot be seen because He is temporal is inconsistent and how a Maturidi theologian refutes it.

Core Idea
Temporality alone does not preclude visibility, as shown by theologian disagreements.

The objection that God cannot be seen because He is temporal seems plausible at first glance. However, a closer look reveals that theologians themselves disagree on which temporal things are invisible. This lesson examines the refutation of this objection, focusing on the key insight about atoms and their state of remaining.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that because God is temporal (muhdath), He cannot be seen. This rests on the premise that all temporal things are invisible.
This premise is not universally accepted.
2
Survey the diversity of opinions
Early authorities, al-Jubbā'ī, Abu Hāshim, and al-Nazzām each have different views on which temporal things are invisible. This variety shows the premise is not clear-cut.
3
Grasp the author's refutation
The author points out that atoms (jawāhir) are seen while remaining (baqā'), yet in that state they are not truly temporal. This undermines the original premise.
Al-Nazzām's exception is specifically rejected as invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه محدثا لأن المحدثات عندهم ما يستحيل رؤيته، لأن عند أوائلهم ما سوى الجسم من الموجودات مستحيل الرؤية وهي محدثة، وعند الجبائي ما وراء الألوان والأكوان من الأعراض يستحيل رؤيتها وإن كانت محدثة وكذا الحكم عند أبي هاشم فيما وراء الألوان من الأعراض، وكذا عند النظام الحركات والأصوات يستحيل رؤيتها وهي محدثة على الجواهر ترى في حالة البقاء وهي في حالة البقاء ليست بمحدثة حقيقة إذ المحدث ما يتعلق به الإحداث وهو يتعلق بها في أول أحوال وجودها لا في حالة البقاء إلا عند النظام خاصة وهو فاسد لاستحالة تعلق الإحداث بموجود انقضى أو أن حدوثه إذ إحداث الموجود وإيجاده محال
And it is not permissible that He be seen because He is muhdath (temporal), because temporal things, according to them, are those whose vision is impossible. Since, according to their early authorities, everything other than body among existing things is impossible to see, and they are temporal. According to al-Jubbā'ī, attributes beyond colors and akwān are impossible to see, even though they are temporal. Likewise, the ruling according to Abu Hāshim regarding attributes beyond colors. Similarly, according to al-Nazzām, motions and sounds are impossible to see, and they are temporal. Yet atoms (jawāhir) are seen in a state of remaining (baqā'), and in that state they are not truly temporal, because the temporal is that to which 'being brought into existence' (ihdāth) attaches, and it attaches to them in the first moment of their existence, not in the state of remaining, except according to al-Nazzām specifically. And this is invalid because it is impossible for 'bringing into existence' to attach to an existent that has passed or to its coming-to-be, since bringing the existent into existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that God cannot be seen because He is 'muhdath' (temporal). The author first notes that the early authorities considered everything except body as invisible and temporal, but this is not universally accepted. He mentions al-Jubbā'ī, Abu Hāshim, and al-Nazzām, who have different views on which temporal things are invisible. Then he points out that atoms (jawāhir) are seen in their state of remaining (baqā'), and in that state they are not truly temporal because 'ihdāth' (bringing into existence) attaches only at the first moment. Al-Nazzām's exception is rejected because it is impossible for 'ihdāth' to apply to an already existing thing. Thus the premise that all temporal things are invisible is not consistently held.

Key Terms
muhdath
ihdāth
jawāhir
a'rād
baqā'
al-Jubbā'ī
Abu Hāshim
al-Nazzām

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All temporal things are invisible.
Correction: Theologians disagree on which temporal things are invisible, and atoms are seen while in a state of remaining, which is not truly temporal.

Checkpoint
What is the author's point about atoms (jawāhir) in relation to temporality?
A) Atoms are never visible.
B) Atoms are seen in a state of remaining, and in that state they are not truly temporal.
C) Atoms are temporal and therefore invisible.
D) Atoms are the only visible temporal things.

Key Takeaway
The argument that God cannot be seen because He is temporal fails because even within theologians' own frameworks, some temporal things are visible, such as atoms during their state of remaining.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological argument simply because it seemed logically sound, without checking whether its premises hold universally? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of assuming a premise is always true?
?
The author uses the example of atoms being seen during their state of remaining to show that temporality is not necessarily a barrier to visibility. Where in your own life do you treat temporary conditions as permanent obstacles, forgetting that God's power transcends such categories?
?
If you truly believe that God can be seen in the afterlife despite His transcendence, how should this affect the way you seek nearness to Him now? Are you living with the hope of that vision, or have you let abstract arguments dull your longing?

Permanence Does Not Preclude Visibility
Refutes permanence as a hindrance to vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the property of being permanent (bāqī) does not logically preclude visibility, and how the visibility of colors and accidents supports this refutation.

Core Idea
Permanence does not cause invisibility.

Some argue that God cannot be seen because He is permanent (bāqī), while changing things can be seen. Sanusi refutes this by showing that even according to their own reasoning, permanent things in our experience are visible, so permanence cannot be the reason for invisibility. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the main claim
The objector claims that permanence prevents visibility. The author refutes this by showing that if permanence were the cause, then by transitivity God would be invisible (since He is permanent), but we have proven visibility of things that are permanent in some sense.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the counter-example
The author points out that colors and akwān (states/modes) are visible, yet they are accidents (a'rād) which are not permanent according to him. Even if some claim they are permanent, the discussion shifts to that issue.
This shows that permanence is irrelevant to visibility.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why the author brings up the Mu'tazila's potential claim of permanence of accidents. He uses their own framework to show inconsistency, but for now, focus on the core logical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جائز أن يرى لكونه باقيا لأنها لو تعلقت بالباقي ثبت ما ادعيناه وأمكنت التعدية إلى الغائب لأن الله تعالى باق على أن أثبتنا بالدليل رؤية الألوان والأكوان وهي أعراض، ويستحيل بقاؤها عندنا، فإن ادعت المعتزلة بقاءها ننقل الكلام إلى تلك المسألة، على أن عند النظام الأصوات باقية ويستحيل رؤيتها عنده، وأجاز الجبائي بقاء الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والحياة والقدرة والعجز والعلم والاعتقاد والكلام، وليست هذه الأشياء بمرئية عنده، وكذا ابنه يقول ببقاء الأعراض التي أجاز أبوه بقاءها إلا الكلام خاصة، ولا يجوز رؤية شيء جميع منها، على أن كل جنس يرى عندهم يكون جائز الرؤية في أول أحوال وجوده بلا خلاف بين العقلاء فضلا عن المتكلمين، لأن ما يستحيل رؤيته لذاته لا يختلف باختلاف الأزمنة، وهي في تلك الحالة ليست بموصوفة بالبقاء إلا عند عباد والكرامية وهو فاسد لصحة قول القائل وجد ولم يبق عند جميع أهل اللسان.
And it is not permissible that He be seen because He is bāqī (permanent). Because if they attach [the rule] to the permanent, what we have claimed would be established and the transitive argument to the absent (ghā'ib) would be possible, since Allah the Exalted is permanent. However, we have proven by evidence the vision of colors and akwān, and they are accidents, and their permanence is impossible according to us. If the Mu'tazila claim their permanence, we transfer the discussion to that issue. Moreover, according to al-Nazzām, sounds are permanent and vision of them is impossible according to him. Al-Jubbā'ī allowed the permanence of heat, cold, moisture, dryness, life, power, incapacity, knowledge, belief, and speech, and these things are not visible according to him. Likewise, his son [Abu Hāshim] says regarding the permanence of the accidents that his father allowed permanence of, except for speech specifically, and vision of none of them is permissible. Moreover, every genus that is seen according to them is permissible of vision in the first state of its existence, without disagreement among the rational people, let alone theologians. Because what is impossible to see by itself does not differ with difference of times. And those [accidents] in that state are not described by permanence except according to 'Abbād and the Karāmiyya, and this is invalid because of the soundness of the statement of the speaker: 'it existed and did not remain' according to all people of the language.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that God cannot be seen because He is bāqī (permanent). The author argues that if permanence prevented visibility, then by transitivity God would be invisible, but we have proven that colors and akwān are visible despite being commonly considered permanent by some. He then notes that various Mu'tazilī scholars (al-Jubbā'ī, Abu Hāshim, al-Nazzām) allow permanence of certain accidents but still deny their visibility, showing that permanence is not the cause of invisibility. Furthermore, everything that is seen is permissible of vision in its first state of existence, before permanence applies, and what is intrinsically invisible does not change with time. The objection is also linguistically invalid because 'existed' does not imply 'remained', as shown by common usage.

Key Terms
bāqī
baqā'
ghā'ib
a'rād

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Permanence (baqā') inherently prevents visibility.
Correction: The author shows that permanence does not preclude visibility, as many permanent things (e.g., colors in some views) are visible.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument the author uses to refute the idea that permanence prevents visibility?
A) Permanence is a positive attribute of God, so it cannot be a hindrance.
B) If permanence caused invisibility, then by transitivity God would be invisible, but we also have visible things that are permanent.
C) The Mu'tazila agree that accidents are not permanent.
D) The author uses a direct quote from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Permanence (baqā') is not a valid reason to deny the vision of Allah, as visible things in our experience can be permanent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila believed that because God is permanent, He cannot be seen—a seemingly logical conclusion. In your own life, when has a 'logical' assumption caused you to doubt a clear truth of the Qur'an and Sunnah? What will you do today to re-align your reasoning with revelation?
?
This lesson shows that a single flawed premise can unravel an entire argument. Examine your own heart: are there any premises you hold about Allah's attributes that actually contradict what He has told you about Himself? Confess one such premise and commit to replacing it with the truth from this passage.
?
The author does not merely reject the Mu'tazila argument but points to a deeper issue: the misuse of analogy in matters of the unseen. Are you guilty of comparing Allah to His creation in your mind, perhaps subtly? How will you guard against this from today onward?

Internal Inconsistencies Among Mu'tazila
Arguments from Mu'tazilī disagreements and language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the disagreements among Mu'tazilī scholars about the permanence of accidents to show that permanence is not the cause of invisibility.

Core Idea
Even Mu'tazila who accept permanence deny visibility of those accidents.

The author deepens his refutation by pointing out internal contradictions among the Mu'tazila themselves. Even those who claim that certain accidents are permanent do not consider them visible. This shows that permanence is not the factor determining visibility. He also adds a linguistic argument from common usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the second passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. This passage lists various Mu'tazilī scholars and their views on permanence and visibility.
Pay attention to the names: al-Nazzām, al-Jubbā'ī, Abu Hāshim.
2
Identify the key examples
Note each scholar and what accidents they consider permanent. For instance, al-Jubbā'ī allows heat, cold, life, etc. Despite that, none of these are considered visible by them.
3
Understand the logical point
The author argues that if permanence caused invisibility, then those who accept permanence of accidents should also accept their visibility. But they don't, so permanence is not the cause.
This is a classic internal critique.
4
Examine the linguistic argument
The author adds that every visible thing is potentially visible in its first state, and what is intrinsically invisible does not change with time. Also, Arabic usage shows 'existed' does not imply 'remained'.
This strengthens the refutation.

The Cause of Visibility Is Existence
Concludes existence as cause of visibility, answers objection on accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is the cause of visibility for God and how the objection that accidents are not directly visible is resolved in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
We see substances, not accidents; accidents are inferred rationally.

This passage ties together the rational argument that existence makes vision possible, then addresses a common objection: if accidents are not directly visible, how can we claim vision of God? The author clarifies that vision is of the substance, while accidents are known through evidence, just as touch perceives objects, not qualities in isolation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author states the conclusion first, then handles an objection.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument continues, but the core point is clear.
2
Identify the cause of visibility
The passage explicitly states that 'the cause in vision is existence'. Understand that this means any existent being is in principle visible, including God.
This is a rational principle, not derived from scripture here.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says: our early authorities did not concede that any accident (like color, motion) is visible. So how can you claim we see God if vision involves accidents?
4
Analyze the response
The author argues that we do not see accidents directly. When we see a moving body, we see the body (substance) and infer motion. Similarly, we see a black object, not blackness itself. The senses perceive substances, not accidents.
The example of touch (hot/cold) reinforces this: we feel the object, not the quality alone.
5
Connect to the larger argument
This passage supports the view that seeing God in the hereafter is possible because He exists; the objection about accidents does not undermine this, as we do not need to see accidents to see God.
This resolves a potential inconsistency in kalam discussions of vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة في الرؤية الوجود: وإذا اندفعت هذه الوجوه تبين أن جواز الرؤية كان متعلقا بالوجود والله تعالى موجود فكان جائز الرؤية، ثم عرفنا ثبوت الرؤية للمسلمين في دار الكرامة بما مر من الدلائل السمعية، فإن قيل: رؤية شيء من الأعراض غير مسلم من أوائلنا فلم قلتم ذلك؟ قولكم: إن التمييز يقع بالبصر بين الساكن والمتحرك والمجتمع والمفترق والأسود والأبيض، كما يقع بين جنس جوهر وجنس آخر فقول بهذا لا يتبين أن السواد والبياض مرئيان، وكذا الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، بل المرئي هو المتحرك والساكن والأسود والأبيض والمجتمع والمفترق لا هذه الأعراض بدليل أن نفاة الأعراض ينكرون وجودها، مع أنهم أرباب الحواس السليمة على ما مر، غير أن المتحرك إذا رؤى عرف وجود الحركة بالدليل، وكذا السواد والبياض، هذا كما يقول إن التمييز يقع بحاسة اللمس بين الحار والبارد والخشن والأملس
The cause (al-'illa) in vision is existence (al-wujūd). And when these ways are repelled, it becomes clear that the permissibility of vision was connected to existence, and Allah the Exalted is existent, so He is permissible to see. Then we know the affirmation of vision for the Muslims in the abode of honor (dār al-karāma) through what has passed of scriptural evidences. If it is said: 'Vision of anything from among the accidents is not conceded by our early authorities, so why do you say that?' [We say:] Your statement that discrimination occurs by sight between the stationary and the moving, the gathered and the separated, the black and the white, just as it occurs between a genus of substance and another genus, then by this it is not established that blackness and whiteness are visible, nor motion and rest, gathering and separation. Rather, what is visible is the moving, the stationary, the black, the white, the gathered, and the separated, not these accidents. The evidence is that the deniers of accidents deny their existence, while they are possessors of sound senses as has passed. However, when the moving is seen, the existence of motion is known by evidence, and similarly blackness and whiteness. This is just as one says that discrimination occurs by the sense of touch between hot and cold, rough and smooth.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion followed by an objection-and-answer. The author states that the true cause (illa) for the possibility of vision is existence (wujud). Since God exists, He is possible to see, and scriptural evidence confirms that Muslims will see Him in the Hereafter. Then an objection is raised: our early authorities did not concede that any accident is visible, so how can you claim that? The answer distinguishes between direct and indirect vision. When we see a moving body, we do not see motion itself; we see the moving body and infer motion by evidence (dalil). Similarly, we see a black object, not blackness as a separate entity. This is supported by the fact that deniers of accidents (like naturalists) have sound senses but deny the existence of accidents altogether, showing that the senses perceive substances, not accidents. The example of touch (hot/cold) illustrates the same principle: we feel the object, not the quality in isolation.

Key Terms
al-'illa
al-wujūd
ru'ya
dār al-karāma
a'rād
dalīl
hāssa al-basar
hāssa al-lams

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think we directly perceive accidents like color and motion when we see objects.
Correction: The author argues that we perceive the substance (the moving body, the black object) and infer accidents through evidence. Accidents are not directly visible.

Checkpoint
What is the cause (illa) for the possibility of seeing God according to this passage?
A) God's attributes.
B) God's existence (wujud).
C) God's permanence (baqa').
D) God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Existence is the cause of visibility: we see substances directly and infer accidents rationally, so God's existence makes Him visible without requiring direct perception of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that you will see Allah in the Hereafter. But does your life reflect the reality that He is already 'seeing' you every moment? How would your actions change if you truly felt the weight of His perpetual gaze—not as a threat, but as the reason you are permitted to exist?
?
This lesson teaches that we infer the existence of accidents like motion from what we see. In your daily life, do you infer Allah's attributes from His creation, or do you live as if He is absent until you get 'proof'? What would it look like to treat every movement and stillness as a sign pointing directly to the One who causes visibility by His existence?
?
If the cause of visibility is existence itself, then your very existence is a call to be seen—first by Allah, then by others as a witness. Are you hiding parts of your life from His sight, as if you could conceal a substance from its own cause? What would it cost you to live completely transparent before the One whose existence makes vision possible?
