Refutation of the Claim That God Must Be Colored if Visible
Refutes the claim that visibility implies color by analogy with motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why visibility does not necessitate color, using the example of motion and rest, and how this relates to divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Visibility does not require color; motion and rest are visible but not colored.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes the argument that if God is visible, He must have a color. He uses a careful analysis of tangibility and the distinction between sensory and intellectual knowledge. By examining the properties of motion and rest, he shows that visibility is not exclusive to color.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then its translation. Get a sense of the overall argument.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the two refutation moves
The passage first addresses a tangibility objection, then directly tackles the color-visibility link. Look for the transition phrase 'then upon stopping at this true statement'.
3
Examine the analogy with motion and rest
The author points out that motion, rest, aggregation, and separation are visible yet not colors. This is the key analogy.
Consider what properties these have in common with color.
4
Understand the role of intellect
The argument also shows that God is known by intellect through signs, not by direct sensation, so visibility does not imply color.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
If visibility entailed color, then motion would be a color—an absurdity. Thus the claim is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكون ممسوسة ؟ فإن عنيتم أنها تمس فتكون ممسوسة كما تكون مرئية فهذا باطل إذ غير الجسم لا يمس، لاستحالة قيام المماسة بالأعراض، ولابد من المس من قيام المماسة بكل واحد من المتماسين. فإن قالوا: لا تصير ممسوسة ولكنها تصير معلومة بالحس بمس الجسم الذي هو محلها. قلنا ما لا يدخل تحت الحس لا يصير معلوما بالحس بل يصير معلوما بالعقل بواسطة الحس فكان العلم الحاصل بالاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون بالعقل بواسطة الحس إذ لم تصر هذه الأشياء ممسوسة كالعلم بالحرارة والبرودة والملامسة والخشونة والصلابة والرخاوة، وعند الرؤية تصير هذه الأشياء أعني الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق مرئية على ما قررنا. ثم بالوقوف على هذه الجملة الحقيقية. يقال لهم : إن عنيتم أن اللون لا يدرك إلا بالبصر ولا يحس بشيء من الحواس الأخر، فكذا الحكم في الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق فإذا لا اختصاص للون. وإن عنيتم أنه لا طريقة لمعرفته إلا الرؤية والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق لمعرفتها طريق آخر سوى الرؤية، قلنا : ذات الله تعالى تعرف بالعقل بواسطة مشاهدة الآيات الدالة عليه، فإن لم يجب تكون الأكوان وهي الحركة والسكون مرئية أن تكون لونا لما أنها تعرف بطريق آخر في الجملة في الحس، فلا يجب كون الباري جل وعلا لونا وإن كان مرئيا لأنه يعلم بطريق آخر فإن وجب وكونه تعالى لونا لكونه مرئيا، وإن كان لمعرفته طريق آخر سوى الرؤية، لوجب كون الأكوان ألوانا لكونها مرئية وإن كان لمعرفتها طريق آخر، والقول به خروج عن المعارف، فكذا هذا.
Is it touched? If you mean that it is touched so it becomes touched just as it becomes visible, this is false because other than a body is not touched, due to the impossibility of contact (al-mumāssa) attaching to accidents, and contact necessarily requires the attachment of contact to each of the two touching things. If they say: It does not become touched, but it becomes known by sense through touching the body which is its locus. We say: What does not enter under sense does not become known by sense, but becomes known by the intellect via sense; thus the knowledge obtained of aggregation, separation, motion, and rest is by the intellect via sense, since these things are not touched, like knowledge of heat, cold, contact, roughness, hardness, and softness. And upon vision, these things—namely motion, rest, aggregation, separation—become visible as we have established. Then upon stopping at this true statement, it is said to them: If you mean that color is not perceived except by sight and is not sensed by any of the other senses, then the same judgment applies to motion, rest, aggregation, and separation; thus color has no exclusivity. And if you mean that there is no way to know it except vision, while motion, rest, aggregation, and separation have another way besides vision, we say: The essence of God, exalted, is known by the intellect through observation of the signs indicating Him. Therefore, if it were not necessary that the accidents (al-akwān)—motion and rest—being visible makes them colors because they are known by another way in general in sense, then it is not necessary that the Creator, mighty and majestic, be a color even if He is visible, because He is known by another way. For if it were necessary that He be a color because He is visible, even though He has another way of knowledge besides vision, then it would be necessary that the accidents be colors because they are visible even though they have another way of knowledge, and holding this is a departure from knowledge. So likewise this.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (radd) of the claim that if God is visible, He must be colored. The author proceeds in two main moves. First, he addresses a prior objection about tangibility: the opponent suggests that something non-corporeal can become known by sense through touching the body it inheres in. The author replies that what is not itself sensible becomes known by the intellect via sense, not by direct sensation—just as motion and rest are understood intellectually even though they are not tangible. Second, he applies this logic to color and visibility. He argues that color has no exclusive claim to visibility, because motion, rest, etc., are also visible yet clearly not colors. Moreover, God is known by intellect through signs, so His visibility does not entail that He is a color. If the opponent insists that visibility necessitates color, then by the same logic, motion and rest would have to be colors—a manifest absurdity. Thus the argument stands refuted.

افتراق (iftirāq)
لون (lawn)
رؤية (ruʾya)
حس (ḥiss)
عقل (ʿaql)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is visible, it must have a color.
Correction: Motion and rest are visible but not colors; visibility is not exclusive to colored objects.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Visibility does not necessarily entail color; attributes like motion and rest are visible but not colors, and God's visibility is of a different order, known through intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What other assumptions about Allah do you hold that might be based on your limited senses, and how can you correct them using reason and revelation?
?
The argument uses analogies from motion and rest to clarify a subtle point. In your own life, where do you rely on faulty analogies to understand Allah? Will you commit to studying the attributes of Allah through the intellect and signs rather than through your imagination?
?
If Allah is known by intellect through signs, then your intellectual effort to understand Him is a form of worship. Are you actively striving to know Allah through His signs and the arguments of the scholars, or do you settle for a vague, comfortable belief? What one step will you take this week to deepen your intellectual understanding of Allah's transcendence?

Visibility as a Judgment of Existence
Proof that visibility is a judgment of existence, not color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi theologian proves that God's visibility is due to His existence, not to being a colored substance.

Core Idea
Visibility belongs to existence, not to color.

This passage presents a rational proof that visibility is a judgment of existence, not of color. The author shows that both God and color are visible because they exist, and that defining color by exclusive visual perception leads to absurdities. This argument defends God's transcendence while affirming that He can be seen by the righteous in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the dense logical structure and the key terms.
The passage uses a dialectical style: it first states the main thesis, then refutes an objection.
2
Identify the main thesis
The author claims that visibility is a judgment (ḥukm) of existence (wujūd), not of color. Both God and color are visible because they exist.
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes the idea that color's visibility is due to its not being perceived by other senses. He argues that attaching a positive judgment to a negative attribute is invalid.
This refutation uses the absurdity that if it were true, abstract attributes like knowledge would be colors.
4
Follow the conclusion
The author concludes that color is not defined by exclusive visual perception, and that God's visibility does not imply He is colored. Three proofs are combined to support this.
These proofs are: visibility follows existence; God is not perceptible by other senses; God is not a color.
5
Reflect on the significance
This argument is crucial for defending the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife while maintaining His transcendence above material attributes.
It shows that visibility does not require color or shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: إنا بينا بالدليل أن كون الذات مرئيا حكم كونه موجودا، والباري موجود فكان مرئيا، واللون موجود فكان كونه مرئيا حكما لكونه موجودا، فكان مرئيا لكونه موجودا لا لكونه لونا، فدل بكونه مرئيا على مطلق الوجود إذ الحكم يدل على علته لا على موجود مقيد، ولا دلالة في كونه مرئيا إلا على مطلق الوجود، فلو كان لونا لكان لا لأنه مرئي بل لأنه لا يدرك بالحواس الآخر، فيكون لكونه لونا حكما لكونه غير مدرك بالحواس الأربع الأخر سوى البصر، وهذا فاسد، لأن هذا تعليق الحكم وهو كونه لونا بالعدم وهو كونه غير مدرك بتلك الحواس وذا باطل، ولأنه يقتضي أن يكون العلوم والقدر والإرادات كلها ألوانا لأنها لا تدرك بشيء من الحواس الأربع، فكانت مساوية لها في علة كونها ألوانا وهي كونها غير مدركة بالحواس الأربع، فكانت مساوية لها في الحكم وهذا باطل. ولأنا بينا بالدليل أن كونه جائز الرؤية من حكم الوجود وهو موجود وأثبتنا بالدليل أن الذوق والشم واللمس والسمع مستحيل على ذاته، وأثبتنا بالدليل أنه ليس بلون، فكان من مقتضى مجموع هذه الدلائل أن اللون ليس ما يختص إدراكه بالرؤية لقيام الدليل على ذات مختص بكونه مرئيا وليس بلون.
Then we say: We have demonstrated by proof that the state of the essence being visible is a judgment of its being existent. The Creator exists, so He is visible. Color exists, so its being visible is a judgment of its being existent. So it is visible because of its existence, not because it is a color. Thus by its being visible, it indicates absolute existence, since the judgment indicates its cause, not a restricted existent. There is no indication in its being visible except to absolute existence. So if it were a color, it would be not because it is visible but because it is not perceived by the other senses. Then it would be that it is a color as a judgment of its being not perceived by the other four senses besides sight. This is invalid, because this attaches the judgment—which is its being a color—to a non-existence, which is its not being perceived by those senses, and that is false. And because it would entail that knowledges, power, and wills are all colors, since they are not perceived by any of the four senses. Thus they would be equal to it in the cause of being colors—namely their being not perceived by the four senses—so they would be equal in the judgment, and this is false. And we have demonstrated by proof that its being possible of vision is from the judgment of existence, and He exists. And we have established by proof that taste, smell, touch, and hearing are impossible for His essence. And we have established by proof that He is not a color. So from the requirement of the totality of these proofs it follows that color is not what is exclusively perceived by vision, because a proof has been established for an essence specifically characterized by being visible while not being a color.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) that visibility is a judgment (ḥukm) of existence (wujūd), not of color. The author begins by stating that both God's essence and color are visible, but the reason (ʿilla) for visibility in both cases is existence, not color. He then argues that if visibility were due to color, then color's visibility would have to be explained by something else—namely, its not being perceived by the other senses—which is a negative attribute, and attaching a positive judgment to a negative attribute is invalid. Moreover, this would absurdly imply that knowledge, power, and will are also colors, since they too are not perceived by the four senses. Finally, the author brings together three proofs: (1) visibility follows from existence, (2) the five senses other than sight cannot perceive God, and (3) God is not a color. The conclusion is that color is not defined as 'what is exclusively perceived by sight', because God is visible yet not colored.

مرئي (marʾiyy)
وجود (wujūd)
علة (ʿilla)
حكم (ḥukm)
دلالة (dalāla)
لون (lawn)
حواس (ḥawāss)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visibility is a property of color alone.
Correction: The author proves that visibility is a property of existence, so God can be visible without being colored.

Checkpoint

A) Color
B) Existence
C) Being a body
D) Being perceived by sight

Key Takeaway
Visibility is a judgment of existence, not color, and therefore God's visibility does not imply that He is a colored body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife, do you find yourself unconsciously imagining a physical form or color? How does this lesson’s proof that visibility depends on existence, not color, challenge that tendency? What do you need to actively replace that image with in your heart and mind?
?
The author argues that defining color by what is not perceived by other senses leads to absurd conclusions. Are there areas in your own faith where you define something by what it is not, rather than by what it truly is? How can this lesson inspire you to seek positive knowledge of Allah’s attributes instead of just negating imperfections?
?
Visibility is a judgment of absolute existence. If Allah is the source of all existence, and you long to see Him, what does that say about the deepest desire of your soul? How does this truth transform your worship and your hope in the Hereafter into a rational conviction that demands action today?

Refuting the Objection to the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Refutation of an objection against the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of seeing Allah by distinguishing between the permissibility of vision and its actual occurrence, and how the absence of screens does not necessitate vision.

Core Idea
Vision is a created act of Allah; absence of screens doesn't force its existence.

This passage presents a detailed rebuttal to an objection that if vision is possible without screens, then absurd consequences follow. The author clarifies that vision is a meaning created by Allah in the eye, and its occurrence depends on Allah's will, not merely the absence of obstacles. He also discusses the role of custom and gives examples.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish permissibility from actuality
Notice how the author separates the question of whether something can be seen (jawaz) from whether it is actually seen (wujud). The previous proof established permissibility; actual vision depends on Allah's creation.
This distinction is crucial for the entire argument.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objector's challenge: if absence of screens does not guarantee vision, then absurd scenarios become possible, leading to sophistry. Ask yourself why this objection seems powerful.
The objector uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Learn the response: vision as a created meaning
The author replies that vision is a 'meaning' (ma'na) created by Allah in the eye. Absence of screens does not obligate vision; Allah may create its opposite. This refutes the necessity of the objection.
The author also mentions Abu Hashim's denial and the consensus of others.
4
Examine the role of custom and examples
See how the author explains that screens customarily cause the opposite of vision for human benefit. Then note the examples (ship motion, Prophet seeing angel) that verify the principle.
These examples show that visibility does not guarantee actual sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : التعليل وقع لجواز الرؤية لا للوجود ، وهذه الأشياء جائزة الرؤية بالدليل الذي تقدم ذكره، فأما وجوب الرؤية فإنه يكون بتخليق الله تعالى للرؤية في آلة الرؤية فإذا خلق الرؤية للشيء يرى ذلك، وإن لم يخلق الرؤية وخلق ضدها لا يرى، ولم يخرج الشيء من أن يكون مرئيا هذا كما أن الله تعالى لو خلق العلم بشيء من الأشياء علم ذلك الشيء، ولو لم يخلق العلم حتى جهله الإنسان بقي مجهولا، ولم يخرج من أن يكون العلم به ممكنا، فكذا هذا. فإن قالوا : هذا الكلام في غاية الفساد فإن وجود المرئي وارتفاع السواتر بينه وبين آلة الرؤية بدون الرؤية لا يتصور. قلنا: لم قلتم ذلك؟ فإن قالوا: لو جاز هذا لبطلت المعارف وصحت السوفسطائية فإن على قود كلامكم هذا يجوز أن يكون ها هنا بحضرتنا قبله عظام ترقص ونيران هائلة تضطرم و دبادب وبوقات تضرب وينفخ فيها، وأعلام منشورة وعساكر مارة، ولم تخلق فينا الرؤية والسمع، فلم نقف على ذلك، وركوب هذا وتجويزه خروج عن المعقول وادعاء ما يعرف كذبه ضرورة وتمسك بالسوفسطائية. قلنا: شيء مما ذكرت ليس بلازم فإن الرؤية إنما كانت معنى في الآلة يخلقه الله تعالى عند فتح الإنسان العين لا محالة بلا خلاف بيننا وبين المعتزلة سوى أبي هاشم فإنه أنكر كون الرؤية وسائر الادراكات معانی، ونحن نستدل على ثبوتها عليه بمثل ما نستدل به على ثبوت جميع الأعراض، ولم يحصل لأبي هاشم بهذا المنع سوى إبطال طرق إثبات الأعراض على نفسه، فإذا كانت الرؤية ثابتة وهي معنى في العين بتخليق الله تعالى عرف ذلك بالإجماع من كل، وبالدليل الذي تلجئ أبا هاشم إلى الاعتراف به والله تعالى يخلق ما يخلق باختياره فمن الجائز أن لا يخلق الرؤية في عين إنسان فلا يرى، وإن لم يكن بينه وبين المرئي حجاب لانعدام الرؤية، وهذا كما لم يخلق في حال تحقيق السواتر والحجب وهناك انعدام الرؤية، بأن لم يخلق الله تعالى الرؤية في الآلة، وخلق ضدها لا لوجود السواثر لأن الشيء إنما يستحيل وجوده إذا اشتغل محله بضده، فأما قيام ما ليس بضد للرؤية في غير محل الرؤية فلا يوجب استحالة الرؤية في محلها. غير أن الله تعالى أجرى العادة أن يخلق ضد الرؤية وجود السواتر نظر العباد ليمكنهم إخفاء ما لا يعجبهم اطلاع غيرهم عليه بتحصيل السواتر فيحصل ضد الرؤية في الآلة فلا توجد الرؤية، فلو لم يخلق الرؤية مع انعدام السواتر وارتفاع الحجب في الجواهر والألوان والأكوان لكانت لا ترى ولا تخرج من كونها جائزة الرؤية فكذا هذه الأشياء. والذي يحقق هذا أن حركة السفينة لا يراها الراكب ويراها غيره لوجود ضدها في بصره، وإن كانت الحركة مرئية عند الجبائي، وصبغ المغزل والسهم لازم عليه وعلى ابنه والذي يحقق هذا ثبوت رؤية النبي عليه السلام الملك وانعدام رؤية غيره بلا ساتر، ولو لم يكن ذلك إلا بخلق الله الرؤية في عين النبي عليه السلام وخلقه ضد الرؤية في عين غيره.
We said: The justification occurred for the permissibility of vision, not for existence. And these things are permissible of vision by the proof that was mentioned previously. As for the necessity of vision, it occurs by the creation of Allah Most High of vision in the organ of vision. So if He creates vision for a thing, it sees that; and if He does not create vision and creates its opposite, it does not see, and the thing does not cease to be visible. This is just as if Allah Most High created knowledge of something, that thing would be known; and if He did not create knowledge so that the human remained ignorant, it would remain unknown, and it would not cease to be possible to know it. So likewise this. If they say: This speech is in extreme corruption, for the existence of the visible object and the removal of screens between it and the organ of vision without vision is inconceivable. We said: Why do you say that? If they say: If this were permissible, then cognitions would be invalid and sophistry would be correct; for according to the implication of your speech, it would be possible that here in our presence there are bones dancing, and huge fires blazing, and drums and trumpets being struck and blown, and banners unfurled, and armies passing, but He did not create in us vision and hearing, so we would not be aware of that. Adopting this and deeming it permissible is a departure from reason and a claim of what is known to be false by necessity, and clinging to sophistry. We said: Nothing of what you mentioned is necessary. For vision is a meaning in the organ that Allah Most High creates upon the human opening his eye necessarily, without disagreement between us and the Mu'tazila except Abu Hashim, for he denied that vision and all perceptions are meanings. And we adduce evidence for their being meanings upon him by similar evidence as we adduce for the existence of all accidents. And Abu Hashim obtained by this denial nothing other than the invalidation of the methods of proving accidents upon himself. So since vision is established and it is a meaning in the eye by the creation of Allah Most High, that is known by consensus from everyone, and by the proof that compels Abu Hashim to acknowledge it. And Allah Most High creates what He creates by His choice, so it is possible that He does not create vision in the eye of a person, so he does not see, even if there is no screen between him and the visible object, due to the absence of vision. And this is just as when screens and veils exist, there is absence of vision. Because Allah Most High did not create vision in the organ, and created its opposite, not because of the existence of screens. Because a thing's existence is only impossible if its place is occupied by its opposite. As for the subsistence of what is not opposite to vision in other than the locus of vision, it does not necessitate the impossibility of vision in its locus. However, Allah Most High has established the custom that the existence of screens creates the opposite of vision for the benefit of servants, so that they can conceal what they dislike others to know by obtaining screens, so the opposite of vision occurs in the organ, and vision does not exist. So if He did not create vision with the absence of screens and the removal of veils in substances, colors, and locations, they would not be seen and would not cease to be permissible of vision. So likewise these things. And what verifies this is that the motion of the ship is not seen by the passenger but is seen by another, due to the existence of its opposite in his sight, even though the motion is visible according to al-Jubba'i. And the dyeing of the spindle and the arrow is incumbent upon him and his son. And what verifies this is the established fact of the Prophet, peace be upon him, seeing the angel and the absence of seeing by others without a screen. And that was only by Allah creating vision in the eye of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and creating the opposite of vision in the eye of another.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised against the possibility of seeing Allah. The author first distinguishes between the permissibility (jawaz) of vision and its actual occurrence (wujud). He states that the previous proof established that certain things (including Allah) are permissible to be seen, but actual vision depends on Allah's creation of a 'meaning' (ma'na) in the organ of vision. If Allah creates vision, the thing is seen; if He creates its opposite (like blindness), it is not seen, but the thing remains inherently visible. He analogizes this to knowledge: if Allah creates knowledge of a thing, it is known; if not, it remains unknown but still knowable. The objector then argues that if we allow the possibility of a visible thing not being seen despite the absence of screens, then we could imagine absurd scenarios (like bones dancing unseen) leading to sophistry. The author responds that the objection is not necessary (lazim). He explains that vision is a created meaning in the eye, created upon opening the eye, according to consensus except for Abu Hashim who denied that perceptions are meanings. The author notes that Abu Hashim's denial undermines his own proof of accidents. Since Allah creates freely, He may or may not create vision; the absence of screens does not necessitate vision. Screens customarily cause the opposite of vision, but this is a habitual arrangement (ada) for human benefit, not a logical necessity. The author provides examples: a ship's motion is not seen by a passenger due to the opposite in his sight, yet the motion is still visible; and the Prophet saw the angel while others did not, without any screen, solely because Allah created vision in the Prophet's eye and its opposite in others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If there is no screen between an object and the eye, vision must occur necessarily.
Correction: Vision is a created accident (meaning) that Allah may choose to create or not create. The absence of screens does not obligate vision; Allah may create its opposite instead.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what determines whether a thing is actually seen?
A) The absence of screens between the thing and the observer
B) The existence of the thing itself
C) Whether Allah creates vision or its opposite in the organ of sight
D) The observer's intention to see

Key Takeaway
Vision is a created accident that depends on Allah's will; the absence of physical impediments does not necessitate actual sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's will is absolutely free and not bound by physical laws? How does this affect your hope to see Him in the afterlife, knowing that vision depends entirely on His sovereign choice?
?
Have you ever assumed that something must happen because all the 'conditions' were met, forgetting that Allah may withhold His action? How can this lesson change your attitude towards everyday expectations and gratitude?
?
The Prophet saw the angel while others did not, without any screen. What does this teach you about spiritual perception? Are you asking Allah to grant you insight that goes beyond physical observation?

How Divine Custom Grounds Our Certainty About God's Non-Vision
Imam al-Nasafi presents the second answer based on divine custom and applies it to the non-vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of divine custom (al-ʿādah) provides a rational basis for certainty about the non-vision of God in this world, and how it applies to everyday examples.

Core Idea
Divine custom is consistent; its violation is only for miracles, so we rely on custom for certainty.

The second answer from some Maturidi scholars shifts the argument from impossibility to divine custom. While it is logically possible for God to create opposite vision in our eyes, He does not ordinarily do so. This consistency, called divine custom, gives us reliable knowledge. The passage uses analogies to show how we judge everyday occurrences with certainty based on custom, and then applies this to the case of God's vision.

Guided Path
1
Understand divine custom
Read the first few lines where the author states that God's custom is not to create the opposite vision for ordinary objects like elephants. Note that this is a consistent pattern.
Custom (ʿādah) is God's consistent way of acting, not a logical necessity.
2
Examine the three analogies
Identify the three analogies: (1) turning men into women in Khorasan, (2) extreme weather in unlikely regions, (3) a man exiting his house. For each, note that we are certain of their falsehood despite logical possibility.
These analogies show how we rely on custom for certainty.
3
Grasp certainty from custom
Understand why the author says 'knowledge of the customary is certain.' Recognize that customary knowledge is a valid source of certainty in kalam.
Customary certainty is distinct from logical certainty but still reliable.
4
Apply to God's vision
See how the same reasoning applies: we do not see God because God customarily creates the opposite of His vision in our eyes, not because it is impossible. Note that the text ends incomplete.
The argument concludes that non-vision is due to custom, not impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض أصحابنا أجابوا أن هذا وإن كان من الجائزات ولكن لما لم يجر الله تعالى العادة بخلق ضد رؤية الفيلة والخيول وغير ذلك في أعيننا فلا نراها، وإن كانت بحضرتنا وقع لنا الأمان عند انعدام رؤيتنا إياها عن وجودها بطريق العادة، فإن الله تعالى لا ينتقض العادة المستمرة إلا عند زمان بعث الرسل عليهم السلام معجزة لهم وحجة على قومهم، أو على يد الولي في بعض الأزمنة المخصوصة كرامة له والعلم بالمعتاد يقين ونقضه ممتنع عادة فيقع لنا الأمان من ذلك، كما أن إنسانا لو أخبر أن الله تعالى حول جميع رجال خراسان إناثا ونساءهم ذكرانا عرفنا كذبه بيقين لانعدام العادة، وإن كان ذلك ممكنا ثابتا في مقدور الله تعالى. وكذا لو أخبر إنسان أن بتهامة في أيام باحوراء اشتد البرد حتى جمدت المياه، أو في قلب الشتاء اشتد الحر وهبت السموم في جبال الترك يعلم كذبه يقينا لانعدام جريان العادة بذلك، وكذا لو أن إنسانا دخل بيته ثم خرج علمنا يقينا أنه عين ذلك الإنسان وإن كان من الممكن أن أعدم الله ذلك الرجل وخلق آخر على شبهه وهيئته ولكن لما لم يكن ذلك معتادا علم بانعدامه كذا فيما نحن فيه. وعلى هذا خرج جواب ما ألزموا من انعدام رؤيتنا الله تعالى في الدنيا وهو إنما لم نره لتعلقه تعالى في أبصارنا ضد رؤيته، وهذا لا يستحيل لأن الله تعالى ليس بذي جنس حتى يقال: لما رأينا جنسه لم يجز انعدام رؤيته على الجواب
And some of our scholars answered: This, although it is among possibilities, but since God, exalted, did not customarily create the opposite vision of elephants, horses, and other things in our eyes, we do not see them; and if they were in our presence, we feel secure [that they are not present] when we do not see them due to [the lack of] their existence by way of custom. For God, exalted, does not violate the continuous custom except at the time of sending the messengers, peace be upon them, as a miracle for them and a proof against their people, or at the hand of a saint (walī) in some specific times as a miracle of honor (karāmah) for him. And knowledge of the customary is certain, and its violation is impossible customarily, so we feel secure from that. Just as if a man were to inform that God, exalted, turned all the men of Khorasan into women and their women into men, we would certainly know his lie due to the absence of custom, even if that is possible and established within God's power. And likewise if a man were to inform that in Tihāmah during the days of Baḥūrā' (?) the cold intensified until the waters froze, or in the heart of winter the heat intensified and the hot winds (samūm) blew in the mountains of the Turks, one would certainly know his lie due to the absence of the custom of that. And likewise if a man entered his house and then came out, we certainly know that he is the same man, even if it is possible that God caused that man to cease to exist and created another in his likeness and form; but since that is not customary, we know its non-existence. Such is the case in our matter. And upon this the answer is based to what they obligated regarding the non-existence of our vision of God, exalted, in this world: that we do not see Him only because He, exalted, attaches in our eyes the opposite of His vision. And this is not impossible because God, exalted, is not of a genus such that it could be said: Since we see His genus, it is not permissible for His vision to be absent, according to the [first] answer.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer from some scholars, which relies on the concept of divine custom (al-ʿādah). The argument is that while it is in principle possible for God to create the opposite vision in our eyes so that we do not see an elephant present, God does not ordinarily do so. God's custom (sunnat Allāh) is consistent except in cases of miracles for prophets or saints. Therefore, when we do not see an elephant in a situation we would expect to see it, we are certain it is not there because the customary absence of its opposite vision gives us that certainty. The author provides three analogies: (1) transforming men into women in Khorasan, (2) extreme cold in a hot region or heat in a cold region, (3) a man exiting his house; in each case, despite the logical possibility, we are certain of the falsity of such reports due to the lack of custom. This certainty (al-ʿilm bil-muʿtād) is reliable. The passage then applies this reasoning to the non-vision of God in this world: we do not see God because God creates in our eyes the opposite of His vision, not because it is impossible—since God has no genus, the argument from homogeneity does not apply. The text ends abruptly, likely continuing with the second answer regarding custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is logically possible, it must occur or be equally likely to occur.
Correction: The passage shows that many logical possibilities never occur because of God's consistent custom; we rely on customary regularity for certainty, not mere possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, why do we feel secure that an elephant is not present when we do not see it?
A) Because it is logically impossible for an elephant to exist unseen.
B) Because God's custom does not include creating opposite vision in our eyes for such cases.
C) Because elephants cannot be present without being heard.
D) Because the opponents admit it.

Key Takeaway
Divine custom provides a rational basis for certainty: we know that we do not see God because God consistently does not create the vision of Himself in this world, not because it is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you rely on God's consistent custom (e.g., the sun rising, your body functioning). But do you trust that God's custom is also at work in your spiritual life—that He will not break His pattern of guiding those who seek Him? How does your routine reflect that trust or the lack of it?
?
The passage uses everyday examples (a man leaving his house) to ground certainty about the unseen. When was the last time you actively used the consistency of God's creation to strengthen your faith in something you cannot see, like the unseen realities of the afterlife? If not, what holds you back?
?
If God's custom is only broken for miracles, what does that mean for your expectations of divine intervention? Are you demanding a miracle when you should be relying on the steady, customary guidance of God in your life? Examine one area where you are impatient for a custom-breaking event—what would it look like to trust God's ordinary means instead?

The Customary Argument for Not Seeing God
The author argues that not seeing God is due to customary continuity, not impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi scholars use the concept of customary continuity to explain why we do not see God in this world, distinguishing between logical impossibility and customary non-occurrence.

Core Idea
Custom, not impossibility, explains why we do not see God in this world.

This passage presents the Maturidi defense against the objection that if seeing God is possible, why do we not see Him? The answer lies not in logical impossibility but in the principle of custom (al-'ādah). Through vivid analogies, the author demonstrates that our certainty about what we do not see is grounded in the regularity of God's customary actions, which are only broken for miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the elephant hypothetical
Start by reading the example where God reduces the eye's capacity so that one only sees a fraction of an elephant. Understand how this shows that absence of perception does not entail absence of object.
This sets up the idea that not seeing could be due to a hindrance in the eye, not the object's non-existence.
2
Identify the companions' answer
The author then gives the answer of some companions: God has not made it customary to create an opposite of vision for large objects, so when we do not see them, we are certain of their absence by custom.
Note the key phrase: 'لما لم يجر الله تعالى العادة بخلق ضد رؤية الفيلة'.
3
Examine the analogies
The author provides analogies: turning men into women, extreme weather changes, and a person entering a house. In each case, we know the claim is false with certainty because it violates custom, even though it is logically possible.
These analogies reinforce that our certainty is based on custom, not logical necessity.
4
Apply to seeing God
Finally, the author applies the same reasoning to the objection about not seeing God: either God creates an opposite of vision in us (which is not impossible because God has no genus) or seeing God is simply not customary in this world.
Thus, the absence of seeing God is explained by custom, not by impossibility.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the overall argument: the Maturidi position is that not seeing God in this world is due to customary continuity, which provides certainty, and that this does not break God's custom because it is maintained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثلها، ولو رفع الله تعالى عن عين الإنسان ما فيها من الرؤية وبقى مقدار ما لو نظر إلى الفيل لم يدرك منه إلا مقدار البقة رأى منه مقداره ولم يره بأجمعه. وبعض أصحابنا أجابوا أن هذا وإن كان من الجائزات ولكن لما لم يجر الله تعالى العادة بخلق ضد رؤية الفيلة والخيول وغير ذلك في أعيننا فلا نراها، وإن كانت بحضرتنا وقع لنا الأمان عند انعدام رؤيتنا إياها عن وجودها بطريق العادة، فإن الله تعالى لا ينتقض العادة المستمرة إلا عند زمان بعث الرسل عليهم السلام معجزة لهم وحجة على قومهم، أو على يد الولي في بعض الأزمنة المخصوصة كرامة له والعلم بالمعتاد يقين ونقضه ممتنع عادة فيقع لنا الأمان من ذلك، كما أن إنسانا لو أخبر أن الله تعالى حول جميع رجال خراسان إناثا ونساءهم ذكرانا عرفنا كذبه بيقين لانعدام العادة، وإن كان ذلك ممكنا ثابتا في مقدور الله تعالى. وكذا لو أخبر إنسان أن بتهامة في أيام باحوراء اشتد البرد حتى جمدت المياه، أو في قلب الشتاء اشتد الحر وهبت السموم في جبال الترك يعلم كذبه يقينا لانعدام جريان العادة بذلك، وكذا لو أن إنسانا دخل بيته ثم خرج علمنا يقينا أنه عين ذلك الإنسان وإن كان من الممكن أن أعدم الله ذلك الرجل وخلق آخر على شبهه وهيئته ولكن لما لم يكن ذلك معتادا علم بانعدامه كذا فيما نحن فيه. وعلى هذا خرج جواب ما ألزموا من انعدام رؤيتنا الله تعالى في الدنيا وهو إنما لم نره لتعلقه تعالى في أبصارنا ضد رؤيته، وهذا لا يستحيل لأن الله تعالى ليس بذي جنس حتى يقال: لما رأينا جنسه لم يجز انعدام رؤيته على الجواب الأول. وعلى الثاني أن رؤيته ليست بمعتادة في الدنيا ليقال: لا تنعدم لئلا يؤدي إلى قلب العادة.
Like it, and if God Most High removed from the human eye what is in it of vision, and there remained only an amount such that if one looked at an elephant he would perceive from it only the size of a flea, he would see its size but would not see it as a whole. And some of our companions answered that this, although it is among the possibilities, yet since God Most High has not made it customary to create an opposite of the vision of elephants, horses, and other things in our eyes, we do not see them, even if they are present, and we feel secure when our vision of them is absent concerning their existence by way of custom. Indeed, God Most High does not break the continuous custom except at the time of sending messengers, peace be upon them, as a miracle for them and a proof against their people, or at the hand of a saint in some specific times as a miracle for him. And knowledge of the customary is certainty, and its breach is impossible customarily, so we feel secure from that. As if a person informed that God Most High turned all the men of Khurasan into women and their women into men, we would know his lie with certainty due to the absence of custom, even if that were possible and established within the power of God Most High. And similarly, if a person informed that in Tihama during the days of the dry heat the cold intensified until waters froze, or in the middle of winter the heat intensified and the hot winds blew in the mountains of the Turks, his lie would be known with certainty due to the absence of the running of custom with that. And similarly, if a person entered his house and then came out, we know with certainty that he is that same person, even if it were possible that God destroyed that man and created another similar to him in likeness and form, but since that is not customary, its non-existence is known. So too in our case. And upon this is based the answer to what they objected regarding the absence of our seeing God Most High in the world: it is that we do not see Him because He Most High creates in our eyes an opposite of His vision. And this is not impossible, because God Most High is not of a genus such that it could be said: since we have seen His genus, it is not permissible for His vision to be absent, according to the first answer. And according to the second: His vision is not customary in the world, such that it could be said: it is not absent lest it lead to breaking the custom.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of why we do not see God in this world, based on the principle of custom (al-'ādah). The author begins with a hypothetical example: if God removed most of the visual capacity from a human eye, leaving only the ability to perceive a tiny fraction, then looking at an elephant would only yield perception of a flea-sized portion, not the whole elephant. This illustrates that the absence of perception does not entail the non-existence of the object. Some Maturidi scholars (aṣḥābunā) then provide the key argument: although it is possible for God to create an opposite of vision (ḍidd al-ru'yah) in our eyes, He has not made it customary to do so for large objects like elephants and horses. Therefore, when we do not see these objects, we feel certain of their absence due to custom, not due to logical necessity. This custom is only broken during prophetic miracles and saintly karamāt. The author reinforces this with examples: if someone claimed God turned all men of Khurasan into women, we would know it is false with certainty because it violates custom, even though it is possible. Similarly for extreme weather events. The author then applies this to the objection about not seeing God in this world. Two answers are derived from the custom argument: (1) God creates an opposite of His vision in our eyes; this is not impossible because God has no genus, so the objection 'we have seen His genus' does not apply. (2) God's vision is simply not customary in this world, so its absence is known through custom and does not break custom. The passage thus defends the Maturidi position that not seeing God is due to customary continuity, not impossibility.

Key Terms
عادة
ضد الرؤية
الإمكان العقلي
يقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that if we do not see something, it must not exist or be impossible to see.
Correction: The passage teaches that absence of vision does not prove non-existence; rather, our certainty of absence comes from the continuity of God's custom.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do we feel certain that we are not seeing a large object like an elephant even when it is present?
A) Because the object is too large for our eyes.
B) Because God has not made it customary to create an opposite of vision for such objects.
C) Because it is logically impossible for us to see it.
D) Because the object does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi argument from custom shows that not seeing God in this world is due to the continuity of God's customary action, not due to any impossibility in God being seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's customary actions are guarantees, or have you ever doubted His power because something didn’t happen in the usual way? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on the usual?
?
If you truly believed that God's not being seen in this world is a matter of custom, not impossibility, would your longing for Him be different? What would change in your relationship with the unseen?
?
The author argues we are certain that a man leaving his house is the same person because of custom. How does your certainty about God's actions—or lack thereof—compare? Are you as confident in God's custom as in everyday events?

Divine Transcendence

Refuting Tashbih via Symmetry and the Nature of Vision
Nasafi refutes tashbih accusation with symmetrical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the accusation that affirming ru'ya entails tashbih by using a symmetrical counter-argument and by showing that vision applies to diverse and contradictory things without causing similarity.

Core Idea
Affirming ru'ya does not imply tashbih; vision itself does not necessitate similarity.

The Mu'tazilites accused Ahl al-Sunnah of tashbih for affirming that Allah will be seen. Nasafi responds with a sharp refutation, turning their own logic against them. He demonstrates that if denying ru'ya leads to tashbih (by likening Allah to invisible things), and since vision in the world applies to diverse and contradictory things without causing similarity, then affirming ru'ya for Allah does not entail tashbih either.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite accusation
Read the opening lines where the Mu'tazilites claim that affirming ru'ya implies tashbih because in this world vision only pertains to bodies, colors, etc. Notice the charge of 'impudence'.
The accusation is that Ahl al-Sunnah liken Allah to created objects by claiming He is seen.
2
Follow the counter-argument from symmetry
Nasafi points out that the Mu'tazilites themselves hold that many things are impossible to see (tastes, wills, etc.). So, if saying 'God is seen' is tashbih, then saying 'God is not seen' is also tashbih by analogy. This is a powerful tu quoque.
This is the core of the refutation: the logic is symmetrical.
3
Examine the objection and response
The Mu'tazilites might reply that the invisible things are diverse, so denial doesn't imply specific tashbih. Nasafi responds by noting that visible things are equally diverse and contradictory, yet being visible does not cause tashbih among them.
This shows that vision itself cannot be a basis for tashbih.
4
Appreciate the final point on vision's scope
Nasafi observes that vision relates to contradictory things (black/white, motion/stillness) and different genera (substances, colors, places) without implying similarity. Therefore, vision relating to both eternal and temporal does not imply tashbih either.
This final point seals the argument by showing that vision transcends the categories of what it perceives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعجب من وقاحتهم أنهم ينسبون أهل الحق إلى التشبيه لإثباتهم الرؤية، ويزعمون أن الرؤية في الشاهد تتعلق بأجناس مخصوصة من الأجسام والألوان والأكوان، فمن زعم أن الله تعالى مرئي فقد شبه الله تعالى بخلقه. فهذا غاية في الوقاحة لأن أدنى ما يجابون عن هذا أن مما يستحيل رؤيته عندكم أجناس مخصوصة من الطعوم والروائح والقدر والإرادات والعلوم والاعتقادات، فمن قال باستحالة رؤية الله تعالى فقد شبهه بها. فإن قالوا ما لا يرى أجناس كثيرة ، فالقول بكونه مستحيل الرؤية لا يؤدي إلى التشبيه بها كلها، لأن ذلك محال لاختلافها في أنفسها ولا إلى التشبيه ببعضها لانعدام دليل التخصيص. قلنا: وكذلك المرئي عندكم أجناس مختلفة وفيها أشياء متضادة، فيستحيل أن تؤدي إلى التشبيه بها كلها أو ببعضها كما قلتم - ثم العجيب من غفلتهم أنهم لم ينظروا إلى أن الرؤية تتعلق بالمتضادات من نحو السواد والبياض والحمرة والخضرة والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق ولا ريب أن لا مشابهة بين المتضادات، وكذا تتعلق بالمختلفات من نحو الجواهر والألوان والأكوان، ولا مشابهة بين هذه الأجناس الثلاثة المختلفة خصوصا على أصلهم، أن المشابهة تجري في الأوصاف النفسية عند الجبائي، وفي أخص الأوصاف النفسية على قول أبي هاشم على ما مر. وإذا كانت الرؤية تتعلق بالمتضادات والمختلفات، ولا توجب تشبيه بضعها ببعض ، فكذا تتعلق بالقديم والمحدث ولا توجب تشبيه البعض بالبعض.
And the wonder is at their impudence that they attribute the people of truth to anthropomorphism for affirming vision, and they claim that vision in the witnessed [world] is related to specific genera of bodies, colors, and places, so whoever claims that Allah the Exalted is seen has likened Allah the Exalted to His creation. This is the ultimate in impudence because the least answer to them about this is that among what is impossible to see according to you are specific genera of tastes, smells, powers, wills, knowledges, and beliefs, so whoever says that seeing Allah the Exalted is impossible has likened Him to them. If they say: What is not seen is many genera, so saying that He is impossible to see does not lead to likening Him to all of them, because that is impossible due to their difference in themselves, nor to likening Him to some of them due to the absence of evidence for specification. We said: Likewise, what is seen according to you is different genera and among them are contradictory things, so it is impossible that it leads to likening Him to all of them or to some of them as you said. Then the wonder at their heedlessness is that they did not consider that vision is related to contradictory things like black, white, red, green, motion, stillness, conjunction, separation, and there is no doubt that there is no similarity between contradistintorys, and likewise it is related to different things like substances, colors, and places, and there is no similarity between these three different genera, especially according to their principle that similarity runs in the essential attributes according to al-Jubba'i, and in the most specific essential attributes according to Abu Hashim as has passed. And since vision is related to contradictions and differences, and does not necessitate likening some to others, so likewise it is related to the eternal and the temporal and does not necessitate likening some to others.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite accusation that affirming the vision of God (ru'ya) entails tashbih (anthropomorphism). The author first expresses astonishment at the Mu'tazilites' audacity in accusing the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) of tashbih because they affirm ru'ya, while the Mu'tazilites themselves claim that in the witnessed world (al-shahid) vision only pertains to bodies, colors, and places. The author then presents a counter-argument: the Mu'tazilites hold that many things are impossible to see, such as tastes, smells, powers, wills, etc. So if someone says God is impossible to see, they are likening Him to these invisible things. The Mu'tazilites might object that these invisible things are diverse, so no specific tashbih results. The author responds: the same applies to visible things—they are diverse and even contradictory (e.g., black and white), yet being visible does not cause tashbih among them. Therefore, vision itself cannot be used as a basis for tashbih. The author further notes that the Mu'tazilites themselves, according to their own principles (e.g., al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim's theories of essential attributes), admit that similarity runs in essential attributes, but vision does not entail sharing such attributes. Hence, affirming ru'ya does not entail tashbih.

Key Terms
تشبيه
رؤية
شاهد
أجناس
متضادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming ru'ya necessarily implies tashbih because vision in this world is only of physical objects.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the same logic would apply to denying ru'ya (making it tashbih to invisible things), and that vision itself does not cause similarity as it applies to diverse and contradictory things.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence of the Mu'tazilite claim that only bodies, colors, and places are seen?
A) It proves ru'ya is impossible for God.
B) It leads to tashbih of God to invisible things if ru'ya is denied.
C) It shows that ru'ya is limited to temporal objects.
D) It is a correct observation with no further implications.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the tashbih accusation by demonstrating that vision itself does not create similarity, and that the Mu'tazilite position is self-defeating through symmetrical logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself instinctively distancing God from an attribute like 'seeing' or 'hearing' because you feared it would make Him too 'human'? What does Nasafi's symmetrical argument reveal about the flaw in that fear? This week, catch yourself any time you hesitate to affirm a divine attribute without proof, and reflect whether the hesitation is from piety or from a logical mistake you need to correct.
?
The Mu'tazilites accused others of tashbih while falling into the same error by their own logic. Is there a belief you hold about God that you have never examined for internal consistency? Take five minutes today to write down one of your core theological convictions and test it with the kind of symmetrical reasoning Nasafi uses. Are you consistent?
?
When you hear that God will be seen in the afterlife, does your heart leap with longing or shrink with doubt? If the latter, what does that reveal about your conception of God's majesty? Commit this week to asking Allah in your prayers to grant you the certainty that vision of Him is possible without reducing His transcendence, just as the scholars have affirmed.

Refuting the Tashbih Objection: Vision Does Not Imply Similarity
Nasafi refutes the tashbih objection by showing vision applies to contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that denying God's visibility leads to anthropomorphism by demonstrating that vision applies to contradictory and diverse things without implying similarity.

Core Idea
Vision applies to contradictories and diverse things without implying similarity — so it does not imply anthropomorphism.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a key objection from those who argue that denying the beatific vision leads to comparing God to created objects. He shows that the same logic of vision applies to many diverse and contradictory visible things, yet no one claims they are similar. Therefore, vision does not necessitate similarity. Let us follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It is a dense argument. Then read the literal translation.
Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that denying vision of God implies tashbih (anthropomorphism) because they compare God to created visible things.
3
Understand Nasafi's counter
Nasafi shows that vision applies to contradictories (e.g., black/white, motion/rest) and different kinds (substances, colors, modes) without implying similarity, so the same applies to God.
He uses the opponents' own principle about psychological attributes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The key lesson: vision does not necessitate similarity, so denying vision does not lead to anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عندكم أجناس مخصوصة من الطعوم والروائح والقدر والإرادات والعلوم والاعتقادات، فمن قال باستحالة رؤية الله تعالى فقد شبهه بها. فإن قالوا ما لا يرى أجناس كثيرة، فالقول بكونه مستحيل الرؤية لا يؤدي إلى التشبيه بها كلها، لأن ذلك محال لاختلافها في أنفسها ولا إلى التشبيه ببعضها لانعدام دليل التخصيص. قلنا: وكذلك المرئي عندكم أجناس مختلفة وفيها أشياء متضادة، فيستحيل أن تؤدي إلى التشبيه بها كلها أو ببعضها كما قلتم - ثم العجيب من غفلتهم أنهم لم ينظروا إلى أن الرؤية تتعلق بالمتضادات من نحو السواد والبياض والحمرة والخضرة والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق ولا ريب أن لا مشابهة بين المتضادات، وكذا يتعلق بالمختلفات من نحو الجواهر والألوان والأكوان، ولا مشابهة بين هذه الأجناس الثلاثة المختلفة خصوصا على أصلهم، أن المشابهة تجري في الأوصاف النفسية عند الجبائي، وفي أخص الأوصاف النفسية على قول أبي هاشم على ما مر. وإذا كانت الرؤية تتعلق بالمتضادات والمختلفات، ولا توجب تشبيه بعضها ببعض، فكذا تتعلق بالقديم والمحدث ولا توجب تشبيه البعض بالبعض.
With you there are specific kinds of tastes and smells and power and wills and knowledge and beliefs, so whoever says that seeing God is impossible has likened Him to them. And if they say: 'What is not seen is many kinds, so saying that He is impossible to see does not lead to likening Him to all of them, because that is impossible due to their difference in themselves, nor to likening Him to some of them due to the absence of evidence for specification.' We say: Likewise, the visible according to you is different kinds including contradictory things, so it is impossible that it leads to likening Him to all of them or to some of them as you said. Then surprising is their heedlessness that they did not consider that vision applies to contradictories such as blackness and whiteness and redness and greenness and motion and rest and conjunction and separation, and there is no doubt that there is no similarity between contradictories. Likewise, it applies to different things such as substances, colors, and modes of existence, and there is no similarity between these three different kinds, especially according to their principle that similarity applies to psychological attributes according to al-Jubba'i, and to the most specific of psychological attributes according to Abu Hashim as has passed. And when vision applies to contradictories and different things and does not necessitate similarity of some to others, likewise it applies to the eternal and the temporal and does not necessitate similarity of some to others.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author first states the opponents' claim that denying vision of God leads to anthropomorphism because they compare God to created visible things. Then he gives a hypothetical objection from the opponents: what is not seen is many kinds, so denying vision does not lead to likening to all or some. The author responds by stating that the visible in their own view includes different and contradictory kinds, so the same logic applies: vision does not necessitate similarity. He then criticizes their oversight: vision applies to contradictories like black/white, motion/rest, etc., and to different kinds like substances, colors, modes, with no similarity between them. Therefore, vision applying to the eternal and temporal likewise does not necessitate similarity.

Correction: Nasafi shows that vision applies to many different and contradictory things without implying similarity, so denying vision does not entail comparison.

Refutation of Two Incorrect Answers on Vision
Al-Nasafi refutes two incorrect arguments about the verse 'No vision can grasp Him'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi refutes two incorrect interpretations of the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' and why the correct praise pertains to God's essence.

Core Idea
God's essence is beyond comprehension, not just unseen.

In this passage, al-Nasafi criticizes two flawed arguments about the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'. Some claimed the verse praises God based on a change in state, others based on an act of negation. Al-Nasafi shows both are wrong and that the true praise is an essential attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Note the flow of al-Nasafi's argument.
The passage is dense with technical terms like حال and تمدح.
2
Identify the two refuted arguments
Look for where al-Nasafi describes the first incorrect answer (about state) and the second (about act). Notice how he rejects each.
The first is criticized as implying deficiency, the second as not unique to God.
3
Understand the positive conclusion
After refuting, al-Nasafi states the correct meaning: the praise pertains to God's essence being beyond idrak. See why this is permanent.
This connects to divine transcendence and essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحال أنقص وفى هذه الحالة أكمل ، وتجويز النقص على الله تعالى كفر فلا سبيل إلى التعويل على هذا الجواب على أصلنا . وبعض أصحابنا يعتمدون على هذا الجواب وإن كانوا يقولون بقدم التكوين وهذا لجهلهم بحقائق مذاهبهم. على أن هذا الكلام في غاية الفساد لأن الخصوم لا يسلمون للأشعرية أن هذا التمدح راجع إلى الفعل لأن التمدح لا يحصل بانتفاء الرؤية عند تخليقه ضد رؤيته في آلة البصر من المبصرين بل يساويه فيه كل ما دب ودرج فإن كل شيء لم يخلق الله تعالى رؤيته في أعين الناس لم ير فلا يحصل له بهذا مدح بل الله تعالى تمدح بأن ذاته ذات لا تحتمل الإدراك ولا يجوز ذلك عليه فكان هذا التمدح راجعا إلى الذات دون الفعل فلا يحتمل الزوال.
The state is deficient and in this state it is complete, and permitting deficiency upon God is disbelief, so there is no way to rely on this answer according to our principle. Some of our companions rely on this answer even though they say that takwin (bringing into existence) is eternal, and this is due to their ignorance of the realities of their doctrines. Moreover, this statement is extremely corrupt because the opponents do not concede to the Ash'aris that this praise refers to the act, because praise does not come about by the negation of vision when He creates the opposite of vision in the organ of sight among those who see; rather, everything that creeps and crawls is equal to Him in this respect. For everything for which God did not create vision in the eyes of people is not seen, so no praise accrues to Him by this. Rather, God praised Himself that His essence is an essence that does not admit of comprehension (idrak) and it is not permissible for Him. So this praise pertains to the essence, not the act, and thus it is not subject to cessation.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two incorrect answers regarding the interpretation of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (No vision can grasp Him). First, al-Nasafi criticizes those who argue based on 'state' (حال), claiming that God's state changes from deficient to complete. He says that such an argument implies deficiency in God, which is disbelief, and thus it cannot be relied upon. He notes that some of his own companions unwittingly endorse this answer while adhering to the doctrine of the eternity of takwin (creative act), revealing their ignorance. Second, he refutes the Ash'ari claim that the praise (تمدح) in the verse pertains to God's act (فعل) rather than His essence (ذات). He argues that if the praise were due to the mere negation of vision, then every being that is not seen would share in that praise, which is absurd. Instead, the true praise is that God's essence is beyond comprehensibility (إدراك). Therefore, the praise is an essential attribute of God, not contingent on any act, and thus it is permanent and not subject to change.

Key Terms
تمدح
إدراك
ذات
فعل
تكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the negation of vision in the verse implies a deficiency in God, or that the praise is based on an act rather than essence.
Correction: The negation affirms God's transcendence; the praise is an essential attribute, not contingent on any act.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi reject the Ash'ari argument that the praise in the verse refers to God's act?
A) Because the act is not eternal
B) Because the negation of vision is common to all unseen beings, so no special praise arises
C) Because the verse is about idrak, not ru'ya
D) Because the act is a property of essence

Key Takeaway
The praise of God in the verse is an essential attribute of His essence, not dependent on any act, and thus it is permanent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Nasafi says that permitting deficiency in God is disbelief. Search your heart: are there any subtle ways you think of God as 'less than' or limited by your own reasoning? Will you repent from that and affirm His absolute transcendence?

Negating Signs of Contingency as Divine Praise
Establishes the principle that negating signs of contingency is praise and shows how some verses negate all such signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that negating signs of contingency (amarat al-huduth) from God is a form of praise, and that some Quranic verses collectively negate all such signs.

Core Idea
Negating signs of contingency is praise; some verses negate all such signs at once.

This lesson focuses on the foundational principle that when a sign of contingency is found in created things, it indicates their originatedness; but when that sign is negated from God, it becomes praise. The author then shows how the beginning and end of certain verses require the negation of all signs of contingency, highlighting God's perfect power and self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author starts with a general principle about signs of contingency in accidents and then applies it to God.
Pay attention to the shift from 'this sign in accidents' to 'its negation from God.'
2
Identify the key claim
The main claim is that negating a sign of contingency from God results in praise. Find the sentence that states this directly.
3
Examine the verses cited
The author quotes 'He created everything' and 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' to show perfect power and negation of a partner. Consider how these verses collectively negate all signs of contingency.
The negation of a partner implies self-sufficiency, which is a condition of pre-eternity and perfection.
4
Understand the concluding statement
The passage ends by stating that self-sufficiency is among the conditions of pre-eternity and perfection. Reflect on how this ties negating signs of contingency to affirming divine perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الأمارة في حق الأعراض زيادة مدح لثبوت حدوثها بدلائل أخرى، فأما انتفاؤها عن الله تعالى فيوجب تمدحاً، لأنه تعالى لا يوصف بشيء آخر سواها من أمارات الحدوث بل في ابتداء الآية وانتهائها ما يوجب نفي جميع أمارات الحدوث. وتبين كمال قدرته بقوله تعالى: ﴿خَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿بَدِيعُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ﴾ ونفي الشريك. إذ في ثبوته عجز وغناه عن غيره ينفي الصاحبة ونفي تجزئه ينفي الولد، ونفى الجهل وإثبات العلم بقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ﴾ وقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ ثم أثبت الألوهية لنفسه والإله من يستحق العبادة والمحدث لا يستحقها وكذا بتسمية نفسه خالقاً أخبر عن انتفاء جميع سمات النقص عن نفسه، إذ الخالق هو المنفرد بفعله حيث لا يحتاج في تحصيله إلى غيره، والاستغناء من شرائط القدم والكمال، فجاء من هذا أن بعض ما تمدح به من الآيات يدل على انتفاء جميع أمارات الحدوث كقوله تعالى: ﴿بَدِيعُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ﴾ وقوله: ﴿لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ خَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ﴾ وقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَكِيلٌ﴾ وبعضه يدل على انتفاء أمارة مخصوصة كنفي الولد فإنه يدل على نفي التجزئ ونفي الصاحبة فإنه يدل على نفي الوحشة والحاجة إلى الاستثناء وقضاء الشهوة وإثبات العلم بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ وبقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ﴾ وكذا قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ لابد من أن يكون فيه إثبات مدح. نفيه مع وقد بينا أنه يثبت إما بإثبات صفات الكمال وإما بنفي سمات النقص، وفي الجملة على الرؤية لا يحصل لا هذا ولا ذاك مع أن الإدراك ليس برؤية وفي انتفاء الرؤية لا يحصل لما بينا، فأما بنفيه مع ثبوت الرؤية فيحصل التمدح بنفي الحدود والأطراف التي هي من أمارات الحدوث، ونفي أمارات الحدوث من دلائل الكمال إذا لم يقترن بالمنفي عنه دليل آخر من الدلائل، وفي الأعراض اقترنت بها دلائل وأمارات أخر، وكذا الكلام في الجوهر، على أن الجوهر له حدود ونهايات وجهات فلا يرد هذا الإشكال، والله الموفق.
This sign (al-amara) regarding accidents (al-a'rad) is an increase of praise for the establishment of their originatedness (huduth) by other proofs. As for its negation from God Almighty, it necessitates praise, because He Almighty is not described with any other sign of contingency (amarat al-huduth) besides it; rather, in the beginning of the verse and its end there is what necessitates the negation of all signs of contingency. And His perfect power is shown by His saying: 'He created everything' and His saying: 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' and the negation of the partner. For in its establishment is incapacity, and His self-sufficiency from other than Him negates the consort, and the negation of His partition negates the child, and He negated ignorance and affirmed knowledge by His saying: 'And He is the Subtle, the Aware' and His saying: 'And He is of all things Knowing.' Then He affirmed divinity (al-uluhiyya) for Himself, and the deity (al-ilah) is the one who deserves worship, and the originated (al-muhdath) does not deserve it. Likewise, by naming Himself 'Creator,' He informed of the negation of all attributes of deficiency (simat al-naqs) from Himself, for the Creator is the one unique in His act, not needing another in its accomplishment, and self-sufficiency is among the conditions of pre-eternity (al-qidam) and perfection (al-kamal). So it follows from this that some of what He praises Himself with from the verses indicates the negation of all signs of contingency, like His saying: 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' and His saying: 'There is no god but Him, He created everything' and His saying: 'And He is over all things a Guardian.' And some of it indicates the negation of a specific sign, like the negation of the child indicates the negation of partition, and the negation of the consort indicates the negation of loneliness and the need for assistance and the fulfillment of desire, and the affirmation of knowledge by His saying: 'And He is of all things Knowing' and by His saying: 'And He is the Subtle, the Aware.' Likewise His saying: 'Vision does not perceive Him' must contain the affirmation of praise. Its negation with—and we have explained that it is established either by the affirmation of attributes of perfection (sifat al-kamal) or by the negation of attributes of deficiency (simat al-naqs), and in summary regarding vision (al-ru'ya), neither this nor that occurs, given that perception (al-idrak) is not vision, and in the negation of vision it does not occur for what we explained. As for its negation with the affirmation of vision, then praise is obtained by the negation of boundaries and extremities which are among the signs of contingency, and the negation of signs of contingency is among the proofs of perfection if there is not associated with the negated thing another proof from the proofs. And in accidents, other proofs and signs are associated with them, and the same speech applies to the atom (al-jawhar), given that the atom has boundaries and ends and directions, so this objection does not arise. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about how Quranic verses that praise God implicitly or explicitly negate all signs of contingency (amarat al-huduth) from Him. The author begins by noting that when a sign of contingency is found in accidents, it serves as praise for their originatedness; but when that sign is negated from God, it becomes praise for Him because He is not described by any such sign. He then examines several verses: 'He created everything' and 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' show perfect power and negate the need for a partner. Negation of a partner implies no consort, which implies no child, and so on. The verses 'He is the Subtle, the Aware' and 'He is of all things Knowing' affirm knowledge and negate ignorance. The author concludes that some verses negate all signs of contingency collectively, while others negate specific signs. For instance, the negation of a child negates partition, and the negation of a consort negates need for companionship. The verse 'Vision does not perceive Him' (la tudrikuhu al-absar) must also contain praise. The author then explains that praise comes either by affirming attributes of perfection or negating attributes of deficiency. In the case of vision, if God is said to be unseen, neither praise nor blame necessarily follows, because 'perceiving' (idrak) is not identical to 'seeing' (ru'ya). But if vision is affirmed while boundaries and ends are negated—since boundaries are signs of contingency—then praise is achieved. However, this negation of signs of contingency only yields perfection if no other signs of contingency are present. In accidents, other signs are present, so the negation of one sign does not prove perfection. The same reasoning applies to the atom (jawhar), which has boundaries and directions, so the objection does not arise. The passage ends with a prayer: 'And God is the Grantor of success.'

أمارات الحدوث
الأعراض
التمددح

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of negating a sign of contingency from God?
A) It proves that God is an accident.
B) It becomes a form of praise for God.
C) It has no effect on God's attributes.
D) It is only applicable to created beings.

Key Takeaway
Negating any sign of contingency from God is an act of praise, and some Quranic verses achieve this by collectively negating all such signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe in your heart that God needs nothing from you—not your worship, not your obedience, not your existence—and yet He still deserves all praise? How does that make you feel about your own need for Him?
?
When you praise God, do you find yourself focusing only on His blessings to you, or do you also praise Him simply for being free from every imperfection? Which is harder for you, and why?
?
The lesson says that negating signs of contingency from God is itself praise. Think of one subtle way you might have attributed a kind of 'need' to God (e.g., needing your worship, needing your obedience). Will you consciously reject that thought today and replace it with praise of His self-sufficiency?

Verses Negating All vs. Specific Signs of Contingency
Classifies Quranic verses into those negating all signs of contingency and those negating specific signs, with examples and implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn that some Quranic verses negate all signs of contingency collectively, while others negate specific signs such as partition or need for companionship.

Core Idea
Some verses negate all signs of contingency; others negate specific ones like partition or need.

After establishing the general principle, the author now classifies the verses into two types: those that negate all signs of contingency at once, and those that negate a particular sign. He provides examples like 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' and 'There is no god but Him, He created everything' for the first type, and the negation of child or consort for the second.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the classification introduced by 'So it follows from this that some... and some of it indicates the negation of a specific sign.'
Notice the logical connector 'fa ja'a min hadha' indicating a conclusion.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two categories: verses that negate all signs of contingency, and verses that negate a specific sign. The author lists examples for each.
3
Analyze the specific signs negated
For the second category, the author shows how negating the child implies negating partition, and negating the consort implies negating loneliness and need. Understand the logical implications.
These negations are not arbitrary; they follow from the specific verses.
4
Reflect on the overall purpose
Think about how this classification helps in understanding that God's praise in the Quran is comprehensive, covering both general and specific aspects of transcendence.

هذه الأمارة في حق الأعراض زيادة مدح لثبوت حدوثها بدلائل أخرى، فأما انتفاؤها عن الله تعالى فيوجب تمدحاً، لأنه تعالى لا يوصف بشيء آخر سواها من أمارات الحدوث بل في ابتداء الآية وانتهائها ما يوجب نفي جميع أمارات الحدوث. وتبين كمال قدرته بقوله تعالى: ﴿خَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿بَدِيعُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ﴾ ونفي الشريك. إذ في ثبوته عجز وغناه عن غيره ينفي الصاحبة ونفي تجزئه ينفي الولد، ونفى الجهل وإثبات العلم بقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ﴾ وقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ ثم أثبت الألوهية لنفسه والإله من يستحق العبادة والمحدث لا يستحقها وكذا بتسمية نفسه خالقاً أخبر عن انتفاء جميع سمات النقص عن نفسه، إذ الخالق هو المنفرد بفعله حيث لا يحتاج في تحصيله إلى غيره، والاستغناء من شرائط القدم والكمال، فجاء من هذا أن بعض ما تمدح به من الآيات يدل على انتفاء جميع أمارات الحدوث كقوله تعالى: ﴿بَدِيعُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ﴾ وقوله: ﴿لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ خَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ﴾ وقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَكِيلٌ﴾ وبعضه يدل على انتفاء أمارة مخصوصة كنفي الولد فإنه يدل على نفي التجزئ ونفي الصاحبة فإنه يدل على نفي الوحشة والحاجة إلى الاستثناء وقضاء الشهوة وإثبات العلم بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ وبقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ﴾ وكذا قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ لابد من أن يكون فيه إثبات مدح. نفيه مع وقد بينا أنه يثبت إما بإثبات صفات الكمال وإما بنفي سمات النقص، وفي الجملة على الرؤية لا يحصل لا هذا ولا ذاك مع أن الإدراك ليس برؤية وفي انتفاء الرؤية لا يحصل لما بينا، فأما بنفيه مع ثبوت الرؤية فيحصل التمدح بنفي الحدود والأطراف التي هي من أمارات الحدوث، ونفي أمارات الحدوث من دلائل الكمال إذا لم يقترن بالمنفي عنه دليل آخر من الدلائل، وفي الأعراض اقترنت بها دلائل وأمارات أخر، وكذا الكلام في الجوهر، على أن الجوهر له حدود ونهايات وجهات فلا يرد هذا الإشكال، والله الموفق.
