Vision and Perception: Negating Limits as Praise
Discusses the verse 'Vision does not perceive Him' and argues that it negates boundaries, not vision itself, thereby praising God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between vision (ru'ya) and perception (idrak), and how the negation of perception in the verse serves to negate boundaries and extremities, which are signs of contingency.

Core Idea
Negating perception of God means negating boundaries, not necessarily vision itself.

The author now delves into the meaning of the verse 'Vision does not perceive Him' (la tudrikuhu al-absar). He explains that this verse must contain praise, and that praise comes either by affirming attributes of perfection or by negating attributes of deficiency. Since perception (idrak) is not identical to vision (ru'ya), the negation of perception can be understood as negating boundaries and extremities, which are signs of contingency. However, this negation only yields perfection if no other signs of contingency remain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the logical progression: the verse must contain praise, praise is either by affirmation or negation, and perception is not vision.
The text is dense; take time to understand each step.
2
Understand the condition for praise
The author says praise is established either by affirming attributes of perfection or by negating attributes of deficiency. Apply this to the vision verse.
Note that the verse negates 'idrak', not 'ru'ya'.
3
Analyze the argument about boundaries
If vision is affirmed, then negating perception must negate boundaries and extremities, which are signs of contingency. This yields praise.
But this only works if no other signs of contingency remain.
4
Consider the objection regarding accidents
The author addresses an objection: in accidents, other signs of contingency are associated, so negating one sign does not prove perfection. The same applies to the atom (jawhar).
This shows the nuance in the argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' proves that God cannot be seen at all, even in the afterlife.
Correction: The author distinguishes between idrak (perception) and ru'ya (vision), and argues that the verse actually negates boundaries, not vision, leaving room for the possibility of seeing God without perceiving Him fully.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the negation of perception (idrak) in the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' actually negate?
A) The possibility of seeing God.
B) Boundaries and extremities.
C) God's knowledge.
D) The existence of accidents.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Vision does not perceive Him' is understood as negating boundaries and extremities, not vision itself, thus serving as praise for God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have recited 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' countless times. Did it ever make you feel distant from Allah, as if He is beyond reach? Now that you know this verse actually negates boundaries, how will you change your desire to see Him in the afterlife? Will you beg Him for that vision tonight in your sujood?
?
The author argues that negating boundaries is praise. What boundaries—physical, mental, or spiritual—have you placed on Allah in your worship? Is He confined to a mosque, a time, or a feeling? How does this lesson demand that you remove those limits from your heart?
?
If you truly believe that negating perception of Allah is praise only because it negates signs of contingency, then what does that say about your own contingency? How should this truth shape your humility and your longing for the vision that removes all limits? Commit to writing a dua asking Allah to be seen without boundaries.

The Muslim Community's Constant Profession of Tawhid
Describes the Muslim community's constant practice of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author portrays the Muslim community's unique and constant utterance of tawhid as a sign of their faith and divine favor.

Core Idea
The Muslim community's constant utterance of tawhid sets them apart from all other religions.

This passage begins by describing a distinctive practice of the Muslim community: the constant and universal profession of tawhid, the word of sincerity. The author emphasizes that Muslims, regardless of their station, call upon one another with this phrase in all circumstances, fulfilling a divine promise. This lesson examines this practice and its significance as a mark of true faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author describes the Muslims' constant utterance of tawhid and the contrast with other religions.
Pay attention to the verse cited as confirmation.

Look for the command to adhere to tawhid, the universality of its utterance, and the scriptural proof from Surah al-Fath.

Consider how this reflects the unique status of the Muslim community.
4
Reflect on the connection to faith
The constant utterance is not mere ritual; it stems from sincere belief (tasdiq). Reflect on how this aligns with the concept of kalimat al-ikhlas.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمر أتباعه بالتمسك به وإجراء كلمة الإخلاص على ألسنتهم، فنجدهم على اختلاف طبقاتهم يتنادون بها في البر والبحر والليل والصباح والرواح مصدقين لما وصفوا به في الكتب المنزلة بأنهم يملأون الأرض تهليلاً وتكبيرًا وتحميدًا وتسبيحا، وأهل سائر الأديان لا يذكرونها إلا بالفرط وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَلْزَمَهُمْ كَلِمَةَ التَّقْوَى وَكَانُوا أَحَقَّ بِهَا وَأَهْلَهَا ﴾ ثم الإيمان بصفاته ليسلم عن التشبيه وقد ورد به وأقام الدلالة عليه فيما جاء به من الكتابة نحو قوله: ﴿ وَآيَةٌ لَهُمُ اللَّيْلُ نَسْلَخُ مِنْهُ النَّهَارَ ﴾ إلى قوله: ﴿ ذَلِكَ تَقْدِيرُ الْعَزِيزِ الْعَلِيمِ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ وما ذكر في آخر سورة الحشر.
And He commanded his followers to adhere to it and to utter the word of sincerity upon their tongues, so we find them, despite their different levels, calling each other with it on land and sea, night and morning, and at all times, believing what was described about them in the revealed books that they will fill the earth with 'La ilaha illa Allah' and takbir, tahmid, and tasbih, while the people of other religions do not mention it except rarely. Commentary
This passage is a commendatory description of the Muslim community's relationship with tawhid. It begins by noting that Allah commanded the Prophet's followers to hold fast to tawhid and to constantly utter the word of sincerity (kalimat al-ikhlas). This is contrasted with other religious communities that rarely mention it. A verse from Surah al-Fath is cited as confirmation. The passage then moves to faith in Allah's attributes: the Muslim community believes in them while avoiding tashbih (anthropomorphism). The Qur'an provides proof, citing the verse about the night and day to show Allah's power without resemblance, and the clear verse 'Nothing is like Him' as the foundation for tanzih (transcendence). The passage concludes by referencing the final verses of Surah al-Hashr, which describe Allah's names and attributes while affirming transcendence.

Affirming Divine Attributes without Anthropomorphism
Establishes faith in Allah's attributes while rejecting tashbih, with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of affirming Allah's attributes while avoiding tashbih, as taught by the Qur'anic proofs.

Core Idea
True faith in Allah's attributes requires affirming them without resemblance to creation.

After discussing the constant practice of tawhid, the author turns to a fundamental theological principle: faith in Allah's attributes must be accompanied by a firm rejection of tashbih (anthropomorphism). The Qur'an provides clear proofs that establish Allah's attributes while maintaining His absolute transcendence. This lesson explores that balance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on attributes
Read the second part of the source passage carefully. Notice the author's progression: faith in attributes, safety from tashbih, then Quranic evidence.
The verse 'Nothing is like Him' is central.
2

The author cites two sets of verses: one from Surah Yasin about the night and day, and the other from Surah al-Shura. What do they establish?
One establishes Allah's power (takdir), the other His transcendence.
3
Understand tanzih and tashbih
Tanzih means declaring Allah free from any likeness to creation. The verse 'Nothing is like Him' is the foundation. Reflect on how the author balances attributes with transcendence.
This is the core of Maturidi theology.
4
Connect to the broader theme
The author links this theological principle to the earlier practice of tawhid. Both are essential aspects of the Muslim community's faith.

The initial response: the objection specifies a disputed point
The author exposes the flaw in the opponent's specification and presents an alternative interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's objection does not invalidate the verse's generality and how the believer can still use the verse to prove God's praise.

Core Idea
The objection picks a specific case that is itself disputed, not the general principle.

The opponent claims that God creating acts like cursing Himself would be foolish. However, the author points out that this claim specifies a particular case that is itself a point of disagreement. The believer in the verse can argue that the verse's generality includes all actions, and God's praise lies precisely in being the Creator of all acts, good and bad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to absorb the flow. Then read the translation to understand the argument.
Notice how the author first dismisses the objection as 'fāsid' (corrupt).
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says that creating evil acts like cursing God is foolish, using an analogy with a human who is wronged.
The author says this is invalid because it assumes the very point being disputed.
3
Understand the believer's alternative argument
The believer can use the verse's generality to show that all actions—good, bad, even those of animals—are created by God, and this is a unique praise for Him.
This shows that the objection does not apply to the verse's overall purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم: إن إيجاد شتم نفسه والافتراء عليه سفه استدلالاً بالمتعرض لذلك في الشاهد كلام فاسد، فإنه يتعين بعض فصول ما اختلفنا فيه، وللمتعلق بالآية أن يقول: أنا أستدل بها على كون ما هو مستحسن في نفسه وصواب وحكمة في ذاته من أفعال المكلفين، وكون ما ليس يوصف بالقبح والسفه من أفعالهم وجميع أفعال الحيوانات التي لا تكليف عليها ولا أمر ولا نهي مخلوقا له تعالى بقضية عموم الآية ليثبت التمدح له بما لا يتصور ذلك في غيره، وهو كونه خالقًا لفعل غيره فيحصل ما هو الغرض من الآية، فحينئذ لا يتوجه هذا الاعتراض عليه بوجه من الوجوه. ثم يقال لهم: إن ساعدناكم وقبلنا منكم هذا التعيين فلم قلتم : إن إيجاد ما قلتم سفه وقبيح ؟ فإن استدللتم بالمعترض لذلك في الشاهد قيل لهم: فبأي وصف تجمعون بين الشاهد والغائب؟ وبأي معنى قبح هذا أمن المعترض لننظر فيه أن ذلك المعنى هل وجد في حق الموجد ليحكم بالتسوية بينهما أو لم يوجد ليمتنع عن التسوية بينهما ؟ وهل هذا منكم إلا التحكم والتشهي ومجرد التشبيه للموجد بالمعترض، وأنى لكم هذا؟ ومن أين تزعمون أن ما قبح في الشاهد يقبح في الغائب؟ أليس أن الله تعالى يرى الزانيين ويعلم بحالهما ويقدر على منعهما ثم لا يمنع عن ذلك جبرًا بل يخلق القدرة على الفعل والشدة في الآلة، ولولا القدرة والشدة لما تصور منهما هذا الفعل؟ في الشاهد من يعلم بزنا عبده بأمته ويقف على ذلك ويمتنع عن منعهما عن ذلك مع القدرة عليه يعد سفيهًا، وإن لم يتول إعطاء القدرة على ذلك وتهيئة أسبابه، أفتزعمون أن الله تعالى سفيه كما أن العبد سفيه؟ أو تزعمون أن العبد ليس بسفيه كما أن الله تعالى ليس بسفيه ؟ فبأي الجوابين أجابوا انسلخوا عن الدين وإن زعموا يوصف بسبب ذلك الصنع بالسفه، والله تعالى لا يوصف به بل هو الحكيم الذي لا يجوز عليه السفه قيل لهم: بطلت تسويتكم إذ بين الشاهد والغائب وظهرت جهالتكم.
Their statement: 'Verily, causing the cursing of Himself and slander against Him is foolishness,' using as evidence the one who is wronged in the witnessed, is corrupt speech. For it specifies some aspects of what we disagree about. The one who adheres to the verse may say: I use it as evidence that what is intrinsically good, correct, and wise among the actions of the morally responsible, and what is not described as ugly and foolish among their actions, and all actions of animals that are not under obligation nor command nor prohibition, are created by Him, exalted, by virtue of the generality of the verse, to establish praise for Him in a way that is not conceivable for other than Him, namely that He is the creator of the act of another. Thus the purpose of the verse is achieved, and then this objection does not apply to him in any way. Then it is said to them: If we assist you and accept from you this specification, then why did you say that creating what you said is foolish and ugly? If you use as evidence the one who is wronged in the witnessed, it is said to them: By what description do you combine the witnessed and the unseen? And by what meaning is this ugly? Is it from the one who is wronged so that we may examine whether that meaning exists regarding the Creator to judge equality between them, or does it not exist to refrain from equality between them? Is this from you nothing but arbitrary judgment, caprice, and mere comparison of the Creator to the one who is wronged? How can you do this? From where do you claim that what is ugly in the witnessed is ugly in the unseen? Does not God see the two fornicators, know their condition, and have power to prevent them, yet He does not prevent them by compulsion, but rather He creates the ability for the act and the vehemence in the instrument? Without the ability and vehemence, this act would not be conceivable from them. In the witnessed, one who knows of the fornication of his slave with his slave woman, stops it, and refrains from preventing them despite being able to, is considered foolish, even if he did not undertake to give the ability for that and prepare its causes. Do you then claim that God is foolish as the slave is foolish? Or do you claim that the slave is not foolish as God is not foolish? Whichever of the two answers they give, they strip themselves from the religion. And if they claim that He is described due to that action as foolish, while God is not described with it, rather He is the Wise for whom foolishness is not permissible. It is said to them: Your comparison between the witnessed and the unseen is invalidated, and your ignorance has become apparent.
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that if God creates evil actions (like cursing Himself), then He would be foolish. The author first notes that the opponent's argument specifies a particular case and is flawed. The one who adheres to the verse can argue that the verse's generality includes all acts, and God's praise is precisely in creating the act of another. Then, even if we accept the specification, the author challenges the opponent: why would creating that act be considered foolish? If they rely on analogy with the witnessed (shahid), the author demands to know the basis for comparing the witnessed (humans) with the unseen (God). He points out that in the witnessed, a person who knows of wrongdoing and has the power to stop it but does not is called foolish. But God creates the ability and means for the act, yet does not forcibly prevent it. The author forces a dilemma: either God is foolish (impossible) or the slave is not foolish (which contradicts the initial claim). Thus the comparison fails, and the opponents are exposed as ignorant.

Key Terms
سفه
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the opponent's objection 'fāsid'?
A) Because it is based on a verse that is not authentic.
B) Because it specifies a particular case about which there is disagreement.
C) Because it relies on an analogy with animals.
D) Because it denies God's power to create evil.

Key Takeaway
A general proof cannot be refuted by an objection that only applies to a specific disputed case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear truth of faith because of a specific doubt or case you couldn't explain? What was that doubt? Are you still holding on to it, or will you accept the general principle that God's actions are beyond our limited analogies?
?
When you encounter an evil in the world that seems to contradict God's wisdom, do you let that specific instance shake your trust in His overall plan? How can this lesson help you hold on to the general truth that He is the Creator of all actions—even those you don't understand?
?
Consider a time when you judged God's ways based on a human comparison. How did that lead to confusion? Will you now affirm that His sovereignty is incomparable, even when you don't have all the answers?

Divine Transcendence
advanced
10 min
The deeper refutation: comparing witnessed and unseen
The author uses a concrete example and a dilemma to demolish the analogy between God and humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam method of challenging analogies between human and divine realms, and the dilemma that exposes the opponent's error.

Core Idea
Comparing God to humans without considering fundamental differences leads to an invalid analogy.

Even if we accept the opponent's specification, the author pushes further: why is creating that act considered foolish? He demands a basis for comparing God (unseen) with humans (witnessed). Using the example of fornicators, he shows that a human who does not prevent wrong is called foolish, but God creates the very ability for the act. This forces a dilemma: either God is foolish (impossible) or the human is not foolish (contradicts the initial claim). Thus the analogy collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the logical progression from acceptance of the premise to the final dilemma.
The author uses a concrete example to illustrate the flaw.
2
Identify the challenge to the analogy
The author asks: by what attribute do you compare the witnessed and unseen? This is a standard kalam move to test the validity of qiyās (analogy).
The opponent cannot provide a valid shared attribute.
3
Examine the fornicator example
In the witnessed realm, a master who knows his slaves are fornicating and does not prevent them, despite being able, is called foolish. But God creates the very ability and means for the act, so the situation is fundamentally different.
The difference lies in the role of creating ability versus merely permitting.
4
Understand the dilemma
The opponent is forced to choose: either say God is foolish (blasphemy) or say the human is not foolish (contradicts the initial claim that the act is foolish). Either way, they lose.
The author concludes that their ignorance is exposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم: إن إيجاد شتم نفسه والافتراء عليه سفه استدلالاً بالمتعرض لذلك في الشاهد كلام فاسد، فإنه يتعين بعض فصول ما اختلفنا فيه، وللمتعلق بالآية أن يقول: أنا أستدل بها على كون ما هو مستحسن في نفسه وصواب وحكمة في ذاته من أفعال المكلفين، وكون ما ليس يوصف بالقبح والسفه من أفعالهم وجميع أفعال الحيوانات التي لا تكليف عليها ولا أمر ولا نهي مخلوقا له تعالى بقضية عموم الآية ليثبت التمدح له بما لا يتصور ذلك في غيره، وهو كونه خالقًا لفعل غيره فيحصل ما هو الغرض من الآية، فحينئذ لا يتوجه هذا الاعتراض عليه بوجه من الوجوه. ثم يقال لهم: إن ساعدناكم وقبلنا منكم هذا التعيين فلم قلتم : إن إيجاد ما قلتم سفه وقبيح ؟ فإن استدللتم بالمعترض لذلك في الشاهد قيل لهم: فبأي وصف تجمعون بين الشاهد والغائب؟ وبأي معنى قبح هذا أمن المعترض لننظر فيه أن ذلك المعنى هل وجد في حق الموجد ليحكم بالتسوية بينهما أو لم يوجد ليمتنع عن التسوية بينهما ؟ وهل هذا منكم إلا التحكم والتشهي ومجرد التشبيه للموجد بالمعترض، وأنى لكم هذا؟ ومن أين تزعمون أن ما قبح في الشاهد يقبح في الغائب؟ أليس أن الله تعالى يرى الزانيين ويعلم بحالهما ويقدر على منعهما ثم لا يمنع عن ذلك جبرًا بل يخلق القدرة على الفعل والشدة في الآلة، ولولا القدرة والشدة لما تصور منهما هذا الفعل؟ في الشاهد من يعلم بزنا عبده بأمته ويقف على ذلك ويمتنع عن منعهما عن ذلك مع القدرة عليه يعد سفيهًا، وإن لم يتول إعطاء القدرة على ذلك وتهيئة أسبابه، أفتزعمون أن الله تعالى سفيه كما أن العبد سفيه؟ أو تزعمون أن العبد ليس بسفيه كما أن الله تعالى ليس بسفيه ؟ فبأي الجوابين أجابوا انسلخوا عن الدين وإن زعموا يوصف بسبب ذلك الصنع بالسفه، والله تعالى لا يوصف به بل هو الحكيم الذي لا يجوز عليه السفه قيل لهم: بطلت تسويتكم إذ بين الشاهد والغائب وظهرت جهالتكم.
Their statement: 'Verily, causing the cursing of Himself and slander against Him is foolishness,' using as evidence the one who is wronged in the witnessed, is corrupt speech. For it specifies some aspects of what we disagree about. The one who adheres to the verse may say: I use it as evidence that what is intrinsically good, correct, and wise among the actions of the morally responsible, and what is not described as ugly and foolish among their actions, and all actions of animals that are not under obligation nor command nor prohibition, are created by Him, exalted, by virtue of the generality of the verse, to establish praise for Him in a way that is not conceivable for other than Him, namely that He is the creator of the act of another. Thus the purpose of the verse is achieved, and then this objection does not apply to him in any way. Then it is said to them: If we assist you and accept from you this specification, then why did you say that creating what you said is foolish and ugly? If you use as evidence the one who is wronged in the witnessed, it is said to them: By what description do you combine the witnessed and the unseen? And by what meaning is this ugly? Is it from the one who is wronged so that we may examine whether that meaning exists regarding the Creator to judge equality between them, or does it not exist to refrain from equality between them? Is this from you nothing but arbitrary judgment, caprice, and mere comparison of the Creator to the one who is wronged? How can you do this? From where do you claim that what is ugly in the witnessed is ugly in the unseen? Does not God see the two fornicators, know their condition, and have power to prevent them, yet He does not prevent them by compulsion, but rather He creates the ability for the act and the vehemence in the instrument? Without the ability and vehemence, this act would not be conceivable from them. In the witnessed, one who knows of the fornication of his slave with his slave woman, stops it, and refrains from preventing them despite being able to, is considered foolish, even if he did not undertake to give the ability for that and prepare its causes. Do you then claim that God is foolish as the slave is foolish? Or do you claim that the slave is not foolish as God is not foolish? Whichever of the two answers they give, they strip themselves from the religion. And if they claim that He is described due to that action as foolish, while God is not described with it, rather He is the Wise for whom foolishness is not permissible. It is said to them: Your comparison between the witnessed and the unseen is invalidated, and your ignorance has become apparent.
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that if God creates evil actions (like cursing Himself), then He would be foolish. The author first notes that the opponent's argument specifies a particular case and is flawed. The one who adheres to the verse can argue that the verse's generality includes all acts, and God's praise is precisely in creating the act of another. Then, even if we accept the specification, the author challenges the opponent: why would creating that act be considered foolish? If they rely on analogy with the witnessed (shahid), the author demands to know the basis for comparing the witnessed (humans) with the unseen (God). He points out that in the witnessed, a person who knows of wrongdoing and has the power to stop it but does not is called foolish. But God creates the ability and means for the act, yet does not forcibly prevent it. The author forces a dilemma: either God is foolish (impossible) or the slave is not foolish (which contradicts the initial claim). Thus the comparison fails, and the opponents are exposed as ignorant.

Key Terms
سفه
شاهد
غائب
تشبيه
مقايسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates evil, He must be evil just like a human who does evil.
Correction: God's creation of evil is not comparable to a human doing evil because God creates the very ability and means, whereas humans only act within that ability.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show the difference between God and humans?
A) A judge who punishes a thief.
B) A master who does not prevent his slaves from fornicating.
C) A king who orders a killing.
D) A father who disciplines his son.

Key Takeaway
An analogy between God and humans is invalid unless a common attribute can be established, and the fornicator example shows that no such attribute exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a sin or a moral failing in your life by comparing yourself to someone else, ignoring the fundamental differences in your circumstances or responsibilities? How does this lesson challenge you to stop making such comparisons, especially regarding God's judgment?
?
The opponent in the lesson used a faulty analogy to accuse God of foolishness. Are there any areas in your life where you secretly question God's wisdom or justice because you are measuring Him by human standards? Will you repent of that today and submit to His absolute wisdom, even when you don't understand?
?
God does not forcibly prevent evil, yet He is not foolish for it. How does this truth about divine wisdom and freedom reshape your reaction to the evil you see in the world? Instead of accusing God, what practical step can you take to trust Him more when you encounter injustice?

The Reason for Distinction between Shahid and Gha'ib
Author begins exposing the reason for distinguishing shahid and gha'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's method of exposing opponents' error by examining the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen, and why this argument remains incomplete.

Core Idea
The distinction between witnessed and unseen hinges on evidence of perfection.

In this passage, the author begins to uncover the rationale behind distinguishing the witnessed from the unseen. He aims to show that opponents cling to superficial similarities while ignoring deeper realities. The discussion starts with the case of slander in the visible world, but the argument is cut short.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the flow of the argument before analysis.
The passage ends mid-sentence at 'wa-'asa' (and perhaps...).
2
Identify the author's goal
The author states he wants to 'uncovers the meaning that necessitates the distinction' to show the opponents' ignorance. This is the opening of a new argument.
Look for the phrase 'ثم تكشف عن المعنى الموجب للتفرقة'.
3
Analyze the example of slander
The author gives an example from the witnessed world: a slandered person lacks automatic evidence of innocence. Listeners may recognize the slanderer's falsehood, but there is no intrinsic proof of innocence.
This contrasts with the divine case, which is not yet completed.
4
Consider the incomplete argument
The passage stops at 'wa-'asa' (and perhaps...). Based on context, the author likely intended to contrast this with God, whose perfection is self-evident and needs no external proof.
The full argument would complete the distinction between shahid and gha'ib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكشف عن المعنى الموجب للتفرقة بين الشاهد والغائب ليظهر للمسترشد غباوة الخصوم وجهلهم بالحقائق وتمسكهم بالظواهر التي لا طائل تحتها ولا محصول وراءها فنقول: إن المعترض في الشاهد للافتراء عليه الحامل غيره على شتمه لم يقم في العقول دلالة تنزهه عما قرن به ونسب إليه من الرذيلة، وإنه يظهر لدى السامعين كذب الشاتم له المفتري عليه ويلحق به عار كذبه وبهته، وعسى
Then he uncovers the meaning that necessitates the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen, so that the seeker of guidance may see the stupidity of the opponents and their ignorance of realities and their clinging to superficialities that have no benefit and no substance behind them. So we say: Indeed, the one who is wronged in the witnessed by slander, the one who causes another to insult him, there has not been established in the intellects an evidence that exonerates him from the vice that is associated with him and attributed to him, and it becomes apparent to the listeners the falsehood of the one who insults him and slanders him, and the shame of his lie and false accusation attaches to him, and perhaps...
Source pages p. 861

Commentary
This passage is a new section where the author intends to uncover the reason that necessitates a distinction between the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). The stated goal is to expose the opponents' ignorance and their reliance on meaningless externals. The author begins explaining: in the witnessed world, when a person is slandered and someone else insults him, there is no definitive intellectual proof that automatically exonerates the slandered person from the vice attributed to him. The falsehood of the slanderer may become apparent to listeners, and the shame of the lie attaches to the slanderer. However, the argument is incomplete as the passage cuts off at 'wa-'asa' (and perhaps...). The full argument would presumably contrast this with the case of God, where His perfection is self-evident. This distinction undermines any simple analogy between human and divine actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that the lack of evidence for innocence in a slandered person is analogous to God, i.e., God also lacks evident innocence.
Correction: The author implies that God, unlike a slandered person, has self-evident perfection; the distinction between witnessed and unseen is based on the presence or absence of such evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is lacking in the case of a slandered person in the witnessed world?
A) A clear intellectual proof of his innocence.
B) The ability to defend himself.
C) The capacity to forgive.
D) The truth of the slander.

Key Takeaway
The author begins to argue that the witnessed world lacks automatic evidence of innocence, a key contrast with the self-evident perfection of God in the unseen realm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author criticizes the opponents for clinging to superficialities (ظواهر). In what area of your life are you settling for appearances rather than seeking the deeper reality of God's transcendence and perfection?
?
This passage implies that God's perfection is so evident that it requires no proof. Have you ever treated God's attributes as if they need to be proven by worldly evidence? How can you train your heart to see the self-evident signs of His majesty?

The Foolishness of Slanderers
Al-Nasafi explains the foolishness of slanderers and the eternal shame they incur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why slanderers are considered foolish and how their actions bring eternal shame upon themselves and their descendants.

Core Idea
Slanderers bring eternal disgrace upon themselves, not just their victims.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi describes the severe consequences of slander: it not only harms the victim but also permanently stains the slanderer and his offspring. He argues that such behavior is the ultimate foolishness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to understand the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the consequences of slander
Note how slander affects the victim's rank and the permanence of the disgrace.
The disgrace may not be washed away by time and persists across generations.
3
Understand why the slanderer is foolish
The slanderer exposes himself to eternal shame and reaches a limit of foolishness that no other fool can match.
4
Reflect on the eternal nature of the shame
Consider the statement that the stain remains upon descendants through ages, emphasizing the severity.
This shows the gravity of slander in Islamic ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السامعون أو أكثرهم أن يصدقوا المفتري فيما افترى فتحط رتبة المشتوم في أعينهم وتنخفض درجته لديهم ويلتحق به عار ربما لا يغسله عنه مرور الأيام وتطاول مدد الزمان بل تبقى وصمة ذلك على الأعقاب على مر الدهور والأحقاب فكان المعترض لذلك متعرضاً لما يكسبه على الدهر ويجلب إليه وإلى ذريته شين الأبد، ولا شك أن من هذا غب فعله وعاقبة صنيعه بلغ في السفه غاية لا يدانيه سفيه فيها وإن بالغ في سفهه وتمادى في طغيانه. السفه فأما الله تعالى فقد أقام على براءته وتنزيه ذاته عما ينسبه إليه المفترون ويصفه به المبطلون أوضح الدلائل وأقواها، ونصب لذلك أرفع الحجج وأعلاها وجعل العقول بفطرها متسارعة إلى تسفيه من لم يعد المعتقد لذلك غاية في والغباوة والمتفوه به نهاية في الجهل والحماقة فكان الله تعالى بإيجاد ذلك عند اختيار الشقي فعله موجدًا أما يوجب إلحاق عار الكذب وشين السفه بما علم منه العدول عن طاعته والإيثار على ذلك لمشاقته وعداوته، فمن جعل الموجد لما يوجب إلحاق النقائض والرذائل يعدوه سفيها اعتبارًا بمن يتعرض لإلحاق العار بنفسه فهو الجاهل بالدلائل غير عارف بالحقائق مع أن الله تعالى في إيجاد الكفر حكمة بالغة نبنيها على الاستقصاء إذا انتهينا إلى بيان دفع القبح عن إيجاد القبيح - إن شاء الله تعالى.
The listeners, or most of them, believe the fabricator in what he fabricated, so the rank of the reviled person falls in their eyes and his status is lowered among them, and he is overtaken by a disgrace that perhaps days passing and long durations of time do not wash away from him, rather the stain of that remains upon the descendants through the passage of ages and periods. So the objector to that exposes himself to what earns him eternal shame and brings upon himself and his offspring the ignominy of eternity. There is no doubt that whoever does this, the consequence of his action and the outcome of his deed reaches in foolishness a limit that no fool can rival, even if he goes to extremes in his foolishness and persists in his transgression. Foolishness. As for Allah the Exalted, He has established upon His innocence and the transcendence of His essence from what the fabricators ascribe to Him and the falsifiers describe Him with, the clearest and strongest proofs, and He has set up for that the highest and loftiest arguments, and He made the intellects by their innate nature quick to deem foolish one who does not consider the one who believes that to be extreme in stupidity and the one who utters it to be ultimate in ignorance and recklessness. So Allah the Exalted, by creating that when the wretched one chooses his action, is the creator of something that necessitates attaching the disgrace of lying and the ignominy of foolishness to one from whom He knew turning away from His obedience and preferring that out of opposition and enmity toward Him. So whoever makes the creator of what necessitates attaching deficiencies and vices to Himself foolish, by analogy with one who exposes himself to attaching disgrace to himself, is ignorant of the proofs, not knowledgeable of realities, while Allah the Exalted in creating disbelief has a profound wisdom which we will build comprehensively when we reach the explanation of repelling the ugliness from creating the ugly - if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 862

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the foolishness (safah) of those who attribute falsehood to God and the wisdom (hikmah) of God's creation of disbelief (kufr). The author first describes how slanderers cause disgrace to the person they slander, and warns that the slanderer brings eternal shame upon himself and his descendants. He then states that no one can match such a person in foolishness. Moving to God, he asserts that God has established clear proofs of His innocence (barā'ah) and transcendence (tanzīh) from what slanderers ascribe. Intellects naturally deem as foolish anyone who does not consider the one who believes such things to be extremely stupid. The author then addresses the key point: God's creation of disbelief at the choice of the wretched person (al-shaqī) does not imply foolishness in God. Rather, God creates an action that necessarily attaches the disgrace of lying and foolishness to the person who chooses disobedience out of enmity. If someone analogizes from human actions and claims that the creator of what brings defects is foolish, he is ignorant. God's creation of disbelief has a profound wisdom (ḥikmah bālighah) which the author promises to explain later, in a future discussion on repelling ugliness from creating the ugly (dafʿ al-qubḥ ʿan ījād al-qabīḥ).

Key Terms
سفه
مفتري
عار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think slander only harms the victim.
Correction: The passage shows slander brings eternal shame on the slanderer and his offspring.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence for the slanderer?
A) He is praised for his cleverness.
B) He incurs eternal shame and is foolish.
C) He is forgiven over time.
D) He gains honor for his skill.

Key Takeaway
Slanderers are the worst fools because they bring eternal disgrace not only on their victims but also on themselves and their descendants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed a rumor about someone without verifying it? How can knowing that slanderers reach 'the ultimate limit of foolishness' change your reaction to gossip you hear today?
?
This passage says disgrace from slander can remain on descendants. Do you consider how your words might affect not only your own reputation but also your children's? What would it take for you to guard your tongue from this?
?
If you have engaged in slander in the past, what steps can you take to repair the harm and seek forgiveness, knowing that time alone may not wash away the stain?

God's Wisdom in Creating Disbelief
Al-Nasafi defends God's wisdom in creating disbelief and rebuts the accusation of foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's creation of disbelief is not foolish but has profound wisdom, and that attributing foolishness to God is itself ignorance.

Core Idea
God's creation of disbelief is wise; calling it foolish is ignorant.

Imam al-Nasafi now turns to defend God's transcendence against slanderers who claim that God's creation of disbelief implies foolishness. He argues that God has provided clear proofs of His innocence and that intellects naturally deem such critics as foolish. Then he explains that God's creation of disbelief at the choice of the wretched is wise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument's structure.
2
Note God's proofs of innocence
God has established clear proofs of His transcendence and innocence from falsehood.
These proofs are the strongest and most evident.
3
Understand the analogy regarding shame
God creates the act of disbelief when the sinner chooses it, and this act attaches shame to the sinner, not to God.
Comparing this to a human who brings disgrace on himself is invalid.
4
Recognize the promised wisdom
The author promises to elaborate on the profound wisdom of creating disbelief later.
This shows the author's systematic approach.

Rather, God creates an action that necessarily attaches the disgrace of lying and foolishness to the person who chooses disobedience out of enmity. If someone analogizes from human actions and claims that the creator of what brings defects is foolish, he is ignorant. God's creation of disbelief has a profound wisdom (ḥikmah bālighah) which the author promises to explain later, in a future discussion on repelling ugliness from creating the ugly (dafʿ al-qubḥ ʿan ījād al-qabīḥ).

Key Terms
سفه
حكمة
براءة
تنزيه
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God creating disbelief implies God is foolish.
Correction: The passage argues that such a belief is itself foolish and ignorant, and God's creation has wisdom.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the one who claims God is foolish for creating disbelief?
A) He is wise.
B) He is ignorant of proofs.
C) He is following a higher wisdom.
D) He is correct.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of disbelief is wise and does not imply foolishness; those who claim otherwise are ignorant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever secretly blamed God for something that went wrong in your life, even knowing the theological arguments? What does your heart need to learn from al-Nasafi's defense of God's wisdom?
?
The passage says intellects naturally deem foolish anyone who claims God is foolish. Yet when you face personal tragedy, do you ever entertain the thought that God's actions are senseless? What would it take for you to truly submit to God's wisdom even when you don't understand it?

The Meaning of 'Khalq' in the Verse 'Aḥsanu l-Khāliqīn'
Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite interpretation of the verse 'أحسن الخالقين'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi interprets the word 'khalq' in the verse 'the best of creators' as estimation and giving form, not literal creation, and how this preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God alone is the Creator; 'khalq' in the verse means shaping, not bringing into existence.

This passage addresses a common Mu'tazilite proof-text used to claim that creatures can be called 'creators'. Imam Nasafi shows that the verse must be understood metaphorically, otherwise it contradicts the clear principle that only God creates. He provides linguistic and poetic evidence to demonstrate that 'khalq' often means estimation and shaping, and that applying the term 'creator' to creatures is only permissible in a figurative, paired sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents and their claim
Read the first part of the passage to see who is using the verse 'أحسن الخالقين' as evidence. Note that early Basrians and Baghdadis are mentioned first, then later Basrians.
The opponents are Mu'tazilites who argue that creatures can be called creators based on this verse.
2
Understand the refutation: 'khalq' means taqdīr and taṣwīr
Imam Nasafi explains that the word 'khalq' in this context refers to estimation (تقدير) and giving form (تصوير), not invention and bringing into existence. This is the key to resolving the apparent contradiction.
3
Examine the poetic evidence from Zuhayr
Read the line of poetry cited: 'ولأنت تفري ما خلقت وبعض القوم يخلق ثم لا يفري'. This shows that 'khalq' can mean shaping or planning, not creating ex nihilo.
The poet uses 'khalq' to mean 'estimate' or 'design', as in cutting leather after measuring.
4
Grasp the principle: servant is fā'il but not khāliq
Imam Nasafi asserts that the servant is a doer (فاعل) but not a creator (خالق) in the absolute sense. Only God is the Creator.
5
Appreciate the analogies for metaphorical usage
The author gives two analogies: 'Sunnah of the two 'Umars' for Abu Bakr and 'Umar, and 'the two moons' for the sun and moon. These show that terms can be applied metaphorically when paired, even if not alone.
This illustrates that calling a servant 'creator' is only permissible when paired with God, not independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة في آية فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين: ثم المتعلق من أوائل البصرية وجميع البغدادية منهم بقوله تعالى أحسن الخالقين ليس بصحيح لمساعدتهم إيانا على أن من سوى الله تعالى ليس بخالق ويستحيل وصف من سواه به ولو تعلق بذلك متأخرو أهل البصرة المجوزون ذلك يقال لهم: الخلق يذكر ويراد به التقدير والتصوير دون الاختراع والإيجاد، وعلى هذا التقدير يجوز أن يسمى العبد خالقا قال قائلهم: ولأنت تفري ما خلقت وبعض القوم يخلق ثم لا يفري فكانت الآية محمولة عليه دفعًا للتناقض عن الآيات والدلائل، على أن العبد يكون فاعلاً ولا يكون خالقًا، والله تعالى هو الخالق، ويجوز إطلاق اسم الخالق على العبد وإن لم يكن خالقا إذا ضم إلى الخالق كما يقال: سنة العمرين في أبي بكر رضي الله عنهما – وإن كان أبو بكر لا يسمى عند الانفراد عمر، وكذا يقال للشمس والقمر قمران قال القائل: أخذنا بآفاق السماء عليكم لنا قمراها والنجوم الطوالع وإن كان اسم القمر لا يطلق على الشمس عند الانفراد فكذا هذا، والله الموفق.
Discussion on the verse 'So blessed be God, the best of creators': Then the attachment of the early Basrians and all the Baghdadis among them to His statement, exalted, 'the best of creators' is not correct, because they agree with us that anyone other than God, exalted, is not a creator, and it is impossible to describe anyone other than Him with it. And if the later Basrians, who permit that, were to attach themselves to it, it is said to them: 'al-khalq' is mentioned and intended by it estimation (al-taqdīr) and giving form (al-taṣwīr), without invention (al-ikhtirā') and bringing into existence (al-ījād). And according to this estimation, it is permissible for the servant to be called a creator. Their poet said: 'And you cut what you create, while some people create but do not cut.' So the verse is carried upon that to remove contradiction from the verses and proofs. The point is that the servant is a doer (fā'il) but not a creator (khāliq), and God, exalted, is the Creator. And it is permissible to apply the name 'creator' to the servant, even if he is not a creator, when joined to the Creator, just as one says 'the Sunnah of the two 'Umars' regarding Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with them both – even though Abu Bakr is not called 'Umar when alone. And likewise, the sun and moon are called 'the two moons', as the poet said: 'We took upon you the horizons of the sky; for us are its two moons and the shining stars.' And even though the name 'moon' is not applied to the sun when alone, it is likewise. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 870

Commentary
This passage is a discussion (مناقشة) of the Quranic verse 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين). Imam Nasafi begins by stating that the interpretation of this verse by the early Basrian Mu'tazilites and all the Baghdadis – who use it as evidence that creatures can be called creators – is incorrect, because they themselves agree that no one besides God is a creator, so they cannot consistently use this verse to prove that. If later Basrians who permit calling creatures 'creators' try to use the verse, they are told that the word 'khalq' in this context means estimation (تقدير) and giving form (تصوير), not invention and bringing into existence. In this sense, it is permissible to call the servant a 'creator' metaphorically. The author then cites the poet Zuhayr's line: 'And you cut what you create, while some people create but do not cut' (ولأنت تفري ما خلقت وبعض القوم يخلق ثم لا يفري), showing that 'khalq' here means 'estimation' or 'shaping'. The verse is therefore interpreted in this metaphorical sense to avoid contradictions with other verses and proofs. The fundamental principle is that the servant is a doer (فاعل) but not a creator (خالق) in the absolute sense. However, it is permissible to call the servant a 'creator' when the term is paired with God, similar to the expression 'Sunnah of the two 'Umars' for Abu Bakr and 'Umar – even though Abu Bakr alone is not called 'Umar. Similarly, the sun and moon can be called 'the two moons' (قمران) in a poetic context, even though 'moon' is not applied to the sun alone. This illustrates that such expressions are metaphorical and do not imply actual participation in the attribute of creation.

Key Terms
خلق
خالق
تقدير
تصوير
اختراع
إيجاد
فاعل
سنة العمرين
قمران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'أحسن الخالقين' implies that creatures can be creators in the real sense.
Correction: Imam Nasafi explains that 'khalq' here means estimation and giving form, not invention and bringing into existence; thus the verse is metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, what is the meaning of 'khalq' in the verse 'أحسن الخالقين' as interpreted for the later Basrians?
A) Invention and bringing into existence.
B) Estimation and giving form.
C) Destruction and annihilation.
D) Worship and obedience.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'أحسن الخالقين' does not grant creatures the attribute of creation; rather, 'khalq' here refers to shaping and estimating, and calling a servant 'creator' is only metaphorical when paired with God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the word 'create' for yourself or others—like creating art or ideas—do you truly recognize that only God brings things into existence from nothing? How can this lesson change the way you speak about your own abilities?
?
The Mu'tazilites clung to a verse that seemed to support their view, but they misunderstood its meaning. Are there any verses or hadith you interpret based on your own assumptions rather than linguistic and theological context? Will you commit to seeking deeper understanding before forming conclusions?
?
This lesson shows that even subtle differences in word meanings can affect our theology. In your daily life, are you careful to distinguish between what is uniquely God’s and what is human? What specific change will you make to guard your heart and tongue from blurring that line?

The Mu'tazilite Path to Anthropomorphism
Shows how the Mu'tazilite identification of act and object implies resemblance between divine and human actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite identification of act and object leads to an unacceptable resemblance between divine and human actions.

Core Idea
When act and object are one, God's act and human act become identical.

The author now presents a fourth argument against the Mu'tazilite position. He demonstrates that their own principles force them into a position they themselves reject: anthropomorphism (tashbih). By identifying the act with its object, they erase the distinction between the Creator’s act and the creature’s act, making human creation resemble divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite principle
Note the Mu'tazilite view that the act (fi'l) and the object (maf'ul) are one and the same.
This is a key tenet that drives the argument.
2
See the shared description of acts
Both God's act and the servant's act are described as bringing something from non-existence into existence.
3
Realize the consequence
If act and object are identical, then a movement caused by God and a movement caused by a servant are indistinguishable, leading to anthropomorphism.
The author uses the example of a tremor (حركة المرتعش) to illustrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا: إنه يؤدي إلى وقوع التشابه بين فعل القديم وفعل الحادث، فإن فعل الله تعالى لما كان إخراجًا من العدم إلى الوجود، وفعل العبد كذلك، كان كل واحد من الفعلين شبيه صاحبه خصوصا على أصلهم فإن عندهم الفعل والمفعول واحد فحركة المرتعش فعل الله ومفعوله وحركة العبد فعل العبد ومفعوله، فلا فرق بين حركة وبين حركة بمعنى من المعاني فكان فعل العبد وخلقه كخلق الله تعالى.
And we say: It leads to the occurrence of resemblance (tashabuh) between the act of the Eternal (al-Qadim) and the act of the temporal (al-hadith). For since the act of God Almighty is bringing forth from non-existence into existence, and the act of the servant is likewise, each one of the two acts resembles its counterpart. Especially according to their principle, for with them the act and the object are one. So the movement of the trembling person is God's act and His object, and the movement of the servant is the servant's act and his object. There is no difference between a movement and a movement in any sense. So the act and creation of the servant becomes like the creation of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 892

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth argument: the Mu'tazilite view leads to tashabuh (resemblance or anthropomorphism) between God's act and the servant's act. The author explains that both God's act and the human act are described as bringing something from non-existence to existence. According to the Mu'tazilite principle that the act (fi'l) and the object (maf'ul) are identical, there is no distinction between a movement caused by God (e.g., the tremor of a sick person) and a movement caused by a human agent. Both are equally acts and objects of their respective agents. Therefore, the human act of creation becomes exactly like God's act of creation, which is a form of tashbih (anthropomorphism) — a position the Mu'tazilites themselves reject. The author concludes that their doctrine inevitably leads to this unacceptable consequence.

Key Terms
تشابه
فعل قديم
فعل حادث
فعل ومفعول واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is accusing the Mu'tazilites of explicitly believing in anthropomorphism.
Correction: The author is showing that their principles logically entail it, whether they intend it or not.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the consequence of the Mu'tazilite view that the act and object are one (الفعل والمفعول واحد)?
A) God's action is unique and incomparable
B) There is no difference between God's act and the servant's act
C) Human actions are not real
D) God does not act in the world

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite identification of act and object forces them into the very anthropomorphism they deny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you reject anthropomorphism—but have you ever assumed that your understanding of God's actions is just like your own actions, only bigger? Take a moment: in what specific ways might you be imagining God's 'bringing into existence' as similar to human creation?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were defending God's transcendence, but their logic trapped them in tashbih. Are there any beliefs you hold that you have never examined for hidden contradictions? Commit to identifying one such belief today.
?
If the servant's act of bringing something from non-existence to existence is exactly like God's act, then what separates the Creator from the creature? Read the source again: where do you see a danger of blurring that line in your own thinking about what you can achieve without God?

Why Creating Evil Is Not Evil for God
Nasafi proves that God's creation of evil is not evil, due to divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating what appears evil is not evil for God, based on the proofs of God's exclusive creative power and His absolute wisdom.

Core Idea
God's wisdom makes all His acts wise, even if humans don't see it.

Nasafi addresses a key Mu'tazilite objection: if God creates evil, He is evil. He counters with two foundational proofs: that only God invents, and that God is absolutely wise. This passage shows that human labeling cannot constrain divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Notice the two proof phrases starting with 'لأن الدليل'.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two arguments Nasafi uses: (1) only God creates (invents), so everything is His creation; (2) God is wise, so all His acts are wise. Write them in your own words.
3
Understand the refutation
See how Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila are ignorant of the true nature of wisdom and that their intellects are limited. Reflect on the difference between human and divine wisdom.
This refutation relies on the two proofs, not on a direct denial of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا من تمسك بالدليلين أعنى ما مر أن العبد له فعل وليست له قدرة الاختراع وعرف فساد قولهم إن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وإيجاد السفه سفه لأن الدليل الذي دل أن لا اختراع إلا من الله تعالى يوجب أن كل حادث كان باختراعه، وقيام الدليل على كونه عز وجل حكيمًا يوجب أنه تعالى في جميع ما أوجده حكيم، قبيحاً كان ذلك الموجد أو حسنًا سفها كان أو حكمة، وعرف بذلك أن ما يظنه المعتزلة حكمًا وسفها ليس كما ظنوا ، وأنهم جاهلون بحقيقة الحكمة والسفه إذ الجهل عليهم جائز ممكن والخطأ على ما أقمنا من الدليل ممتنع ، والحاصل أنهم يحكمون بكون ما لا اطلاع لهم على وجه الحكمة بالسفه، وهذا جهل إذ عقولهم قاصرة عن كثير من الحكم البشرية، ودعوى الاطلاع على جميع الحكم الربوبية دعوى ظهر فسادها للبداهة، والله الموفق.
And likewise, whoever adheres to the two proofs, namely what has passed: that the servant has an act but does not have the power of invention, and knows the falsity of their statement that 'creating evil is evil' and 'creating foolishness is foolishness', because the proof that there is no invention except from God, may He be exalted, necessitates that every originated thing is by His invention; and the establishment of the proof that He, may He be exalted and glorified, is wise necessitates that He, exalted, is wise in all that He creates, whether that which He creates is considered evil or good, foolish or wise. And by this it is known that what the Mu'tazila imagine to be wisdom and foolishness is not as they imagine, and that they are ignorant of the reality of wisdom and foolishness, since ignorance is possible and permissible for them, while error is impossible according to the proof we have established. The upshot: they judge as foolishness that whose aspect of wisdom they have no knowledge of. This is ignorance, since their intellects are deficient with respect to many human wisdoms, and the claim to know all divine wisdoms is a claim whose falsity is evident to the mind. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 924

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) that creating evil is not evil, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that it is. The author presents two proofs (dalilayn). First, the proof that no act of invention (ikhtira') occurs except from God alone, which means every originated being is His creation. Second, the proof that God is wise (hakim) absolutely, which means that all His acts are wise, regardless of whether humans label them evil or foolish. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's labeling is based on ignorance: they do not grasp the wisdom behind divine acts. Human intellects are limited even with respect to human wisdoms, let alone divine wisdoms. Thus, their claim of access to all divine wisdoms is patently false. The passage ends with a call for divine assistance.

Key Terms
إيجاد
القبيح
السفه
اختراع
حكيم
حكمة
بداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila believe that creating what humans call 'evil' is itself evil, and that God is therefore unjust or foolish.
Correction: Since God is wise by definition, all His acts are wise, even if humans do not perceive the wisdom. Human labeling does not change the nature of divine acts.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the Mu'tazilite claim that creating evil is evil?
A) Because evil does not exist in God's creation
B) Because the proof that God is wise entails that all His acts are wise, regardless of human opinion
C) Because humans have no free will
D) Because the Mu'tazila have no proof

Key Takeaway
Because God alone creates and is absolutely wise, every divine act is wise, and human judgments of evil or foolishness are irrelevant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial that seems meaningless or unjust, do you, even for a moment, accuse God of foolishness in your heart? What would it mean to truly trust that He is wise even when you see no wisdom?
?
You claim to believe God is wise, but do you secretly resent certain divine decrees, such as the death of a loved one or a personal failure? How can you train your heart to accept that what you call 'evil' may be wisdom beyond your grasp?
?
Do you live as if God owes you an explanation for His decrees? How can you cultivate the humility to admit that your intellect is deficient even in human matters, let alone divine—and what practical step will you take this week to surrender your need to understand?

Divine Transcendence

Evil and Foolishness Defined by Divine Command
Nasafi defines qabih and sufh and distinguishes divine from human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni theological definition of evil (qabih) and foolishness (sufh) as actions prohibited by divine command, and how this differs from the Mu'tazilite view based on benefit.

Core Idea
Evil is not inherent in actions; it is what God prohibits.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents the Sunni position that moral categories like 'evil' and 'foolishness' are defined by divine command, not by human reasoning or benefit. He uses a striking example—God not preventing a slave from committing adultery—to illustrate that God's actions are not subject to the same standards as human actions. This establishes a fundamental distinction between the witnessed realm (shahid) and the absent (gha'ib).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition, example, distinction, and refutation of the Mu'tazila.
The passage opens with the author's intention to volunteer a response.
2
Identify the core definition
Find the sentence stating what al-qabih and al-sufh are according to the majority of Sunni theologians. Note the key term 'al-nahy' (prohibition).
3
Analyze the example
Consider why God's not preventing the slave's adultery is not evil. Understand that because God has no prohibition on Him, His actions cannot be judged by human standards.
This example highlights the difference between shahid (witnessed) and gha'ib (absent).
4
Contrast with Mu'tazilite view
The passage ends with the Mu'tazilite position. Identify their criteria for wisdom (benefit) and why Nasafi rejects their equation of shahid and gha'ib.
The Mu'tazila hold that what is evil in the witnessed is evil in the absent without regard to context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول متبرعين في ذلك على الخصوم وفاتحين طريق العلم على المسترشدين، وهو أن القبيح والسفه عند الجمهور من متكلمي أهل الحديث هو ما ورد عنه النهي ولا نهي ولا حد على الله تعالى فلا يكون فيما يفعل مرتكبًا نهيًا ولا مجاوزا حدًا حد له ولا رسماً رسم له، فلم يكن فيما يفعل سفيها ولا فعله قبيحًا، ولهذا لم يكن تركه عبده يزني بأمته مع القدرة على المنع وتخليقه في الآلة الشدة والقوة علمه أنه مع يزني بأمته قبيحًا ولا سفها، وإن كان ذلك في الشاهد قبيحًا لكون الشاهد تحت أمر خالقه ونهيه واستحالة ذلك على الله تعالى. وبهذا يفرقون بين الشاهد والغائب، ويأبون تسوية المعتزلة بين الشاهد والغائب وتعليقهم وجه الحكمة على النفع إذ من مذهب المعتزلة أن ما قبح في الشاهد يقبح في الغائب من غير نظر إلى المعنى ويقولون: لابد لكون الفعل حكمة من نفع يوجد فيه إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه، ويتعلقون أن في الشاهد هكذا.
Then we say, volunteering in that against the opponents and opening the path of knowledge for those seeking guidance: that evil and foolishness according to the majority of the speculative theologians of the people of hadith is that which is prohibited by divine command. There is no prohibition nor limit upon God, the Exalted, so He is not, in what He does, committing a prohibition nor exceeding a limit that was set for Him nor a drawing that was drawn for Him. Therefore He is not foolish in what He does, nor is His act evil. For this reason, His not preventing His slave from committing adultery with his slave-woman, despite having the power to prevent it and having created in the tool strength and power, and knowing that he will commit adultery with his slave-woman, is not considered evil nor foolish, even though that would be evil in the witnessed world because the witnessed is under the command of his Creator and His prohibition, while that is impossible for God. And by this they distinguish between the witnessed and the absent, and they reject the Mu'tazila's equating the witnessed and the absent and their condition for wisdom being benefit, since it is from the doctrine of the Mu'tazila that what is evil in the witnessed is evil in the absent without consideration of meaning, and they say: for an act to be wise, it must involve a benefit existing either for the doer or for another, and whatever lacks benefit is foolish, and they hold that in the witnessed it is so.
Source pages p. 925

Commentary
This passage provides the positive Sunni definition of evil (qabih) and foolishness (sufh). It begins by stating that the author is volunteering a response to the opponents and opening the way for students. The definition: evil and foolishness are what have been prohibited by divine command (nahy). Since God has no prohibition or limit imposed on Him, He cannot be said to commit an evil or foolish act. An example is given: God's not stopping His slave from committing adultery with his own slave-woman, despite God's power and knowledge. This would be evil in human terms (al-shahid) because humans are bound by commands and prohibitions, but for God it is not evil because God is not subject to any command. This illustrates the principle of differentiating between the witnessed realm (al-shahid) and the absent (al-gha'ib), a key refutation of the Mu'tazilite method. The Mu'tazila hold that what is evil in the witnessed is evil in the absent without considering the different context, and they make hikmah dependent on benefit (naf'). The author criticizes this and affirms the Sunni position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions can be judged by the same moral standards as human actions.
Correction: God is not subject to any command or prohibition, so His actions are beyond human moral categories.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni position presented, what makes an action evil or foolish?
A) The presence of harm or lack of benefit
B) Being prohibited by divine command
C) Human rational judgment
D) The absence of wisdom

Key Takeaway
Evil and foolishness are defined by divine prohibition; God's actions are beyond such categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says God's not preventing a man from adultery is not evil. Does this unsettle your sense of justice? Can you trust that His wisdom is beyond your comprehension, even when you see suffering or apparent injustice? What is one specific area where you need to surrender your moral judgment to His?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded that every act of God have a benefit. In your heart, do you make the same demand of God before you obey? Will you abandon that condition and obey simply because He commands, without needing to see the benefit?

Nasafi's Refutation of Mu'tazilite Analogy
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila by citing divine actions that would be evil in witness but good from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine actions cannot be judged by human moral standards, and how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila's analogy between the witnessed and unseen worlds.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by human categories of good and evil.

In this passage, Nasafi takes on a central Mu'tazilite argument: that what is good or evil in the human world must apply to God. He counters with concrete examples from God's actual deeds—like allowing sin and creating its ability—which would be evil in human terms but are not from God. This reveals a fundamental distinction between the witnessed (al-shahid) and unseen (al-gha'ib) realms, shattering the Mu'tazilite analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the examples Nasafi gives and the conclusion.
2
Identify the examples
Identify the two specific divine actions Nasafi mentions: God allowing adultery without preventing it, and God giving the disbeliever ability to insult Him. Note how each would be considered evil in human society.
3
Understand the distinction
Nasafi concludes that these actions are 'good from Him' despite being 'evil in the witnessed world.' This is the key point: the norms of al-shahid do not apply to al-gha'ib.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy. If God's actions can be categorically different, then reasoning from human experience to divine actions is invalid.
This ties back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنوقضت المعتزلة بما بينا أن الباري يرى عبده يزني بأمته ويخلى بينهما مع القدرة على المنع، ولا يكتفى بذلك بل يخلق قدرة ذلك الفعل والشدة في الآلة، ويعطي الكافر قدرة مع علمه أنه يشتمه بها ويعتدي عليه ومثل هذا في الشاهد قبيح، وقد حسن ذلك منه ووقعت المفارقة بين الشاهد والغائب، فبطل بذلك استشهادهم وتسويتهم بين الشاهد والغائب.
So the Mu'tazila were refuted by what we explained: that the Creator sees His servant committing adultery with his slave-woman and leaves them alone despite having the power to prevent, and He is not content with that, but rather He creates the ability for that act and the intensity in the organ, and He gives the disbeliever ability while knowing that he will insult Him with it and transgress against Him. Something like this in the witnessed world is evil, yet from Him it is good, and the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen occurred. Thus their citation and their equating between the witnessed and the unseen was invalidated.
Source pages p. 926

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument from analogy with the witnessed world. The author (Nasafi) presents examples from God's actions that would be considered evil if done by humans: God sees His servant committing adultery with his female slave and does not prevent it, despite being able to; moreover, God creates the very ability for the sinful act and gives the disbeliever power to insult Him. In human terms, such actions are evil (qabih), but coming from God they are good (hasan). This demonstrates a fundamental distinction (mufaraqa) between the witnessed world (al-shahid) and the unseen world (al-gha'ib), thereby invalidating the Mu'tazila's use of analogy. The key point is that divine actions are not bound by human norms of good and evil.

Correction: Nasafi demonstrates that God does actions that would be evil in human society but are good from Him, proving that human moral categories do not apply to God.

Refutation of Analogy Between Witnessed and Unseen
Nasafi refutes the analogy between shahid and gha'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between the witnessed world and the unseen divine realm is invalid, and how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila and Thunawiyya arguments.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by human standards of benefit.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi confronts a key argument of the Mu'tazila and the Thunawiyya: that what is good or bad in our world must also apply to Allah. Using their own logic, he shows that this leads to absurdities. Let's see how he dismantles the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow.
Notice the two distinct refutations: the direct example and the dialectical argument.
2
Identify the two refutations
First, Nasafi uses the example of God giving ability to a disbeliever who abuses it—ugly in the witnessed, yet God does it. Second, he turns the Thunawiyya's logic against them: if they insist on benefit as criterion, they must either deny God's wisdom or attribute need to Him.
These two moves together invalidate the analogy.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
The core principle is divine transcendence: God is not subject to the categories of good and bad that apply to human actions. His wisdom is not measured by human benefit.
This is a key Maturidi principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على المنع، ولا يكتفى بذلك بل يخلق قدرة ذلك الفعل والشدة في الآلة، ويعطي الكافر قدرة مع علمه أنه يشتمه بها ويعتدي عليه ومثل هذا في الشاهد قبيح، وقد حسن ذلك منه ووقعت المفارقة بين الشاهد والغائب، فبطل بذلك استشهادهم وتسويتهم بين الشاهد والغائب.
وقيل لهم : ما تنكرون على من يقول لا يحسن الفعل في الشاهد إلا لنفع نفسه كما زعمت الثنوية، ومن نفع غيره إنما حسن فعله لأن من نفع غيره نفع نفسه في الحقيقة إنما بنيل الثواب، وإما باكتساب الجاه، وإما بإحراز المحمدة والثناء الجميل حتى إن من نفع غيره بلا حمد يستحق ولا أجر ينال لا يكون ذلك حكمة، فإن تمسكتم بالشاهد فقولوا: إن الله تعالى ليس بحكيم لما أنه لا ينفع بفعله، وإن قلتم: إنه حكيم فقولوا: إنه منتفع محل للحاجات دافع للضرورات، فبأي أمر تمسكوا خرجوا عن الدين، وإن أبوهما أبطلوا استشهادهم بالشاهد، وإذا بطل ذلك
... on prohibition, and it is not sufficient with that, but He creates the ability for that action and the intensity in the tool, and He gives the disbeliever ability while knowing that he will abuse Him with it and transgress against Him, and the like of this in the witnessed is ugly, and that has been deemed good from Him, and the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen has occurred, so their argument and their equating between the witnessed and the unseen is invalidated. And it was said to them: What do you deny of one who says that an action is not good in the witnessed except for one's own benefit, as the Thunawiyya claimed, and one's benefiting others is only good because one who benefits others benefits himself in reality, either by obtaining reward, or by acquiring prestige, or by attaining praise and commendation, such that one who benefits others without deserving praise or obtaining any reward, that is not wisdom. If you hold to the witnessed, then say that God Almighty is not wise because He does not benefit by His action; and if you say He is wise, then say that He is one who benefits, a subject of needs, a repeller of harms. By whatever matter they hold, they exit from the religion, and if they deny both, they invalidate their argument from the witnessed. And when that is invalidated...
Source pages p. 927

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila and Thunawiyya who argue that God's actions must conform to the same standards of good and bad that apply in the witnessed world (shahid). The author first points out that God gives the disbeliever the ability to abuse Him, which would be ugly in the witnessed, yet God does it, showing a distinction. Then he turns the Thunawiyya's own logic against them: if they insist on the witnessed standard, they must either deny God's wisdom or attribute need to Him—both impossible and leading them out of the religion. Thus the analogy between shahid and ghā'ib is invalid.

Correction: The author shows that benefit is not a condition for divine actions; God's wisdom is not measured by human standards.

Checkpoint

B) He forces them to either deny God's wisdom or attribute need to Him.
C) He argues that all actions are neutral.
D) He says the Thunawiyya misunderstood the definition of benefit.

Key Takeaway
The analogy between the witnessed and the unseen is invalid because applying human standards of good and benefit to God leads to contradictions.
